Types of Taḍādd and Malaka wa ‘Adam and Their Naming
Defines the two types of Taḍādd and Malaka wa ‘Adam and explains the shift in terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of contrariety (taḍādd) and the two types of possession-privation (malaka wa ‘adam), as well as how the terms 'real' (ḥaqīqī) and 'well-known' (mashhūrī) are applied differently to each category.

Core Idea
Contrariety and possession-privation each have a common and a real sense, but the naming flips between logic and philosophy.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a subtle but important distinction in the classification of opposites. The terms 'real' (ḥaqīqī) and 'well-known' (mashhūrī) are used differently for contrariety (taḍādd) and for possession-privation (malaka wa ‘adam). Understanding this shift is essential for interpreting philosophical texts correctly, as the same term can refer to different types of opposition in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the distinctions being made. Pay attention to the conditions mentioned for each type.
2
Identify the two types of taḍādd
Notice that when extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is considered, it is called ḥaqīqī (real) taḍādd; without it, it is called mashhūrī (well-known) taḍādd. Understand which is more general.
The absolute (without condition) is more general.
3
Identify the two types of malaka wa ‘adam
For possession-privation, when the subject's preparedness (isti‘dād al-maḥall) is considered, it is called mashhūrī; without that condition, it is called ḥaqīqī. Again, note which is more general.
Here the absolute is called ḥaqīqī, opposite to taḍādd.
4
Examine the examples given
The passage gives examples like white vs. yellow (mashhūrī taḍādd) and sight vs. its absence from a tree (absolute malaka wa ‘adam). It also lists examples where one opposite is a privation of the other (e.g., rest for motion). Consider how these fit the definitions.
5
Understand the terminology reversal
Note that in logic, the definitions are as initially stated, but in philosophy (ḥikma), an additional condition is added for each. Consequently, in taḍādd, the absolute is called mashhūrī and the restricted ḥaqīqī; in malaka wa ‘adam, the absolute is called ḥaqīqī and the restricted mashhūrī. This reversal is a key point.
Memorize the reversed naming to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَد يُعتَبَرُ في التضاد غاية الخلاف ويسمى بالتضاد الحقيقي، والأول بالمشهوري وفي الملكة والعدم استعداد المحل للوجود في ذلك الوقت، ويخص باسم المشهورى والأول بالحقيقي، وأول كل أعم، وباعتباره يدخل في أقسام التقابل تقابل مثل البياض مع الصفرة، والبصر وعدمه من الشجر إلا أنهم صرحوا بأن أحد الضدين في المشهوري قد يكون عدماً للآخر، كالسكون للحركة والظلمة للنور والمرض للصحة والعجمة للنطق والأنوثة للذكورة والفردية للزوجية، وإن غاية الخلاف شرط في المشهوري أيضاً. ما مر من تفسير التضاد وتفسير الملكة والعدم هو الذي أورده قدماء الفلاسفة في أوائل المنطق، وأما في مباحث الفلسفة فقد اعتبروا في كل منهما قيداً آخر وهو في المتضادين، أن يكون بينهما غاية الخلاف كالسواد والبياض، بخلاف البياض والصفرة وفي الملكة والعدم، أن يكون العدم سلب الوجود عما هو من شأنه في ذلك الوقت كعدم اللحية عن الكوسج بخلافه عن الأمرد، وكل من التضاد والملكة والعدم، بالمعنى الأول أعم منه بالمعنى الثاني ضرورة أن المطلق أعم من المقيد إلا أن المطلق من التضاد يسمى بالمشهوري لكونه المشهور فيما بين عوام الفلاسفة والمقيد بالحقيقي لكونه المعتبر في علومهم الحقيقية، والملكة والعدم بالعكس حيث يسمون المطلق بالحقيقي، والمقيد بالمشهوري
And sometimes extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is considered in taḍādd (contrariety), and it is called real (ḥaqīqī) taḍādd, while the first (without that condition) is called well-known (mashhūrī) taḍādd. And in al-malaka wa al-‘adam (possession and privation), the preparedness of the subject for existence at that time is considered, and that is specified by the name well-known (mashhūrī), while the first (without that condition) is called real (ḥaqīqī). And the first of each is more general. And by this consideration, the opposition of white with yellow enters into the divisions of opposition, and the opposition of sight and its absence from a tree—except that they stated that one of the two opposites in mashhūrī [taḍādd] may be a privation of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, dumbness for speech, femaleness for maleness, and oddness for evenness. And extreme difference is also a condition in mashhūrī. The interpretation of taḍādd and the interpretation of al-malaka wa al-‘adam that has passed is what the ancient philosophers presented in the beginnings of logic. But in the investigations of philosophy, they considered an additional condition in each: in the two contraries, that there be between them extreme difference, like black and white, unlike white and yellow; and in al-malaka wa al-‘adam, that the privation be the negation of existence from what is naturally disposed for it at that time, like the lack of a beard from a beardless youth, unlike the lack of a beard from a beardless boy. And both taḍādd and al-malaka wa al-‘adam in the first sense are more general than in the second sense necessarily, since the absolute is more general than the restricted. However, the absolute of taḍādd is called mashhūrī because it is well-known among the common philosophers, and the restricted is called ḥaqīqī because it is considered in their real sciences. And al-malaka wa al-‘adam is the opposite: they call the absolute ḥaqīqī and the restricted mashhūrī.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the two types of taḍādd (contrariety) and the two types of malaka wa ‘adam (possession and privation), and clarifies the shift in terminology between the domains of logic and philosophy. It first states that when extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is considered in taḍādd, it is called real (ḥaqīqī) taḍādd, and without it, it is called well-known (mashhūrī) taḍādd. For malaka wa ‘adam, the condition is the preparedness of the subject (isti‘dād al-maḥall) for existence at that time, and that specified sense is called mashhūrī, while the absolute sense is called ḥaqīqī. The absolute (al-muṭlaq) is more general than the restricted (al-muqayyad). Then it mentions a consequence: by this consideration, the opposition of white with yellow enters into the divisions of opposition (since it is a type of mashhūrī taḍādd). Similarly, the opposition of sight and its absence from a tree (i.e., a tree's lack of sight) is an example of malaka wa ‘adam in the absolute sense. However, it is noted that the philosophers stated that in mashhūrī taḍādd, one opposite may be a privation of the other, giving examples like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, etc. And extreme difference is also a condition in mashhūrī? This seems contradictory to the earlier statement, but the text continues: The interpretation of taḍādd and malaka wa ‘adam that was given earlier is what the ancient philosophers presented in logic. But in philosophy (ḥikma), they added a further condition: for taḍādd, there must be extreme difference (like black vs. white, not white vs. yellow); for malaka wa ‘adam, the privation must be the absence of existence from something that is naturally disposed for it at that time (like lack of beard from a youth who should have one, not from a boy). Thus, the absolute sense in taḍādd is called mashhūrī (common), and the restricted sense is called ḥaqīqī (real). For malaka wa ‘adam, the terminology is reversed: the absolute is called ḥaqīqī, and the restricted mashhūrī.

Key Terms
تضاد
حققي
مشهوري
ملكة وعدم
غاية الخلاف
سلب الوجود
استعداد المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms ḥaqīqī and mashhūrī have fixed meanings: ḥaqīqī always indicates a stricter condition and mashhūrī a looser one.
Correction: In taḍādd, ḥaqīqī is the specific (with extreme difference) and mashhūrī is general; in malaka wa ‘adam, it is reversed: ḥaqīqī is the general and mashhūrī is the specific. The application depends on the category.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for real (ḥaqīqī) taḍādd?
A) The preparedness of the subject
B) Extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf)
C) The absence of extreme difference
D) That one opposite is a privation of the other

Key Takeaway
The classification of opposites into real and common types differs for contrariety and possession-privation, with the naming reversed in philosophical contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character flaws, do you see them as mere absences of virtue (like the lack of sight in a tree) or as privations of qualities you were created to possess (like the lack of a beard in a youth)? How does your answer determine whether you feel responsible to acquire those virtues or merely resigned to your nature?
?
The passage shows that scholars reversed the naming of 'real' and 'well-known' depending on the type of opposition. Are you more concerned with what people commonly label as 'real' faith or with the deeper reality that may be hidden behind conventional terms? What would change if you prioritized the latter?
?
If your relationship with God were classified as either absolute (malaka wa ‘adam without restriction) or restricted (with conditions), which would describe you? Do you consider yourself always in a state of preparedness for His mercy, or do you limit it based on your current state? How does that perspective affect your hope and effort?

Objection to the Fourfold Classification and the Later Philosophers' Response
Presents a logical objection, the later philosophers' answer, and the beginning of a critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection to the fourfold classification of opposites, the later philosophers' defense, and the beginning of al-Sanūsī's critique concerning mashhūrī taḍādd.

Core Idea
Classifications of opposites are not self-evident; they require careful definition and defense.

Logical classifications can seem neat, but exceptions often arise. This lesson explores an objection to the standard fourfold classification of opposition (taḍādd, malaka wa ‘adam, ījāb wa salab, and taḥawwul?). The objector points to pairs like white-yellow that lack extreme difference, and cases like sight-absence in scorpions. The later philosophers defend the classification by appealing to a broader notion of opposition. However, the author—Imām al-Sanūsī—begins to critique their answer, revealing subtleties in how opposites are defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: objection (qādih), answer, and criticism (naẓar).
2
Understand the objection
Identify why pairs like white-yellow and sight-absence in scorpions supposedly fall outside the fourfold classification.
The objector argues they are neither real opposites (taddād) nor possession-privation in the specific sense.
3
Follow the later philosophers' answer
How do the later philosophers (al-muta’akhkhirūn) respond? They say the classification is based on 'the more important meaning' (al-ma‘nā al-ahamm), i.e., mashhūrī taḍādd and haqīqī malaka wa ‘adam.
4
Examine the author's first criticism
The author says 'fīhi naẓar' and gives the first reason: in mashhūrī taḍādd, one opposite can be a privation of the other, which causes overlap with possession-privation and affirmation-negation. Why is this a problem?
This undermines the neat separation of categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان تقابل مثل البياض مع الصفرة والسواد مع الحمرة ونحو ذلك مما ليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، وكذا (الالتحاء والمرودة وتقابل البصر وعدمه) عن العقرب أو الشجر قادحاً في حصر التقابل في الأقسام الأربعة لكونه خارجاً عن التضاد وعن الملكة والعدم بالمعنى الأخص . أجاب المتأخرون بأن الحصر إنما هو باعتبار المعنى الأهم أعني المشهوري التضاد، والحقيقي من الملكة والعدم ليدخل أمثال ذلك . وفيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأن الضدين في التضاد والمشهوري لا يلزم أن يكونا وجوديين، بل قد يكون أحدهما عدماً للآخر كالسكون للحركة والظلمة للنور والمرض للصحة والعجمة للنطق، والأنوثة للذكورة، والفردية للزوجية . صرح بذلك ابن سينا وغيره فهو لا يكون قسيماً لتقابل الملكة والعدم، وتقابل الإيجاب والسلب، بل وفي كلامهم، أنه اسم يقع على التضاد الحقيقي، وعلى بعض أقسام الملكة والعدم، أعني ما يمكن فيه انتقال الموضوع من العدم إلى الملكة كالسكون والحركة بخلاف العمى والبصر، والحق أنه
And when the opposition of white with yellow, black with red, and the like where there is no extreme difference between them, and also (having a beard and manliness, and the opposition of sight and its absence) regarding a scorpion or tree, is an objection to the enumeration of opposition into four divisions, because it falls outside taḍādd and outside al-malaka wa al-‘adam in the specific sense. The later philosophers answered that the enumeration is only by considering the more important meaning, namely, mashhūrī taḍādd and ḥaqīqī of al-malaka wa al-‘adam, to include such instances. But there is consideration in that. First, because the two opposites in mashhūrī taḍādd do not necessarily have to be existents; rather, one of them may be a privation of the other, like rest for motion, darkness for light, illness for health, dumbness for speech, femaleness for maleness, and oddness for evenness. Ibn Sīnā and others stated that. So it is not exclusive to the opposition of possession-privation and the opposition of affirmation-negation. Rather, in their speech, that name applies to real taḍādd and to some divisions of possession-privation, namely, what the subject can transition from privation to possession, like rest and motion, unlike blindness and sight. The truth is that...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (qādih) against the standard fourfold classification of opposition (taḍādd, malaka wa ‘adam, ījāb wa salab, and taghyūr?). The objector points to pairs like white-yellow, black-red, etc., which lack extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf), and also examples like having a beard and manliness (al-iltiḥā’ wa al-murū’a), and the opposition of sight and its absence in scorpions or trees. These fall outside both taḍādd (since no extreme difference) and malaka wa ‘adam in the specific sense (since the subject is not naturally disposed for sight). The later philosophers (al-muta’akhkhirūn) answered that the enumeration is based on the more important meaning (al-ma‘nā al-ahamm), i.e., mashhūrī taḍādd (which does not require extreme difference) and ḥaqīqī malaka wa ‘adam (which requires natural disposition). This would include white-yellow as a kind of mashhūrī taḍādd, and scorpions lacking sight as an example of malaka wa ‘adam in the absolute sense? The author then criticizes this answer with "wa fīhi naẓar" (there is consideration). First, because in mashhūrī taḍādd, the two opposites do not have to be both existents; one can be a privation of the other, as Ibn Sīnā stated, with examples like rest/motion, darkness/light, etc. Therefore, mashhūrī taḍādd overlaps with the opposition of possession-privation and also affirmation-negation. Moreover, in the philosophers' own usage, the term 'taḍādd' sometimes refers to real taḍādd and sometimes to parts of malaka wa ‘adam, specifically cases where the subject can transition from privation to possession (like rest/motion), unlike permanent privations (like blindness/sight). The passage ends mid-sentence, but clearly the criticism undermines the neat fourfold classification.

الأقسام الأربعة
المتأخرون
نظر

وجوديان
عدمي

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author criticizes the later philosophers' answer to the objection?
A) Because extreme difference is not required in mashhūrī taḍādd
B) Because in mashhūrī taḍādd, one opposite can be a privation of the other, causing overlap with other categories
C) Because the classification should include more than four types
D) Because Ibn Sīnā did not accept mashhūrī taḍādd

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of opposites is not self-contained; the later philosophers' defense using mashhūrī taḍādd blurs the boundaries between categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that even logical classifications like opposition can become blurred and require adjustment. In your own life, where have you clung to black-and-white categories (e.g., good/bad, believer/disbeliever) only to discover that reality is more complex? Will you now embrace that complexity with humility, or continue to force people into boxes?
?
Consider the example of rest as the privation of motion. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you view a 'lack' (e.g., lack of prayer, lack of gratitude) as a neutral absence rather than a positive opposite? How does that shift in understanding from mere privation to genuine opposition change your sense of responsibility before Allah?

Reduction of Even-Odd Opposition
Explains that even-odd opposition reduces to affirmation-negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the opposition of even and odd numbers reduces to the logical opposition of affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
Even and odd opposition is just affirmation and negation in disguise.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid investigates the nature of the opposition between even and odd numbers. At first glance, this might seem like a primitive contrast, but upon closer analysis, it reduces to the logical opposition of affirmation and negation. Let's see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage about even and odd. Notice how the author defines even and odd.
2
Understand the reduction
The author explains that even is a number divisible into equal parts (affirmation) and odd is not divisible (negation). So the opposition is between having a property and not having it.
3
Recognize the source
Note that this reasoning is attributed to Ibn Sina. This shows the continuity of Islamic logic with Aristotle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعم من ذلك إذ لا يمكن الانتقال في النطق، والعجمة، وفي الذكورة والأنوثة، وفي الزوجية والفردية، على أن تقابل الزوجية والفردية عند التحقيق راجع إلى الإيجاب والسلب، فإن الزوج عدد ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد عدد لا ينقسم بمتساويين، فالأول اسم للموضوع، أعني العدد مع الإيجاب، والثاني اسم له مع السلب، كذا ذكره ابن سينا. وأما ثانياً: فلأنه صرح ابن سينا وغيره بأن غاية الخلاف شرط في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل البياض والحمرة خارجاً عن الأقسام.
More general than that, because transition is not possible in utterance, and non-Arabic pronunciation, and in masculinity and femininity, and in evenness and oddness, such that the opposition of evenness and oddness upon investigation reduces to affirmation and negation. For even is a number divided into two equal parts, and odd is a number not divided into two equal parts. The first is a name for the subject, i.e., the number with affirmation, and the second is a name for it with negation. This is as mentioned by Ibn Sina. And secondly: because Ibn Sina and others have stated that extreme difference is a condition in the well-known contrariety as well, and in that case the opposition like whiteness and redness is excluded from the divisions.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two points. First, it discusses the opposition of even and odd (zawjiyya wa fardiyya). It states that upon investigation, this opposition reduces to the opposition of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb). Even is defined as a number divisible into two equal parts (affirmation of divisibility), and odd as not divisible (negation). So the pair even-odd is not a primitive type of opposition but falls under affirmation and negation. This is attributed to Ibn Sina. Second, it mentions that Ibn Sina and others have made extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf) a condition in the well-known contrariety (tadhad al-mashhuri). Consequently, oppositions like whiteness and redness, which do not have extreme difference, are excluded from the divisions of true contrariety. Only pairs that have extreme difference (like black and white) are considered true contraries.

Key Terms
زوجية
فردية
إيجاب
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think even and odd are a primitive type of opposition like black and white.
Correction: The passage clarifies that even-odd reduces to affirmation-negation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does even mean?
A) A number not divisible into two equal parts
B) A number divisible into two equal parts
C) A number that is prime
D) A number that is odd

Key Takeaway
The opposition of even and odd is not primitive but reduces to the logical opposition of affirmation and negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moral dichotomy you often use—like 'generous' vs. 'stingy.' Does this lesson's reduction of even-odd to affirmation-negation expose a similar pattern? How might you be wrongly treating such pairs as two distinct natures rather than as a single trait affirmed or negated? What would change if you saw them that way?
?
When you label someone as 'right' or 'wrong' in a religious disagreement, are you truly identifying two opposite categories, or are you simply affirming or negating a single criterion? How can this logical insight guard you against self-righteousness?
?
This passage shows that seemingly primitive oppositions can be reduced to simpler logical forms. Where in your own thinking about faith—such as 'true belief' vs. 'hypocrisy'—might you be mistakenly assuming a primitive opposition? What would it cost you to re-examine that assumption?

Extreme Difference as Condition in Contrariety
States that extreme difference is a condition in contrariety, excluding pairs like white and red.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that extreme difference is a necessary condition for the well-known contrariety, and that oppositions lacking this are excluded.

Core Idea
True contrariety requires extreme difference; otherwise it is not genuine.

In this lesson, we examine the second point made in the passage: Ibn Sina and others stipulate that extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf) is a condition for contrariety. This means that only pairs like black and white count as true contraries, while pairs like white and red do not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'وأما ثانياً'. Note the claim about extreme difference.
2
Understand the condition
The author states that extreme difference is a condition in contrariety. This means that the two opposites must be at the farthest extremes of a spectrum.
3
See the implication
Consequently, oppositions like whiteness and redness are excluded from being true contraries because they are not at extreme ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعم من ذلك إذ لا يمكن الانتقال في النطق، والعجمة، وفي الذكورة والأنوثة، وفي الزوجية والفردية، على أن تقابل الزوجية والفردية عند التحقيق راجع إلى الإيجاب والسلب، فإن الزوج عدد ينقسم بمتساويين، والفرد عدد لا ينقسم بمتساويين، فالأول اسم للموضوع، أعني العدد مع الإيجاب، والثاني اسم له مع السلب، كذا ذكره ابن سينا. وأما ثانياً: فلأنه صرح ابن سينا وغيره بأن غاية الخلاف شرط في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً، وحينئذ يكون تقابل مثل البياض والحمرة خارجاً عن الأقسام.
More general than that, because transition is not possible in utterance, and non-Arabic pronunciation, and in masculinity and femininity, and in evenness and oddness, such that the opposition of evenness and oddness upon investigation reduces to affirmation and negation. For even is a number divided into two equal parts, and odd is a number not divided into two equal parts. The first is a name for the subject, i.e., the number with affirmation, and the second is a name for it with negation. This is as mentioned by Ibn Sina. And secondly: because Ibn Sina and others have stated that extreme difference is a condition in the well-known contrariety as well, and in that case the opposition like whiteness and redness is excluded from the divisions.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two points. First, it discusses the opposition of even and odd (zawjiyya wa fardiyya). It states that upon investigation, this opposition reduces to the opposition of affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb). Even is defined as a number divisible into two equal parts (affirmation of divisibility), and odd as not divisible (negation). So the pair even-odd is not a primitive type of opposition but falls under affirmation and negation. This is attributed to Ibn Sina. Second, it mentions that Ibn Sina and others have made extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf) a condition in the well-known contrariety (tadhad al-mashhuri). Consequently, oppositions like whiteness and redness, which do not have extreme difference, are excluded from the divisions of true contrariety. Only pairs that have extreme difference (like black and white) are considered true contraries.

Key Terms
تضاد
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think any two different colors are contraries.
Correction: Only those with extreme difference (e.g., black vs white) are true contraries.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for contrariety according to Ibn Sina as mentioned in the passage?
A) Extreme similarity
B) Extreme difference
C) Partial overlap
D) Complete absence

Key Takeaway
Extreme difference is a necessary condition for true contrariety; pairs lacking it are not considered contraries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a disagreement you've had with someone where you assumed your positions were completely opposite. Now, applying the condition of extreme difference from this lesson, consider: Was it truly a case of contrariety, or were you mistaking a lesser difference for a fundamental opposition? What would change if you acknowledged that?
?
In your spiritual life, you may face choices that seem contradictory—for example, between two good deeds or two paths. How can the criterion of 'extreme difference' help you decide which oppositions are real and which are merely superficial? Be specific about a current choice you are weighing.

Opposition is Predicated by Tashkik
Explains gradational predication of opposition and primacy of affirmation-negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why opposition is applied to its subcategories by gradation (tashkik) and why affirmation-negation is the most intense type.

Core Idea
Affirmation-negation is the strongest opposition because its impossibility of combination is essential.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the term 'opposition' does not apply equally to its four types but by gradation. Among them, affirmation and negation stand out as the most fundamental, a point illustrated with the example of 'good'. We will break down the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of opposition
Read the passage and note the four subdivisions: contrariety, correlation, affirmation-negation, and absence. Understand that they are not all on the same level.
The footnotes clarify these four.
2
Understand predication by tashkik
Grasp that 'opposition' is said of its types in a graded manner, not univocally or equivocally. Some types manifest opposition more intensely.
Tashkik means gradation or analogy.
3
Why affirmation-negation is strongest
The impossibility of combination in affirmation-negation is essential and apparent, whereas in other types it is derived or secondary.
4
Analyze the example of 'good'
See the two knots: being good (essential) and not being evil (accidental). Negating the essential (not good) is stronger than negating the accidental (evil).
This illustrates why affirmation-negation is primary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أحكام التقابل مقوليته على ما تحته (قال: وإن مقوليته على الأقسام (۱) بالتشكيك (٣) ، وأشهرها الإيجاب والسلب، إذ باعتباره يمتنع الاجتماع (۳) في البواقي لا التضاد باعتبـار غـايـة الخلاف ، إذ لا غاية فوق التنافي (الذاتي) . يريد أن من حكم التقابل أنه ليس جنساً لأقسامه، إذ لا يتوقف تعقلها على تعقله وهذا ظاهر في التضايف، كما أن التوقف ظاهر في التضاد، وأما (في التضاد وأما) (ه) في الباقين فمتردد . ، وبالجملة فمقوليته على الكل بالتشكيك لكونه في الإيجاب والسلب أشد لأن امتناع الاجتماع فيهما ظاهر وبحسب الذات وفي البواقي لاشتمالها على ذلك، وأوضح ذلك بأن الخير فيه عقدان عقد أنه خير وهو ذاتي، وعقد أنه ليس بشر وهو عرضي، وكونه ليس بخير ينفي ،الذاتي، وكونه شراً ينفي العرضي، ولا خفاء في أن النافي الذاتي أقوى وفي التجريد ما يشعر بأنه في التضاد أشد لأنه قال : وأشدها فيه . الثالث : أي أشد أنواع التقابل في التقابل هو التضاد ووجه (٦) بأن التضاد مشروط بغاية الخلاف وهي غاية في امتناع الاجتماع ورد بأنه لا يتصور غاية خلاف فوق التنافي الذاتي بأن يكون أحدهما صريح سلب الآخر بخلاف الضدين، فإن أحدهما إنما يستلزم سلب الآخر . (۱) الأربعة التي تحته وهي التضاد والتضايف والإيجاب والسلب والعدم . (۲) بمعنى أنه إنما يقال على هذه الأقسام ويصدق عليها بطريق التشكيل لا بطريق التواطؤ والاشتراك . (۳) كان الإيجاب والسلب أقوى هذه الثلاثة إنما هو باعتبار اشتمالها على الإيجاب والسلب. (٤) الذي بين الضدين . (٥) ما بين القوسين من (أ) و (ج) ساقط. (٦) في (ب) وجه بدلاً من (ووجه) .
Among the rulings of opposition is that it is predicated on what is under it. He said: And its predication on the divisions (1) is by tashkik (2), and the most famous of them is affirmation and negation, because by it the impossibility of combination (3) is [apparent] in the rest, not contrariety by virtue of extreme difference, for there is no extreme above the essential non-existence. He means that among the rulings of opposition is that it is not a genus for its divisions, because understanding them does not depend on understanding it, and this is apparent in correlation, just as dependence is apparent in contrariety. As for (in contrariety and as for) (?) in the rest, it is uncertain. In summary, its predication on all is by tashkik, because it is more intense in affirmation and negation due to the impossibility of combination being apparent and essential, while in the rest it is because of their inclusion of that. And he clarified that with the example of good: in it are two knots—a knot that it is good (essential) and a knot that it is not evil (accidental). And its not being good negates the essential, and its being evil negates the accidental. There is no doubt that the essential negation is stronger. And in al-Tajrid there is an indication that it is more intense in contrariety because he said: 'and the most intense of them in it.' The third: that the most intense type of opposition in opposition is contrariety, and it is argued (6) that contrariety is conditioned by extreme difference, which is the ultimate in the impossibility of combination. It is refuted by the fact that no extreme difference is conceivable above the essential non-existence, such that one is the outright negation of the other, unlike the two contraries, for one of them only entails the negation of the other. (1) The four that are under it: contrariety, correlation, affirmation and negation, and absence. (2) Meaning that it is said of these divisions and applies to them by way of gradation, not by way of univocity or equivocity. (3) That affirmation and negation are the strongest of these three is only because of their inclusion of affirmation and negation. (4) That which is between the two contraries. (5) What is between the parentheses in (A) and (C) is missing. (6) In (B) 'wajh' instead of 'wa wajh'.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of how the term 'opposition' (taqabul) applies to its subcategories. It begins by quoting the matn: 'And its predication on the divisions is by tashkik (gradation/analogy), and the most famous of them is affirmation and negation, because in it the impossibility of combination is by essence, not contrariety with its extreme difference, for there is no extreme above essential non-existence.' The commentator explains that opposition is not a genus for its divisions because understanding the divisions does not require prior understanding of opposition; this is clear for correlation (tadayuf) and contrariety (tadhad) but uncertain for the others. Then it summarises that opposition is predicated on all types by tashkik, with affirmation-negation being the most intense because the impossibility of combination is essential. An example is given: in the concept of 'good' there are two knots: being good (essential) and not being evil (accidental). Negating the essential (saying it is not good) is stronger than negating the accidental (saying it is evil). Some, citing al-Tajrid, claim that contrariety is strongest. The objection is that contrariety is conditioned by extreme difference, which is the ultimate in impossibility of combination. The refutation is that no extreme difference exceeds essential non-existence (al-tanafi al-dhati), which is outright negation of the other. In contrariety, one contrary only entails the negation of the other, but is not outright negation itself. So affirmation-negation remains strongest. The footnotes clarify the divisions and textual variants.

Key Terms
تقابل
تشكيك
إيجاب
سلب
تضاد
تضايف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposition applies equally (univocally) to all its types.
Correction: Opposition is predicated by tashkik, meaning it applies with varying degrees; affirmation-negation is the most intense.

Checkpoint
What does the example of 'good' illustrate?
A) That contrariety is the strongest opposition
B) That essential negation is stronger than accidental negation
C) That opposition is a genus
D) That correlation is not a true opposition

Key Takeaway
Opposition is predicated by tashkik, with affirmation-negation being the strongest type due to its essential impossibility of combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own beliefs, do you ever confuse essential affirmations (e.g., 'I am a believer') with accidental ones (e.g., 'I am not a sinner')? How does this distinction challenge your self-assessment? What would it mean to affirm your faith with essential negation of disbelief, not just accidental avoidance of sin?
?
The passage teaches that essential negation is stronger than accidental negation. Is there a truth about yourself you have only negated accidentally (e.g., 'I am not arrogant') but not essentially? What would it look like to confront that as an essential negation?
?
If opposition by affirmation-negation is the strongest form, what does that imply for your understanding of tawheed? Are you affirming Allah's oneness essentially, or only in contrast to shirk (accidentally)? How can you make your affirmation more essential?

Refuting the Claim that Contrariety is Stronger
Presents and refutes the objection that contrariety is the strongest type of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that contrariety is the strongest opposition and its refutation using the concept of essential non-existence.

Core Idea
No extreme difference surpasses essential non-existence, making affirmation-negation strongest.

Some Islamic logicians argued that contrariety (tadhad) is the most intense type of opposition because it involves extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf). The commentator refutes this by showing that the essential non-existence in affirmation-negation is a stronger form of impossibility. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that contrariety is strongest because it is conditioned by extreme difference, which is the ultimate in impossibility of combination.
This view is attributed to al-Tajrid.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that no extreme difference can exceed essential non-existence (al-tanafi al-dhati), where one thing is the outright negation of the other.
3
Compare the two types
In affirmation-negation, one term is the direct negation of the other. In contrariety, one term only entails the negation of the other, not directly.
This shows affirmation-negation is stronger.

The Graded Predication of Opposition and the Superiority of Affirmation-Negation
Taftazani discusses the graded predication of taqabul and the superiority of ijab wa salb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqabul (opposition) is predicated over its divisions by gradation (tashkik) and why the division of affirmation and negation (ijab wa salb) is considered the strongest type.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation is the strongest opposition because its impossibility of conjunction is intrinsic.

In logic, the term 'taqabul' (opposition) applies to four main types: contrariety, correlation, affirmation-negation, and privation-possession. Taftazani here clarifies that the term is not used univocally but by gradation (tashkik), meaning it applies more strongly to some types than others. This passage explains that the strongest type is affirmation and negation, and gives the reason why.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without focusing on analysis. Then read the literal English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms for opposition types: تضاد, تضايف, إيجاب وسلب, عدم وملكة.
2
Identify the key claim about tashkik
The passage begins by stating that the predication of taqabul over its divisions is by tashkik. Ask yourself: what does gradation mean here? Why can't it be a genus?
The commentary says taqabul is not a genus because understanding the divisions does not require understanding taqabul first, especially for correlation.
3
Understand why ijab wa salb is strongest
The passage explains that the impossibility of conjunction (two things cannot both be true or both false) is intrinsic in affirmation and negation, while in contrariety it is only based on extreme difference. This makes affirmation and negation the strongest.
4
Analyze the example of 'the good'
Taftazani illustrates with al-khayr (the good): it has an essential concept (being good) and an accidental concept (not being evil). Negating the essential is stronger than negating the accidental, showing why intrinsic negation is stronger.
This example clarifies the abstract point.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Summarize the flow: taqabul is predicated by gradation -> affirmation-negation is the strongest because intrinsic impossibility of conjunction -> example with al-khayr confirms this.
You should now be able to explain why some types of opposition are 'stronger' than others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أحكام التقابل مقوليته على ما تحته
قال: وإن مقوليته على الأقسام بالتشكيك، وأشهرها الإيجاب والسلب، إذ باعتباره يمتنع الاجتماع في البواقي لا التضاد باعتبار غاية الخلاف، إذ لا غاية فوق التنافي الذاتي.
يريد أن من حكم التقابل أنه ليس جنساً لأقسامه، إذ لا يتوقف تعقلها على تعقله وهذا ظاهر في التضايف، كما أن التوقف ظاهر في التضاد، وأما في التضاد وأما في الباقين فمتردد.
وبالجملة فمقوليته على الكل بالتشكيك لكونه في الإيجاب والسلب أشد لأن امتناع الاجتماع فيهما ظاهر وبحسب الذات وفي البواقي لاشتمالها على ذلك، وأوضح ذلك بأن الخير فيه عقدان عقد أنه خير وهو ذاتي، وعقد أنه ليس بشر وهو عرضي، وكونه ليس بخير ينفي الذاتي، وكونه شراً ينفي العرضي، ولا خفاء في أن النافي الذاتي أقوى
From the rulings of taqabul (opposition/contrariety) is its predication over what is under it. He said: And indeed its predication over the divisions is by tashkik (gradation), and the most famous of them are ijab (affirmation) and salb (negation), for by reason of it impossibility of conjunction [exists] in the remaining [types], not in tadad (contrariety) by reason of extreme difference, for there is no extreme beyond intrinsic self-negation. He means: that from the ruling of taqabul is that it is not a genus for its divisions, for their conceptualization does not depend on its conceptualization, and this is apparent in mutual relation (tadāyuf); just as dependence is apparent in contrariety. As for the remaining, it is ambiguous. In summary, its predication over all is by gradation, and it is stronger in affirmation and negation because the impossibility of conjunction in them is apparent and by essence, while in the remaining it is because they include that. The clearest explanation of that is that the good (al-khayr) has two conceptualizations: the conceptualization that it is good, which is essential, and the conceptualization that it is not evil, which is accidental. And that it is not good negates the essential, and that it is evil negates the accidental. There is no doubt that the negator of the essential is stronger.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the ruling of taqabul (opposition). It states that taqabul is predicated on its four divisions (tadād, tadāyuf, ijāb wa salb, and 'adam wa malaka) by way of tashkik (gradation), meaning the term applies to them with different degrees of strength, not uniformly. The strongest division is ijāb wa salb (affirmation and negation) because in them the impossibility of conjunction (i.e., they cannot both be true or both false) is intrinsic and by essence. In contrast, in tadād (contrariety), this impossibility is not intrinsic but based on extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf). The commentary explains that taqabul is not a genus for its divisions because understanding the divisions does not require prior understanding of taqabul; this is clear for tadāyuf (correlatives) where the relation is understood together. For tadād, dependence is apparent, but for the remaining types it is ambiguous. The author then clarifies with an example: 'the good' (al-khayr) involves two concepts: the essential concept of being good, and the accidental concept of not being evil. Negating the essential is stronger than negating the accidental. Thus, the intrinsic negation in affirmation-negation makes them the strongest type of opposition.

Key Terms
تقابل
تشكيك
إيجاب
سلب
تضاد
تضايف
تمتنع الاجتماع
غاية الخلاف
تنافي ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think tadād (contrariety) is the strongest type because it involves extreme difference.
Correction: The passage shows that ijāb wa salb (affirmation and negation) is stronger due to intrinsic impossibility of conjunction, whereas in tadād the impossibility is only based on extreme difference, which is not as fundamental.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani consider ijāb wa salb (affirmation and negation) to be the strongest type of opposition?
A) Because they involve extreme difference.
B) Because the impossibility of conjunction in them is intrinsic and by essence.
C) Because they are most commonly used in logic.
D) Because they are based on mutual relation.

Key Takeaway
The predication of taqabul over its divisions is by gradation, with affirmation and negation being the strongest due to the intrinsic impossibility of conjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your faith, do you find yourself more focused on affirming core truths about Allah (like His oneness and mercy) or on avoiding what contradicts those truths? Which carries more weight in your daily decisions, and why?
?
The passage shows that negating the essential (e.g., 'not good') is stronger than negating the accidental (e.g., 'evil'). In your spiritual life, are you more concerned with negating major sins or neglect of obligatory acts? What does that reveal about your understanding of what truly matters to Allah?
?
The intrinsic impossibility of conjunction in affirmation-negation means that truth and falsehood cannot coexist. Have you ever allowed a contradiction to persist in your beliefs or actions—holding something true while living as if it were false? What would it cost you to fully align your life with the truth you affirm?

Objection: Tadād as the Strongest, and the Rebuttal
Taftazani presents an objection from al-Tajrīd that tadād is the strongest type of opposition and answers it with the concept of intrinsic negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intrinsic negation (ijāb wa salb) is considered stronger than contrariety (tadād) in the classification of opposition.

Core Idea
Extreme difference in tadād is not as strong as explicit negation.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses a potential objection from al-Tajrīd that claims tadād (contrariety) is the strongest type of opposition. He presents the argument and its rebuttal, demonstrating that intrinsic self-negation (i.e., the relationship between affirmation and negation) is even more fundamental. This lesson will help you grasp the logical reasoning behind this ranking.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: objection, rebuttal, alternative interpretation.
The passage mentions two different views; distinguish them.
2
Identify the objection
Find the claim: that tadād is the strongest because it is conditioned by 'extreme difference' (ghāyat al-khilāf).
3
Understand the rebuttal
The rebuttal argues that no extreme difference surpasses intrinsic negation, where one term is the explicit negation of the other. In tadād, one contrary only entails negation of the other, not directly negates it.
This is the key distinction.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
A second opinion suggests that al-Tajrīd's statement might be about tashtik (gradation) within tadād, not overall strength.
This clarifies a possible misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on the significance
Understand why ijāb wa salb is considered the strongest type in traditional logic, and how this affects the classification of opposites.
It's about direct vs. indirect negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجريد ما يشعر بأنه في التضاد أشد لأنه قال: وأشدها فيه. الثالث: أي أشد أنواع التقابل في التقابل هو التضاد ووجه بأن التضاد مشروط بغاية الخلاف وهي غاية في امتناع الاجتماع ورد بأنه لا يتصور غاية خلاف فوق التنافي الذاتي بأن يكون أحدهما صريح سلب الآخر بخلاف الضدين، فإن أحدهما إنما يستلزم سلب الآخر. وقيل: يعني كلامه أن شد الأنواع في التشكيك هو التضاد، لأن قبول القوة والضعف في أصنافه من الحركة والسكون، والحرارة والبرودة، والسواد والبياض وغير ذلك في غاية الظهور بخلاف البواقي.
And in al-Tajrīd there is what suggests that [opposition] is stronger in tadād, because he said: 'And the strongest of them in it is the third.' That is: the strongest types of opposition within opposition is tadād, and it was argued that tadād is conditioned by extreme difference, which is an extreme in impossibility of conjunction. And it was replied that an extreme difference beyond intrinsic self-negation is inconceivable, namely that one is an explicit negation of the other, unlike two contraries, for one only entails the negation of the other. And it was said: his speech means that the strongest of the types in tashkik is tadād, because the acceptance of strength and weakness in its kinds—like motion and rest, heat and cold, blackness and whiteness, and others—is extremely apparent, unlike the remaining [types].
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the work al-Tajrīd that claims tadād (contrariety) is the strongest type of opposition. The argument is that tadād requires ghāyat al-khilāf (extreme difference), which is an extreme form of impossibility of conjunction. The answer (al-radd) refutes this by stating that no extreme difference surpasses intrinsic self-negation (al-tanāfī al-dhātī), where one term is the explicit negation of the other (e.g., affirmation and negation). In tadād, one contrary does not directly negate the other; it only entails its negation. Therefore, ijāb wa salb are stronger. A second opinion (qīla) is then mentioned: perhaps al-Tajrīd's statement means that within the gradation of taqabul, tadād shows the clearest gradation (strength and weakness) in its instances (e.g., motion/rest, hot/cold), which is more apparent than in other types. This does not challenge the primacy of ijāb wa salb but notes a different aspect.

Misconception: One might think that because tadād involves extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf), it is the strongest type of opposition.
Correction: The rebuttal shows that ijāb wa salb (affirmation and negation) is stronger because one term is the explicit negation of the other, whereas in tadād, one only entails negation.

Key Takeaway
The strongest type of opposition is not tadād but ijāb wa salb (intrinsic self-negation), because one term directly negates the other.

The Reference of Affirmation and Negation: Speech and Mental Concepts
Taftazani explains the reference of affirmation and negation to linguistic and mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negations are mental considerations (i'tibarat) with verbal expressions, not external realities, and how this relates to the reference of affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
Negations are mental, not external; affirmations can refer to external things.

This lesson explores a key logical and theological distinction: do affirmation and negation refer to things in the external world or to mental concepts? Taftazani explains, following Ibn Sina, that negations are purely mental considerations expressed in speech, not real essences. Understanding this prevents the absurdity of attributing infinite negations to a single entity. Let's dive into the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the key terms and the logical flow from the main claim to the hypothetical and clarification.
The passage includes both the matn and the commentary.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on terms like 'reference' (marja'), 'negations' (sulub), 'mental considerations' (i'tibarat), 'essences' (dhawat), and the three types of existence: verbal, mental, external.
These terms are essential for understanding the argument.
3
Analyze the hypothetical and the clarification
Consider why negations cannot be external: they would lead to infinite attributes for any subject. Then note the clarification that affirmations are different—they can refer to external qualities like blackness.
This shows the asymmetry between affirmation and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرجع الإيجاب والسلب
قال: ومن حكم الإيجاب والسلب أن مرجعهما إلى القول والعقد إذ السلوب اعتبارات لها عبارات لا ذوات وإلا لكان للإنسان بحسب سلب ما عداه معان لا تتناهى.
أي الوجود اللفظي والذهني دون العيني بمعنى أن السلوب اعتبارات عقلية لها عبارات لفظية لا ذوات حقيقية، وإلا لكان للإنسان مثلاً معان غير متناهية لأنه ليس بفرس ولا ثور، ولا ثعلب، ولا أشياء غير متناهية كذا ذكره ابن سينا و به يظهر أن ليس معناه ما فهمه بعضهم من أنه ليس في الخارج شيء هو إيجاب أو سلب كيف، ولا يعنون بالإيجاب إلا مثل السواد بالنسبة إلى اللاسواد وهو موجود في الخارج.
The reference of affirmation and negation. He said: And from the rulings of affirmation and negation is that their reference is to speech (al-qawl) and mental concept (al-‘aqd), for negations are mental considerations (i‘tibārāt) for which there are verbal expressions (ʿibārāt), not essences (dhawāt). Otherwise, for a human being, according to the negation of everything other than him, there would be infinite meanings. That is, [they have] verbal and mental existence, not external existence, meaning that negations are rational considerations that have verbal expressions, not real essences. Otherwise, for a human being, for example, there would be infinite meanings, because he is not a horse, nor an ox, nor a fox, nor infinite things. Thus Ibn Sīnā mentioned it. And by this it becomes clear that its meaning is not what some understood, that there is nothing external that is affirmation or negation at all, nor do they mean by affirmation except like blackness in relation to non-blackness, which exists externally.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the 'reference' (marja‘) of affirmation and negation. The matn states that their reference is to speech (al-qawl) and mental concept (al-‘aqd), not to external existence. Negations (al-sulūb) are i‘tibārāt (mental considerations) that have verbal expressions, not real essences (dhawāt). The reason is that if they were real external entities, then a human would have infinite meanings corresponding to the infinite things he is not (e.g., not a horse, not an ox, etc.). The commentary elaborates that this is about 'verbal and mental existence' (al-wujūd al-lafẓī wa al-dhihnī), not 'external existence' (al-‘aynī). It cites Ibn Sīnā and clarifies a misunderstanding: some thought this means that there is no external entity that is affirmation or negation at all, but the intended meaning is not that; rather, affirmation can refer to external qualities, like blackness in relation to non-blackness, which exists externally. So negations are mental, but affirmations can have external referents.

The Ruling of Affirmation and Negation in Judgment
Taftazani states that affirmation and negation, when used in judgments, exclusively yield truth and falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirmation and negation in judgments form a true contradictory pair: one is always true and the other false, and they cannot both be false or both true.

Core Idea
In logical judgments, affirmation and negation are strict contradictories: one always true, the other always false.

We now turn to a key rule in kalam logic: what happens when abstract terms like 'affirmation' and 'negation' are actually used in a judgment? Taftazani lays down the fundamental principle that such pairs divide truth and falsity exhaustively. This lesson unpacks that rule and its basis in the law of non-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the ruling is stated, then the reason.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that affirmation and negation 'divide truth and falsity'. Understand that this means in any judgment involving a contradictory pair, one is true and the other false.
The phrase 'wa al-bawāqī' refers to other types of opposition (like contrariety) that do not necessarily divide truth and falsity.
3
Understand the impossibility
The passage explicitly says they cannot both be false nor both true. Ask yourself why that is—the reason is given next.
4
Connect to the principle of non-contradiction
The reason is 'imtinā' ijtima' al-naqīḍayn' (impossibility of conjunction of contradictories). This is the classical law of non-contradiction, which holds whether the judgment is affirmative or negative.
This principle underlies all rational discourse in kalam.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this matters for theology: if a proposition about Allah is true, its negation must be false, and vice versa. This logical rule gives certainty to doctrinal statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أحكام السلب والإيجاب
قال: وإنهما عند النقل إلى الحكم يقتسمان الصدق والكذب والبواقي
فيكون أحدهما صادقاً والآخر كاذباً ولا يجتمعان على كذب معاً ولا على صدق معاً لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين ثبوتاً أو نفياً.
From the rulings of negation and affirmation. He said: And indeed they, when transferred to judgment (al-ḥukm), divide truth and falsity among themselves, and the remaining [types do not]. So one of them is true and the other false, and they do not both concur on falsehood together, nor on truth together, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories (naqīḍayn) in affirmation or negation.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling of affirmation and negation when they are transferred from individual concepts to actual judgments (al-ḥukm). The matn says that they 'divide truth and falsity' (yaqtasimān al-ṣidq wa al-kidhb), meaning that in a logical contradictory pair, one must be true and the other false. They cannot both be false (which would happen in contrariety) nor both true. The commentary explains the reason: impossibility of conjunction of contradictories (imtinā' ijtima' al-naqīḍayn), whether in positive or negative form. This is the principle of non-contradiction. The phrase 'and the remaining' (wa al-bawāqī) refers to other types of opposition (like contrariety) which do not necessarily divide truth and falsity in this way (e.g., two contraries can both be false).

إيجاب
نقل إلى الحكم
صدق

نقيضان
امتناع اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that affirmation and negation can both be false (as in some non-Aristotelian logics), but classical kalam asserts their contradictory nature.
Correction: According to this passage, they cannot both be false. The principle of non-contradiction prevents that.

Checkpoint

A) They can both be true.
B) They can both be false.
C) One is true and the other is false, and they cannot be both false or both true.
D) They are independent of truth and falsity.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation in judgments are true contradictories: one true, one false, never both false or both true, due to the impossibility of conjunction of contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This logical principle demands that for any judgment, your affirmation or negation must be true and the other false. In your life, are there beliefs about Allah—like affirming His mercy while denying His justice—that you have not reconciled? How will you confront that contradiction today?

The lesson teaches that in a judgment, truth and falsity are strictly divided. When it comes to the existence of Allah, your stance is either true or false. Have you examined the evidence with the same rigor you would apply to any life-changing decision, or are you merely following comfort? What step will you take this week to ensure your belief is grounded in truth?
?
Non-contradiction means you cannot hold two opposite beliefs simultaneously. Is there any area of your faith where your actions contradict your words—for example, claiming tawheed while your heart seeks security in wealth or status? What specific change will you make today to align your inner conviction with your outward profession?

Affirmation and Negation: Words and Beliefs, Not External Realities
Sanusi explains that affirmation and negation belong to speech and belief, not external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirmation and negation are considered matters of speech and mental consideration, not external essences, and how this prevents an infinite regress of meanings.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation live in language and mind, not in the world outside.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial point of logic and theology: where do affirmation and negation actually exist? The author argues that they belong to the realm of words (al-qawl) and beliefs (al-'aqd)—mental constructs that we express through language—rather than being real entities in the external world. Understanding this distinction prevents a logical absurdity: that every negation would produce an infinite number of meanings for any subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Begin by carefully reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the author's core claim that affirmation and negation refer to speech and belief.
Pay attention to the phrase 'since negations are considerations that have expressions, not essences'.
2
Understand the argument against infinite meanings
The author argues that if negations were real essences, then for any entity (e.g., a human) negating everything else would result in infinite meanings. This is impossible, so negations cannot be real essences.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove the initial claim.
3
Reflect on the correction of the misunderstanding
The author clarifies that while negations are mental, affirmations can refer to external realities like blackness. This prevents the misinterpretation that nothing external corresponds to affirmation or negation.
This step roots the discussion back to reality, ensuring the doctrine is not purely nominalist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال): ومن حكم الإيجاب والسلب أن مرجعهما إلى القول والعقد إذ السلوب اعتبارات لها عبارات لا ذوات وإلا لكان للإنسان بحسب سلب ما عداه معان لا تتناهى . أي الوجود اللفظي والذهني دون العيني بمعنى أن السلوب اعتبارات عقلية لها عبارات لفظية لا ذوات حقيقية، وإلا لكان للإنسان مثلاً معان غير متناهية لأنه ليس بفرس ولا ثور، ولا ثعلب، ولا أشياء غير متناهية كذا ذكره ابن سینا و به يظهر أن ليس معناه ما فهمه بعضهم من أنه ليس في الخارج شيء هو إيجاب أو سلب كيف، ولا يعنون بالإيجاب إلا مثل السواد بالنسبة إلى اللاسواد وهو موجود في الخارج.
He said: And from the judgment of affirmation and negation is that their reference is to speech and belief, since negations are considerations that have expressions, not essences; otherwise man would have, according to the negation of what is other than him, infinite meanings. That is, verbal and mental existence, not external, meaning that negations are mental considerations that have verbal expressions, not real essences; otherwise man would have, for example, infinite meanings because he is not a horse, nor an ox, nor a fox, nor infinite things. Commentary
This passage defines the reference of affirmation and negation. The author begins by stating that the reference of affirmation and negation is to speech (al-qawl) and belief (al-'aqd), meaning they belong to the realm of words and mental concepts, not to external existence. He explains that negations (al-sulub) are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) that have verbal expressions (al-alfaz), not real essences (dhawat). If they were real essences, then for any entity like man, by negating everything other than him (e.g., not being horse, not being ox, etc.), there would be infinite meanings, which is impossible. He cites Ibn Sina to support this. The author then clarifies a common misunderstanding: some thought that this means there is no external reality corresponding to affirmation or negation, but the correct understanding is that affirmation refers to something like blackness in relation to non-blackness, which does exist externally.

The Rule of Contradictories in Propositions
Sanusi explains the rule of contradictories and contrasts them with other opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirmation and negation in propositions always yield one true and one false, regardless of subject existence, and how this differs from other types of opposites.

Core Idea
Contradictories are unique: they cannot both be true or both false, even if the subject doesn't exist.

In this passage, the author sets down a foundational rule of logic: when affirmation and negation become propositional judgments, they are like two sides of a coin—one must be true, the other false. This holds even if the subject is non-existent, unlike other opposites such as black and white, which can both be false if the subject lacks them. Let's read the first part where this rule is stated and contrasted.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule statement
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning until 'أو على العقل'. This part states the main rule and gives examples.
Notice the key terms: النقيضان (contradictories) and المتقابلان (opposites).
2
Identify the three types of opposites
The author mentions other opposites: correlatives (تضايف), possession and privation (ملكة وعدم), and quality opposites like black/white. Observe their examples.
These opposites can both be false if the subject does not exist or lacks both.
3
Understand the logical reason
Reflect on why affirmation and negation cannot both be false: because they are contradictories, which by definition cannot be simultaneously false (or true).
The phrase 'ضرورة امتناع اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما' is key.
4
Contrast with other opposites
Compare the rule for contradictories with the other opposites. See how the examples with a non-existent subject (phoenix or intellect) show both can be false.
This contrast clarifies the special status of affirmation and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإنهما عند النقل إلى الحكم يقتسمان الصدق والكذب والبواقي قد يكذب لعدم الموضوع أو الخلوه . أي ومن حكم الإيجاب والسلب أنهما إذا نقلا إلى الحكم والقضية، كان أحدهما صادقاً، والأخر كاذباً البتة سواء وجد الموضوع أو لم يوجد ضرورة امتناع اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما بخلاف سائر الأقسام، فأنه يجوز أن يكذب فيه المتقابلان لعدم الموضوع أو لخلوه عنهما ، كما إذا حمل الأعمى والبصير، أو الأسود والأبيض، أو الأب والابن على العنقاء أو على العقل . فإن قيل : إن أريد بالنقل إلى القضية حمل المتقابلين على موضوع، فالإيجاب والسلب أيضاً قد يكذبان لعدم الموضوع . كما في قولنا : العنقاء أسود، ولا أسود لاقتضاء المعدولة وجود الموضوع ، وإن أريد اعتبار التقابل بين النقيضين فهذا لا يتصور في التضايف، ولا في ملكة والعدم . قلنا : المراد الثاني في الإيجاب والسلب، والأول في البواقي، وقد يقال القضية إنما تكون معدولة مفتقرة إلى وجود الموضوع إذا أريد بالمحمول مفهوم ثبوتي يصدق عليه النقيض، عدل في التعبير عنه إلى طريق السلب وأما إذا أريد به نفس مفهوم النقيض فهو موجبة سالبة المحمول مستغنية عن وجود الموضوع لكونها في قوة السالبة فقولنا العنقاء لا أسود إذا أريد باللا أسود نقيض الأسود، أعني رفعه فهي صادقة بمنزلة قولنا ليس العنقاء أسود.
He said: And they [affirmation and negation] when transferred to judgment are divided into truth and falsehood, while the rest may be false due to absence of subject or vacuity. That is, from the judgment of affirmation and negation is that when they are transferred to judgment and proposition, one of them is necessarily true and the other false, whether the subject exists or not, due to the impossibility of the conjunction and elevation of the two contradictories, unlike the other categories; for it is permissible that the two opposites be false due to the absence of the subject or its vacuity from both, as when 'blind' and 'seeing', or 'black' and 'white', or 'father' and 'son' are predicated of the phoenix or of the intellect. For if it is said: If by 'transferred to proposition' is meant predicating the two opposites of a subject, then affirmation and negation may also both be false due to absence of subject, as in our saying: 'The phoenix is black' and 'the phoenix is not black' because the modified (al-ma'dula) requires the existence of the subject; and if by 'considering the opposition between the two contradictories' is meant, then this is not conceivable in correlation (al-tadāyuf) nor in possession and privation (al-malaka wa al-'adam). We say: The second is intended in affirmation and negation, and the first in the rest. And it may be said that the proposition is only modified (ma'dula) and dependent on the existence of the subject if what is intended by the predicate is a positive concept to which the contradictory applies, and it is modified in expression by way of negation; but if what is intended is the very concept of the contradictory, then it is an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate (mujiba saliba al-mahmul) that is independent of the existence of the subject because it is in the force of a negative proposition. So our saying 'the phoenix is not black' if by 'not black' is meant the contradictory of black, i.e., its removal, then it is true just as our saying 'the phoenix is not black' (laysa al-'anqa' aswad).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical behavior of affirmation and negation when they become parts of propositions (al-qadiyya). The author states that when affirmation and negation are transferred to judgment (i.e., used in a proposition), they are such that one is true and the other false necessarily, regardless of whether the subject exists or not, because it is impossible for both contradictories to be true or both false. This is in contrast to other types of opposites (e.g., contraries like black and white, correlatives like father and son, possession and privation like sight and blindness) where both can be false if the subject does not exist or lacks both properties. The author then presents an objection: if affirmation and negation in propositions are just predicating two opposites of a subject, then they too could both be false when the subject is non-existent (e.g., 'the phoenix is black' and 'the phoenix is not black' could both be false if the subject does not exist, because a modified proposition requires the existence of the subject). The answer is that in affirmation and negation, what is intended is the opposition between the two contradictories themselves, not the predication of two opposites on a subject. The author clarifies that a modified proposition (ma'dula) like 'the phoenix is not black' if 'not black' is taken as a positive concept (like non-blackness) requires subject existence; but if 'not black' is taken as the contradictory of black, i.e., the negation itself, then it is equivalent to a negative proposition (saliba) and does not require subject existence. Thus the original rule stands.

Key Terms
إيجاب
سلب
الصدق
الكذب
موضوع
نقيضان
متقابلان
تضايف
ملكة وعدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirmation and negation can both be false if the subject doesn't exist, like other opposites.
Correction: The author shows that contradictories cannot both be false due to the law of non-contradiction and excluded middle, even with a non-existent subject.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do affirmation and negation always yield one true and one false even if the subject does not exist?
A) Because they are correlatives
B) Because they are contradictories that cannot both be true or both be false
C) Because they require subject existence
D) Because they are examples of possession and privation

Key Takeaway
The unique property of contradictory opposites is that they cannot both be false or both true, regardless of subject existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm something about Allah—like 'Allah is just'—do you ever feel the weight that its contradictory ('Allah is not just') must be false? How does this logical certainty affect your trust in His attributes, even when circumstances seem to contradict them?
?
In your own arguments or beliefs, where might you be treating a mere contrary (like 'kind' vs 'harsh') as if it were a contradictory, creating a false dilemma? How can you apply Sanusi's rule to bring more precision to your convictions?
?
Consider a non-existent subject like 'a perfect society'—when you hear claims about it, are you aware that contradictory pairs still hold logical force? How does this challenge you to evaluate ideals not as real entities but as propositions subject to truth?

Objection and Clarification on Modified Propositions
Sanusi addresses an objection about modified propositions and clarifies the interpretation of negative predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection regarding modified propositions is resolved and why a negative predicate can be equivalent to a simple negative under specific interpretation.

Core Idea
A modified proposition's truth depends on whether the negated term is taken as a positive concept or as the contradictory itself.

The author anticipated an objection: if affirmation and negation are just predication of opposites on a subject, then they too could both be false when the subject is non-existent, just like other opposites. This objection revolves around propositions like 'the phoenix is not black' which seem to require existence. The answer lies in distinguishing two ways of understanding the negative predicate. Let's explore this nuanced clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'فإن قيل' onward. The objection claims that if we consider the predication of opposites on a subject, affirmation and negation could both be false when the subject doesn't exist, because a modified proposition (المعدولة) requires subject existence.
Example: both 'The phoenix is black' and 'The phoenix is not black' might be false if 'not black' is taken as a positive quality.
2
Understand the author's response
The author replies that in affirmation and negation, the intended opposition is between the contradictories themselves, not between two predicates on a subject. Then he distinguishes two interpretations of a negative predicate.
The key distinction: 'لا أسود' can mean either the positive concept of non-blackness or the contradictory of black (i.e., the negation).
3
Grasp the concept of 'mujiba saliba al-mahmul'
If 'not black' means the contradictory (رفع السواد), then the proposition is equivalent to a simple negative ('not black' = 'it is not the case that it is black') and does not require subject existence.
This is called 'موجبة سالبة المحمول' (affirmative with negative predicate) and is in the force of a negative proposition.
4
Apply the clarification
Consider the example: 'The phoenix is not black'. If we mean the contradictory, it is true because there is no blackness, just like saying 'The phoenix is not black' as a simple negative.
This shows the original rule stands: one of 'black' and 'not black' (as contradictory) must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
This lesson shows that the same words can carry different logical structures, leading to opposite truth values. Where in your own thinking or speech do you tend to confuse 'the negation of a term' with 'the affirmation of its opposite'? How might this confusion lead you to make false judgments about people or ideas?
?
The author distinguishes between what is intended in the mind and the mere expression of words. In your daily conversations, how often do you reflect on what you truly intend by your words rather than just the words themselves? Commit to one specific change in how you speak or listen to avoid such logical traps.

The Judgment of Opposition: Subject Not Free from One Contrary
Sanusi states the rule of opposition regarding the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical rule of contraries: that any subject capable of receiving opposite qualities must be characterized by one of them.

Core Idea
A subject of contraries cannot be neutral – it must have one of them.

In this passage, Sanusi explains a fundamental rule of opposition (al-tadd). The rule is straightforward: if a thing can have two opposite qualities, it cannot be without either—it must have one. This principle is essential for arguments about attributes and change. Let's explore the exact wording and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'subject' (المضوع), 'contraries' (الضدان), and 'opposition' (التضاد). How does the author define them?
The commentary clarifies that 'subject' means the essence capable of receiving contraries.
3
Understand the rule
The rule states that a subject capable of receiving contraries must be characterized by one of them. It cannot be free from both.
This is a logical necessity, not a probabilistic statement.
4
Consider the logical significance
Why is this rule important? It establishes that certain attributes are necessary for subjects that can receive opposites, which is foundational for arguments about change and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال) ومن حكم التضاد أن الموضوع قد لا يخلو عن أحد الضدين . أي الذات التي من شأنها أن يعرض لها الضدان فتكون موضوعاً لهما مقوماً لما يحل فيه منهما . المتقابلين عليه لاتصافه بأحدهما بعينه دون الآخر.
He said: And from the judgment of opposition (al-tadd) is that the subject may not be free from one of the two contraries. i.e., the essence that is such that the two contraries can occur to it, so it becomes a subject for them, constituting what inheres in them. The two opposites are upon it due to its being qualified by one of them specifically to the exclusion of the other.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage states a logical rule regarding contraries (al-tadd). The author says that a subject (al-mawdu‘) — here meaning a substance capable of receiving opposite qualities — cannot be devoid of both contraries; it must possess one of them. The commentary clarifies that 'subject' refers to the essence (al-dhat) that can be affected by the contraries, serving as a substrate (mawdu‘) that constitutes what inheres in them. The two opposites (al-mutaqabilan) characterize the subject by one of them specifically, not both. This is a standard rule in Aristotelian logic: contraries cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time, but the subject must have one of them if it is capable of receiving them.

Key Terms
تضاد
موضوع
ضد
ذات
متقابلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a subject capable of both contraries can be neutral, having neither.
Correction: The rule states the subject must have one of the two contraries; it cannot be free from both if it is capable of receiving them.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the judgment of opposition regarding the subject?
A) The subject can be free from both contraries
B) The subject must have both contraries simultaneously
C) The subject may not be free from one of the two contraries
D) The subject is always characterized by the privation

Key Takeaway
The logical rule of contraries: a subject capable of receiving opposite qualities must be characterized by exactly one of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart is the subject capable of both iman and kufr. Right now, which one actually characterizes you? Are you pretending to be neutral when this rule says you must be one?
?
In what area of your life are you avoiding clarity—trying to be neither seeker of truth nor content with falsehood? The logical rule demands you are qualified by one. Which one is it?
?
You have the capacity to know the truth and to ignore it. If you are not actively pursuing knowledge, then ignorance is what characterizes you. Can you accept that? What will you do about it today?
