The Definition and Restriction of Real Opposition
The author defines real opposition and states its restriction to two final types under a genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of real opposition (al-ṭadd al-ḥaqīqī) and why it is restricted to two final types under a single genus.

Core Idea
Real opposition is only between two final types of a single genus.

In this passage, the author clarifies the precise meaning of real opposition. He explains that it is not simply a matter of succession on the same subject, but a specific relationship between two types within one genus. This distinction is crucial for understanding how opposites function in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Notice the initial remark about succession not being necessary.
2
Identify the definition and restriction
Find where the author states that real opposition is between two final types under a genus. Note the examples given.
3
Understand what it excludes
The passage lists what real opposition is not: between genera, between types from different genera, or among multiple types. Reflect on why these are excluded.
4
Consider the reliance on induction
The author mentions that this view relies on induction but hints at a problem (naẓar). Think about why induction might be insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر وفيه إشارة إلى أن تعاقب الضدين على الموضوع الواحد ليس بلازم.
التضاد الحقيقي يوجد بين نوعين.
قال: وإن الحقيقي منه إنما يكون بين نوعين آخرين من جنس واحد كالسواد والبياض، لا بين جنسين كالفضيلة والرذيلة، وكالخير والشر، أو نوعين من جنسين كالعفة والفجور، أو أنواع من جنس كالسواد والبياض والحمرة، وعولوا في ذلك على الاستقراء وفيه نظر.
Apparent, and in it is an indication that the succession of two opposites upon one subject is not necessary. Real opposition exists between two types. He said: Indeed, real opposition only occurs between two final types from a single genus, like blackness and whiteness, not between two genera like virtue and vice, nor good and evil, nor between two types from two genera like chastity and lewdness, nor between multiple types from a genus like blackness, whiteness, and redness. And they relied in that on induction, and there is a problem (naẓar) in it.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage defines real opposition (al-ṭadd al-ḥaqīqī) and its condition. It begins by clarifying that real opposition does not require opposites to succeed each other on the same subject; this is a preliminary remark. Then it states that real opposition exists between two types. The author quotes an authority who says real opposition is only between two final types (ākharayn) under a single genus, e.g., black and white under color. It is not between two genera like virtue and vice, nor between two types from different genera like chastity (a virtue) and lewdness (a vice), nor among multiple types under a genus like black, white, and red. The proponents of this view rely on induction (istiqrāʾ), but the author immediately notes that there is a problem (naẓar) in this induction—a hint of criticism that will be elaborated later.

Key Terms
التضاد الحقيقي
النوعان الآخران
الجنس
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Real opposition requires the two opposites to succeed each other on the same subject.
Correction: The passage explicitly indicates that such succession is not necessary for real opposition to exist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, real opposition (al-ṭadd al-ḥaqīqī) occurs between which of the following?
A) Two genera like virtue and vice
B) Two types from two different genera
C) Two final types under a single genus
D) Multiple types under a single genus

Key Takeaway
Real opposition is confined to two final types under a single genus, as opposed to broader categories or multiple types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you instinctively reduce a complex situation to 'good versus evil' or 'virtue versus vice,' are you committing the error the author warns against? In what one area of your life can you stop forcing a binary and start seeing a spectrum, starting today?
?
The author points out that the restriction to two final types is based on induction but has a problem. What belief do you hold about yourself or others that you have never critically examined? Will you examine it this week, knowing it might be built on shaky ground?
?
This lesson teaches that real opposition is rare and specific. How often do you trap yourself in a 'us vs. them' mentality, assuming two opposing sides where many possibilities exist? Name one relationship where you can commit to seeing beyond binary opposition, and take one concrete step today.

The Common Notion of Opposition (al-Ṭadd al-Mashhūrī)
The author enumerates the four categories of common opposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common notion of opposition (al-ṭadd al-mashhūrī) and the four types of opposition it includes.

Core Idea
Common opposition is broader than real opposition; it can be between genera, types, or multiple types.

This lesson explores the common or well-known view of opposition (al-ṭadd al-mashhūrī) as presented by scholars of kalām. Unlike the restricted real opposition, this view includes opposition between two different genera, between two types of the same genus, and even between multiple types. The passage provides clear examples to illustrate each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: it lists four categories with examples.
This passage is a short, clear enumeration.
2
Identify the four categories
Find each category mentioned: (1) between two genera, (2) between two types from a genus, (3) between multiple types from a genus, (4) between multiple types from two genera. Note the examples given for each.
3
Contrast with real opposition
Think about how this common view differs from the strict real opposition (al-ṭadd al-ḥaqīqī) which only allows two types under a single genus. Common opposition is more inclusive.
This contrast is implicit; the passage emphasizes the 'common' nature.
4
Reflect with your own examples
Try to come up with a new example for each category. For instance, for category (1), consider 'light and darkness'. Check if it fits the pattern.
This deepens understanding of the classification.

Source Text

وأما المشهورى فقد صرحوا بأنه قد يكون بين جنسين كالخير والشر، أو نوعين من جنس كالعفة والفجور، أو أنواع من جنس كالسواد والبياض والحمرة أو من جنسين كالشجاعة والتهور والجبن .
As for the common (mashhūrī) [opposition], they explicitly stated that it may be between two genera like good and evil, or between two types from a genus like chastity and lewdness, or between multiple types from a genus like blackness, whiteness, and redness, or between two genera like courage, recklessness, and cowardice.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the common or well-known view (al-ṭadd al-mashhūrī) of opposition, contrasted with the restricted real opposition. The practitioners of this view (al-mashhūrī) assert that opposition is not limited to two types under a genus. It can occur: (1) between two genera, e.g., good and evil or virtue and vice; (2) between two types from the same genus, e.g., chastity and lewdness (both under virtue and vice respectively); (3) between multiple types from a single genus, e.g., black, white, and red under color; or (4) between multiple types from two genera, e.g., courage (a virtue), recklessness and cowardice (vices). This view is more inclusive and does not rely on the strict induction criticized earlier.

Key Terms
التضاد المشهوري
جنسين
نوعين من جنس
أنواع من جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all opposition is of the restricted real type (only between two species under a genus).
Correction: The common notion of opposition is broader, including opposition between genera, between two or more types, and even across multiple genera.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of common opposition (al-ṭadd al-mashhūrī) mentioned in the passage?
A) Black and white
B) Virtue and vice
C) Hot and cold
D) Up and down

Key Takeaway
Common opposition includes four types: between genera, types of a genus, multiple types of one genus, and multiple types from two genera, making it more flexible than real opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the example of 'chastity vs. lewdness' from the passage. In your own character, where do you see opposition not as a simple binary but as a spectrum that includes intermediate states? How does that change how you assess your own growth?

First Objection: Circularity in Induction
First objection: circularity of inductive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection argues that the inductive proof for restricting real opposition is circular.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning assumes the very point it tries to prove.

The first objection targets the use of induction (istiqrā') to prove that real opposition only occurs between two types under a genus. The author argues that the induction is not valid because it secretly assumes the restriction it aims to establish. This is a classic logical fallacy of circular reasoning (dawr ẓāhir).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read carefully the Arabic and translation. Identify the claim and the reason for circularity.
2
Understand the logical move
The objection says that to deny opposition between virtue and vice, one must already accept that opposition is only between two types of the same genus. That is assuming the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the importance
Circularity is a fatal flaw in any proof. This objection shows the need for independent evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه نظر من وجوه:
الأول: أن معنى الاستقراء في انحصار التضاد بين نوعين من جنس هو أنا وجدناه فيما بينهما دون غيرهما، ولا طريق إلى نفيه عما بين الفضيلة والرذيلة أو العفة والفجور، سوى أنه لا يكون إلا فيما بين نوعين من جنس وهذان جنسان أو نوعان من جنسين وهذا دور ظاهر.
الثاني: أنه إن اشترط في التضاد غاية الخلاف فكونه فيما بين نوعين دون أنواع من جنس ضروري لا استقرائي لأن غاية الخلاف إنما يكون بين الطرفين لا بين الطرف وبعض الأوساط، وإن لم يشترط فبطلانه ظاهر، كما في أنواع اللون.
الثالث: أنهم أطبقوا على تضاد السواد والبياض على الإطلاق، مع أنهما ليسا آخرين.
There is a problem (naẓar) in several respects:
First: That the meaning of induction (istiqrāʾ) in restricting opposition to two types from a genus is that we found it existing between them to the exclusion of others, and there is no way to deny it concerning virtue and vice or chastity and lewdness, except that it occurs only between two types from a genus—and these are two genera or two types from two genera—and this is an obvious circle (dawr ẓāhir).
Second: That if the ultimate difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) is stipulated in opposition, then its being between two types to the exclusion of multiple types from a genus is necessary (ḍarūrī), not based on induction, because the ultimate difference only occurs between the two extremes (ṭarfayn), not between an extreme and some intermediates. And if it is not stipulated, then its falsity is obvious, as in the types of color.
Third: That they unanimously agree on the opposition of black and white absolutely, despite that they are not the two final [types]?
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents three objections (wujūh al-naẓar) against the claim that real opposition is restricted to two final types under a genus. The objections are directed at the inductive argument used to support that restriction.

Objection 1 (Circularity): The induction relied upon claims we observe opposition only between two types under a genus, like black and white. But to deny opposition between virtue and vice (two genera) or between chastity and lewdness (types from different genera), one must assume the very point at issue: that opposition only occurs between two types of the same genus. Since virtue and vice are genera, or types from different genera, the argument is circular (dawr ẓāhir).

Objection 2 (Ultimate Difference): If by 'real opposition' we mean 'ultimate difference' (ghāyat al-khilāf), then such difference naturally exists only between the two extremes (al-ṭarfayn), not between an extreme and an intermediate. Hence it is a logical necessity (ḍarūrī) that ultimate difference holds between two items, not between multiple items. This does not require induction. However, if 'ultimate difference' is not the criterion, then the restriction is patently false, because many items under a genus differ in degree, e.g., colors are all different but not all opposites.

Objection 3 (Common Usage): The proponents themselves acknowledge that black and white are absolute opposites, yet black and white are not the two final types under color (since there are intermediates like red). So their own example contradicts their restriction.

Key Terms
دور ظاهر
استقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection claim the induction is circular?
A) Because it uses an unsound analogy
B) Because it assumes the restriction it tries to prove
C) Because it contradicts consensus
D) Because it is based on incomplete data

Key Takeaway
The first objection reveals that the inductive argument for restricting opposition is vitiated by circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief by using an assumption that itself depends on that belief? What was at stake in that reasoning, and how did you break the cycle?
?
The objection reveals an 'obvious circle' in reasoning. Search your own heart: is there a core conviction about your faith or life that you have never examined because you assumed it as a starting point? What would it cost you to test that assumption?
?
The inductive proof failed because it presupposed its conclusion. In your daily judgments, do you ever gather evidence to support what you already believe, ignoring evidence that might break the circle? Commit to one area where you will actively seek contrary evidence this week.

Second Objection: Ultimate Difference and Necessity
Second objection: dilemma regarding ultimate difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection: that if ultimate difference is required, then restriction to two is necessary, not inductive; if not, the restriction is false.

Core Idea
If opposition means ultimate difference, it naturally holds between two extremes.

The second objection examines the criterion of 'ultimate difference' (ghāyat al-khilāf). The author presents a dilemma: either ultimate difference is stipulated or not. If it is, then the restriction to two extremes is a logical necessity, not a discovery of induction. If it is not stipulated, then the restriction is obviously false, as seen in colors. This objection challenges the definition used in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
Notice the two conditions: if ultimate difference is stipulated or not. Understand each branch.
2
See the conclusion
Under the first branch, the restriction is necessary (ḍarūrī), not inductive. Under the second, it's false.
3
Apply the example
The colors example shows that without ultimate difference, many types can differ without being opposites.

Third Objection: Counterexample of Black and White
Third objection: counterexample of black and white.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third objection points to black and white as a counterexample to the restriction.

Core Idea
Black and white are considered opposites despite not being the two final types.

The third objection is a direct counterexample: scholars unanimously agree that black and white are opposites, yet they are not the two final types under color (because there are intermediate colors like red). This contradicts the claim that real opposition only occurs between two final types under a genus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The restriction says opposition is between two final types. Black and white are not final.
2
Recognize the consensus
The objector says scholars agree black and white are opposites absolutely.
3
See the contradiction
If the restriction were true, black and white would not be opposites. But they are. So the restriction is false.

Refutation of Induction for Opposition Between Two Species
Refutes the claim that opposition is only between two species via induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why induction cannot prove that opposition is restricted to two species within a single genus, due to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Induction cannot prove absolute restrictions without circularity.

This lesson examines a key objection against the claim that true opposition only occurs between two species of a genus. The argument relies on induction, but the objector reveals a fundamental flaw: the induction assumes the very principle it seeks to prove, resulting in circular reasoning. We will see how this critique applies to examples like virtue and vice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal English translation. Let the text's own argument unfold without analysis first.
Pay attention to the examples given: virtue/vice and chastity/debauchery.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
Recognize that the claim under attack is that true opposition only exists between two species of one genus. The passage refutes the induction used to support this claim.
3
Understand the induction and its problem
The induction is based on observed cases, but it cannot rule out unobserved possibilities. The objection highlights that the denial of opposition between virtue and vice presupposes the very restriction being proved.
Notice the circularity: the argument uses the restriction to deny counterexamples, then claims the restriction is proven.
4
Recognize the circular reasoning
The text explicitly calls this 'obvious circularity' (dawr ẓāhir). The principle that opposition is only between two species of one genus is assumed in dismissing other cases.
5
Reflect on broader implications
Consider how this critique applies to other inductive arguments in theology and philosophy. Induction alone cannot establish universal necessary truths.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعولوا في إثبات ذلك على الاستقراء. وفيه نظر من وجوه أن معنى الاستقراء في انحصار التضاد بين نوعين من جنس هو أنا وجدناه فيما بينهما دون غيرهما، ولا طريق الى نفيه عما بين الفضيلة والرذيلة أو العفة والفجور، سوى أنه لا يكون إلا فيما بين نوعين من جنس وهذان جنسان أو نوعان من جنسين وهذا دور ظاهر.
And they relied for proving that upon induction. But there is a problem in it from several aspects: the meaning of induction in restricting opposition to two species within a genus is that we have found it to be between them and not between others, and there is no way to deny it between virtue and vice or chastity and debauchery except [the claim] that it occurs only between two species of a genus, but these are two genera or two species from two genera, and this is an obvious circularity.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the claim that true opposition (taḍād) only occurs between two species within a single genus. The objector argues that those who hold this view base it on induction (istiqrā'), i.e., empirical observation. However, the induction is flawed: they claim to have found opposition only between two species of one genus, so they deny it between virtue and vice (which are genera) or between chastity and debauchery (which are species from different genera). But their denial assumes the very principle they are trying to prove—that opposition can only be between two species of one genus. This is circular reasoning (dawr ẓāhir).

Key Terms
الاستقراء
التضاد
الجنس
النوع
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction can prove that opposition is universally limited to two species of one genus.
Correction: Induction only shows observed cases; it cannot rule out opposition between genera or cross-species pairs. The argument becomes circular because the denial of such cases presupposes the principle that opposition is only between two species of one genus.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw identified in the induction-based argument for opposition being only between two species of a genus?
A) The induction is incomplete because it doesn't cover all cases
B) The argument is circular, assuming what it needs to prove
C) The induction is based on false examples
D) The argument confuses genus and species

Key Takeaway
Inductive reasoning cannot prove a universal restriction when the restriction itself is used to dismiss counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Objection: Extreme Difference and Opposition
Second objection: whether extreme difference is necessary for opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second objection against the claim that opposition occurs only between two species of a genus.

Core Idea
Extreme difference limits opposition to two extremes by necessity, not induction.

This passage presents the second objection in a series of critiques. It examines whether the condition of extreme difference is required for two things to be opposites. The objector argues that if extreme difference is required, then opposition is necessarily between only two species; if not, the claim of opposition between only two is false. This logical dilemma challenges the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...' and 'if not... then...'.
2
Identify the two conditional scenarios
The objection presents two possibilities: either extreme difference is stipulated or it is not. Identify the consequence of each scenario.
3
Understand the logical necessity
If extreme difference is required, then the restriction to two species is necessary (ḍarūrī), not inductive. Why? Because extremes are only two by definition.
Extremes are the two ends of a range, so there can only be two.
4
Consider the color example
If extreme difference is not required, then the restriction is false, as shown by colors: there are many species (black, white, red, etc.) that are opposites without being extreme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه إن اشترط في التضاد غاية الخلاف فكونه فيما بين نوعين دون أنواع من جنس ضروري لا استقرائي لأن غاية الخلاف إنما يكون بين الطرفين لا بين الطرف وبعض الأوساط، وإن لم يشترط فبطلانه ظاهر، كما في أنواع اللون.
The second [objection]: that if extreme difference is stipulated in opposition, then its being between two species [of one genus] rather than among species of a genus is necessary, not inductive, because extreme difference only occurs between the two extremes, not between an extreme and some intermediates; and if it is not stipulated, then its falsity is apparent, as in the species of color.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection. The opponent's claim that opposition is only between two species of one genus either requires that there be extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) between the opposites or not. If extreme difference is required, then the restriction to two species is necessary by definition (extremes are only two), not something proven by induction. If extreme difference is not required, then the restriction is false, because there can be opposition among many species of a genus, like colors (black, white, red, etc.), which are not just two extremes.

Key Terms
غاية الخلاف
الطرفين
الأوساط
اللون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that opposition always requires a middle ground, and that there can be more than two extremes.
Correction: Extreme difference only occurs between two endpoints; by definition, there cannot be more than two extremes.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, if ghāyat al-khilāf (extreme difference) is stipulated for opposition, what follows?
A) Opposition can only be between two species of one genus by necessity
B) Opposition can exist among many species of a genus
C) Induction is still needed to determine the extremes
D) The stipulation is impossible to satisfy

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that requiring extreme difference for opposition leads to necessary two-species opposition, while not requiring it makes the restriction false, as with colors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your belief about Allah's attributes have you perhaps accepted a lukewarm position instead of the clear extreme of tawhid? This week, identify one ambiguous area and commit to seeking the necessary truth from revelation.
?
The passage shows that if extreme difference is required, the result is necessary, not based on evidence. Have you ever held a conviction that you thought was based on strong evidence, but upon reflection, it was actually self-evident from the nature of things? How can you increase your reliance on necessary truths in your certainty about the Hereafter?
?
Colors like black, white, red, and yellow all oppose each other without being extremes. This shows that not requiring extremes opens up many possibilities. In your own life, have you artificially limited your understanding of Allah's names and attributes by assuming they must fit into a binary? Are you ready to let go of that restriction and embrace the full spectrum of what He has revealed?

Third Objection: Black and White Are Not Single Species
Third objection: black and white contain multiple species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third objection argues that black and white cannot be simply opposed because each comprises multiple species.

Core Idea
Black and white are not ultimate species; opposition requires extreme difference.

The third objection challenges the claim that black and white are absolute opposites. The objector points out that black and white are not single species but each includes many shades. Thus true opposition would only occur between extremes, not between any black and any white. This passage shows how technical definitions can have surprising consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector first states the common view, then rejects it, then draws a consequence.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'النوع الأخير' and 'التشكيك'.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'نوع أخير' (ultimate species), 'تشكيك' (analogy), and 'غاية الخلاف' (extreme difference). Understand their precise meanings in this context.
These terms are central to the objection's logical force.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the reasoning: if black and white are not ultimate species but each has multiple species, then according to the definition of true opposition (requiring extreme difference), only the extremes of black and white would be truly opposed. This contradicts the unrestricted claim of opposition.
The objection attempts to refute the common view by showing its own definition leads to a restricted result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنهم أطبقوا على تضاد السواد والبياض على الإطلاق، مع أنهما ليسا نوعين أخيرين من اللون بل السوادات المتفاوتة أنواع مختلفة مشتركة في عارض السواد المقول بالتشكيك، وكذا البياض، فعلى ما ذكروا من أن التضاد الحقيقي لا يكون إلا بين نوعين بينهما غاية الخلاف يلزم أن لا يكون في الألوان إلا بين غاية السواد وغاية البياض.
The third [objection]: that they universally affirm opposition between black and white absolutely, while they are not two ultimate species of color; rather the varying blacks are different species sharing the accident of blackness which is said by analogy, and likewise whiteness. So according to what they mentioned, that true opposition is only between two species between which there is extreme difference, it would follow that among colors there is only opposition between the extreme of blackness and the extreme of whiteness.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The opponents claim that black and white are opposites absolutely, but black and white are not single ultimate species (nawʿayn ākharayn) of color. Rather, there are many species of black (e.g., different shades) and many species of white. The attribute 'blackness' is said by analogy (tashkīk) across these species. If true opposition (taḍād ḥaqīqī) requires two species with extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf), then the only true opposition in colors would be between the extreme black and extreme white, not between any black and any white. This contradicts the common view that all blacks and whites are opposites.

Key Terms
السواد
البياض
النوع الأخير
التشكيك
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Black and white are single, simple species of color.
Correction: Each includes multiple species that share the attribute by analogy, so they are not ultimate species.

Checkpoint
Why do black and white not form a case of simple opposition between two species according to this objection?
A) They are not colors
B) Each includes multiple species, so they are not ultimate species
C) They are not opposites
D) The opposition is only by convention

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that a strict definition of opposition can limit which pairs are truly opposite, because black and white as general terms cover many species.

Fourth Objection: Ibn Sīnā's Inconsistency on Famous Opposition
Fourth objection: Ibn Sīnā's account of famous opposition contradicts his own condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against restricting opposition to two species, which highlights Ibn Sīnā's inconsistency in his account of famous opposition.

Core Idea
Ibn Sīnā's own example of three types contradicts his condition of extreme difference.

Sanusi presents the fourth objection, targeting Ibn Sīnā's own account of famous opposition. Ibn Sīnā required extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) for famous opposition, yet his example includes three terms—courage, recklessness, cowardice—which are not two extremes. This inconsistency weakens his restriction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the objection claims a conflict between Ibn Sīnā's definition and his example.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify Ibn Sīnā's two claims
First, Ibn Sīnā gives an example of famous opposition: courage, recklessness, cowardice. Second, he stipulates that famous opposition requires extreme difference. Note both.
Extreme difference means the two items are as far apart as possible within a genus.
3
Recognize the inconsistency
If famous opposition requires extreme difference, only two terms can be opposed to each other. But his example has three terms, implying opposites beyond two. Hence his own account is inconsistent.
The objection does not say three-term opposition is impossible; it says Ibn Sīnā's condition rules it out.
4
Reflect on the broader argument
Consider why this objection matters: it undermines Ibn Sīnā's restriction of opposition to two species, since his own criteria are not met by his example.
This supports the view that opposition can have more than two species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع أن ما ذكره ابن سينا من تحقيق التضاد المشهورى بين أنواع كالشجاعة والتهور والجبن ينافي ما ذكره من اشتراط غاية الخلاف في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً.
The fourth [objection]: that what Ibn Sīnā mentioned concerning the verification of famous opposition among types such as courage, recklessness, and cowardice conflicts with what he mentioned of stipulating extreme difference in famous opposition as well.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against the restriction of opposition to two species. It targets Ibn Sīnā's (Avicenna) own account. He defined famous opposition (al-taḍād al-mashhūrī) as occurring among multiple types (e.g., courage, recklessness, and cowardice). However, he also stipulated extreme difference (ghāyat al-khilāf) for famous opposition. The objection points out that the example includes three types, not two, and therefore conflicts with the stipulation of extreme difference, which would require only two extremes. Thus, Ibn Sīnā's own view is inconsistent.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Ibn Sīnā explicitly required extreme difference for famous opposition, which his own example violates.

Checkpoint

Denying Essential Opposition Between Unity and Multiplicity
First objection and answer denying essential opposition between unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are not considered essential opposites in kalam, and how the objection based on subject identity is refuted.

Core Idea
Opposition does not require personal identity; specific or generic identity suffices.

This lesson tackles a key logical objection raised against a core theological claim: that unity and multiplicity are not inherently opposed. The author first states his denial of essential opposition, then provides two reasons, and finally addresses a potential counterargument regarding subject identity. Understanding this exchange helps clarify how kalam thinkers handle conceptual relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first makes a claim, then gives two reasons, and then refutes an objection to the first reason.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection says that unity and multiplicity cannot be opposites because their subject is not identical in person. Understand why this seems to disprove opposition.
Think about what 'subject' means here (the thing that is one or many).
3
Analyze the refutation
The author refutes by saying the subject may be identical in species or genus, not necessarily in person. Consider how this preserves the possibility of opposition.
This shows that opposition does not require numerical identity.
4
Reflect on the second reason
The second reason is that one opposite is not constituted by the other. Why does this matter? It reinforces that they are not essential opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : لا تقابل بين الكثرة والوحدة بالذات، بل يعارض العلية والمكيالية، لأن موضوعهما لا يتحد بالشخص، ولأن أحد المتقابلين لا يتقوم بالآخر. ورد الأول : بأن الموضوع قد لا يتحد بالشخص، بل بالنوع أو بالجنس أو بعارض أعم، ومع ذلك فبمجرد الفرض.
He said: There is no essential opposition between multiplicity and unity; rather they are opposed in terms of causality and measurability, because their subject is not identical in person, and because one of the two opposites is not constituted by the other. And the first [objection] was refuted: by [saying] that the subject may not be identical in person, but rather in species or genus or a more general accident, and despite that, [opposition] is merely by assumption.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the denial of opposition between unity (waḥdah) and multiplicity (kathrah). The author claims there is no essential opposition (taqābul bi al-dhāt) between them; rather they are opposed in terms of causality (ʿilliyyah) and measurability (mikyāliyyah). Two reasons are given: (1) their subject is not identical in person, and (2) one opposite is not constituted by the other. Then the first reason is refuted: the subject may not be identical in person but can be identical in species, genus, or a common accident, and even then opposition can be assumed hypothetically. This answer shows that the lack of personal identity does not preclude opposition.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الكثرة
التقابل بالذات
العلية
المكيالية
المتقابلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that for two concepts to be opposites, their subjects must be exactly the same individual.
Correction: The refutation shows that identity in species, genus, or a common accident is sufficient for opposition to be considered, so personal identity is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first reason given for denying essential opposition between unity and multiplicity?
A) Their subjects are always different in person
B) They are not opposed in causality or measurability
C) One opposite is constituted by the other
D) They are actually opposites by essence

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not essential opposites; their opposition is based on causality and measurability, and the objection concerning subject identity is refuted by recognizing that identity in species or genus suffices for opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second Objection: Are Unity and Multiplicity Essentially Opposites?
Second objection and answer: whether one opposite is constituted by the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are not considered essential opposites in Islamic logic, because their essences are not mutually exclusive.

Core Idea
Essential opposites are defined by mutual exclusivity of essences, not by constitution.

In this passage, the author addresses a second objection to the claim that unity and multiplicity are not opposites. The objection suggests that multiplicity is constituted by unity, implying opposition. The answer clarifies what true essential opposites are and shows that unity and multiplicity do not fit that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the objection and answer.
Pay attention to the examples given, like horse and not-horse.
2
Identify the definition of essential opposites
Find the sentence that defines essential opposites (المتقابلين بالذات) and note the condition of mutual exclusivity of essences.
The text says 'اللذان تتنافى حقيقتهما لنفسهما'.
3
Apply the definition to unity and multiplicity
Consider whether unity and multiplicity meet the condition. The passage explicitly states 'لا تنافي بين ذاتيهما'.
They can coexist in the same subject, so they are not essentially opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني بمنع تقوم الكثرة بالوحدة وإنما يتقوم معروضها بمعروضها ولا نزاع في أن المتقابلين إذا أخذا مع الموضوع كالفرس واللا فرس والبصير والأعمى والأب والابن والأسود والأبيض لم يكونا متقابلين بالذات فكيف نفس المعروض لأن المتقابلين بالذات هما الأمران اللذان تتنافى حقيقتهما لنفسهما لا اللازم لهما في معروض يقبلهما، والوحدة والكثرة ليست كذلك إذ لا تنافي بين ذاتيهما .
And the second [objection] was refuted by denying that multiplicity is constituted by unity; rather its subject is constituted by its subject, and there is no dispute that when the two opposites are taken together with their subject—like horse and not-horse, sighted and blind, father and son, black and white—they are not essentially opposites. So [consider] the same subject. For the two essentially opposites are those two realities that are mutually exclusive in themselves, not the necessary concomitants of them in a subject that receives them. And unity and multiplicity are not like that, for there is no mutual exclusivity between their essences.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer regarding the claim that unity and multiplicity are not opposites. The objection was that one opposite is not constituted by the other (e.g., multiplicity is not constituted by unity). The answer denies that multiplicity is constituted by unity; rather, it is the subject (maʿrūḍ) of multiplicity that is constituted by its subject (i.e., the subject of unity). The answer further clarifies that essential opposites (mutāqābilān bi al-dhāt) are those whose realities (ḥaqīqatān) are mutually exclusive in themselves, not their necessary concomitants in a subject. For example, 'horse' and 'not-horse' are not essential opposites because they involve a subject, whereas 'horse' and 'non-horse' considered abstractly might be. Unity and multiplicity do not have mutually exclusive essences; they can coexist in the same subject (e.g., a group is many but also one as a group). Therefore, they are not essentially opposites.

Key Terms
تقوم
المتقابلان بالذات
المعروض
حقيقة
تنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since multiplicity seems to depend on unity (e.g., many things are one group), they might be considered opposites.
Correction: The answer clarifies that essential opposites are defined by mutual exclusivity of essences, not by dependency or constitution. Unity and multiplicity can coexist in the same subject, so they are not opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, why are unity and multiplicity not essential opposites?
A) Because their essences are not mutually exclusive
B) Because multiplicity depends on unity
C) Because they have different subjects
D) Because one is a genus and the other a species

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not essential opposites because their essences do not mutually exclude each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed two things are opposites just because they are often mentioned together? How does this lesson challenge you to think more deeply about what truly defines opposition in your own beliefs?
?
Consider a time when you thought a person or idea was in direct conflict with another. Could it be that the conflict was not in their essences but in how they were related to a common subject? How does this insight change how you approach disagreements?
?

Objection to Real Opposition in Colors
Objection to Ibn Sina's condition for real opposition in colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequence of Ibn Sina's condition for real opposition when applied to colors.

Core Idea
If real opposition requires extreme difference, only extreme black and white truly oppose.

The first objection challenges Ibn Sina's definition of real opposition. By applying his condition to the color spectrum, the author shows that only the extremes qualify as true opposites, excluding intermediate shades. This reveals an inconsistency in Ibn Sina's theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully to grasp the objection. Note the logical structure: premise, condition, conclusion.
The passage begins by describing varying shades of black and white.
2
Identify the condition for real opposition
Find where the author states that real opposition requires extreme difference between two types.
The phrase 'غاية الخلاف' (extreme difference) is key.
3
Understand the argument's logical structure
The argument is conditional: if real opposition requires extreme difference, then only extreme black and white are real opposites.
4
Notice the implication
Intermediate shades are not real opposites, which may conflict with common intuition. Reflect on why this is problematic for Ibn Sina's theory.
The objection does not deny gradations; it shows the condition leads to an unwanted consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليسا نوعين أخيرين من اللون بل السوادات المتفاوتة أنواع مختلفة (۱) مشتركة في عارض السواد المقول بالتشكيك، وكذا البياض، فعلى ما ذكروا من أن التضاد الحقيقي لا يكون إلا بين نوعين بينهما غاية الخلاف يلزم أن لا يكون في الألوان إلا بين غاية السواد وغاية البياض .
الرابع أن ما ذكره ابن سينا من تحقيق التضاد المشهورى بين أنواع كالشجاعة والتهور والجبن (۲) ينافي ما ذكره من اشتراط غاية الخلاف في التضاد المشهورى أيضاً .
They are not two final types of color, but rather the varying blacks are different types (1) sharing in the accident of blackness predicated by analogy, and similarly whiteness. So according to what they mentioned, that real opposition is only between two types between which there is extreme difference, it follows that in colors there is only opposition between extreme black and extreme white.
Fourth: That what Ibn Sina mentioned of verifying popular opposition between types like courage, recklessness, and cowardice (2) contradicts what he mentioned of requiring extreme difference in popular opposition as well.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to Ibn Sina's theory of opposition. The first objection concerns colors: it argues that if real opposition (al-tadadd al-haqiqi) requires extreme difference between two types, then only extreme black and extreme white would be opposites, not gradations. The second objection (the fourth in a series) points out that Ibn Sina's own example of popular opposition (al-tadadd al-mashhuri) between moral qualities like courage, recklessness, and cowardice contradicts his requirement of extreme difference, since these are not extremes but intermediate states.

Key Terms
التضاد الحقيقي
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all shades of black and white are real opposites of each other.
Correction: Under Ibn Sina's condition, only extreme black and extreme white qualify as real opposites.

Checkpoint

A) All colors are opposites of each other.
B) Only extreme black and extreme white would be real opposites.
C) Colors cannot be real opposites at all.
D) Ibn Sina's condition applies only to popular opposition.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's condition for real opposition leads to the counterintuitive result that only pure black and pure white are real opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection to Ibn Sina's Example of Popular Opposition
Objection to Ibn Sina's example of popular opposition contradicting his condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina's own example of popular opposition contradicts his requirement of extreme difference.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's example of courage, recklessness, and cowardice violates his own condition for popular opposition.

The fourth objection targets Ibn Sina's account of popular opposition. He had previously required extreme difference even for this type of opposition, but his example of courage, recklessness, and cowardice does not exhibit extreme difference among them. This exposes an inconsistency in his theory.

Read the passage

4

The example does not satisfy the condition of extreme difference; thus, it contradicts Ibn Sina's own requirement.

C) It is an example of real opposition.
D) It is not a valid example of opposition.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's requirement of extreme difference for popular opposition is undermined by his own example of moral virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you argued for a principle but your own examples or actions contradicted it. How did that feel, and what did you learn about consistency? Can you identify a situation today where you might be doing the same?
?
This lesson shows that even a great scholar like Ibn Sina can have an inconsistency in his reasoning. When you encounter a flaw in someone you respect, how do you respond? Does it humble you or tempt you to dismiss the person entirely? What does that say about your own pursuit of truth?
?
The condition of 'extreme difference' was meant to ensure clarity in opposition. In your own life, where do you need to clarify the boundaries between what you stand for and what you reject? Are your convictions truly opposed to their opposites, or are they on a vague spectrum?

No Direct Opposition Between Unity and Multiplicity
Denial of direct opposition with refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are not direct opposites in Islamic theology and how objections to this view are refuted.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites in themselves—they are related by causality and measurement.

This passage tackles a subtle logical point: the relationship between unity and multiplicity. While they may seem like opposites, the author argues they are not opposites in themselves. Instead, their connection is through causality and measurability. Two objections are raised and refuted, clarifying the nature of true opposition. Let's read the source and unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the key terms: الوحدة, الكثرة, التقابل بالذات.
2
Identify the main claim and reasons
The author makes a claim that there is no direct opposition between unity and multiplicity. Two reasons are given. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the refutations
The author then refutes each reason. Note how the refutation of the first reason appeals to identity by type or genus, and the second refutation denies that multiplicity is constituted by unity.
4
Grasp the concluding principle
The passage ends with a general principle: opposites taken with their subjects are not opposites in themselves. See the examples (horse/non-horse, etc.).
This principle reinforces the earlier denial.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Think about why this distinction matters for understanding God's attributes and creation. Unity and multiplicity are not conflicting in God’s oneness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي التقابل بين الوحدة والكثرة
(قال : لا تقابل بين الكثرة والوحدة بالذات (۳)، بل يعارض العلية (٤) والمكيالية، لأن موضوعهما لا يتحد بالشخص، ولأن أحد المتقابلين لا يتقوم بالآخر . ورد الأول : بأن الموضوع قد لا يتحد بالشخص، بل بالنوع أو بالجنس أو بعارض أعم، ومع ذلك فبمجرد الفرض . والثاني بمنع تقوم الكثرة بالوحدة وإنما يتقوم معروضها، بمعر وضها ) ولا نزاع في أن المتقابلين إذا أخذا مع الموضوع كالفرس واللا فرس والبصير والأعمى والأب والابن والأسود والأبيض لم يكونا متقابلين بالذات (٦) فكيف نفس المعروض
(۱) في (ج) متباينة بدلاً من (مختلفة). (۲) في (ب) الخير بدلاً من (الجبن ) . لأن المتقابلين بالذات هما الأمران اللذان تتنافى حقيقتهما لنفسهما لا اللازم لهما في معروض يقبلهما، والوحدة والكثرة ليست كذلك إذ لا تنافي بين ذاتيهما .
(۳)
(٤) يعارض وصف العلية للوحدة، ووصف المعلولية للكثرة ويعارض وصف المكيالية للوحدة، ووصف المكيالية للكثرة فإن الكثرة حيث كانت هي مجموع فهي مكيلة بالوحدة أي معدودة ومجموعة بها والوحدة مكيال لها أي جامعة وعادة لها . (٥) سقط من (أ) و (ب) بمعروضها . Denial of opposition between unity and multiplicity
(He said: There is no opposition between multiplicity and unity in themselves (3), rather they are associated by causality (4) and measurability, because their subject is not identical in person, and because one of the two opposites is not constituted by the other. The first is refuted: because the subject may not be identical in person, but in type or genus or a more general accident, and yet by mere assumption. The second is refuted by denying that multiplicity is constituted by unity; rather its subject is constituted by its subject.) And there is no dispute that opposites when taken with the subject, like horse and non-horse, sighted and blind, father and son, black and white, are not opposites in themselves (6); then how about the subject itself?
(1) In (c): different instead of varying. (2) In (b): good instead of cowardice. Because the opposites in themselves are the two things whose essences are mutually exclusive for themselves, not what is necessary for them in a subject that admits them, and unity and multiplicity are not like that, since there is no mutual exclusion between their essences.
(3)
(4) The description of causality is associated with unity, and the description of effectness with multiplicity, and the description of measurability with unity, and the description of measurability with multiplicity; for multiplicity, since it is a sum, is measured by unity i.e. counted and aggregated by it, and unity is a measure for it i.e. a gatherer and counter for it. (5) Omitted from (a) and (b): its subject. (6) Unanimously, because when the two loci are considered with the two descriptions, and the two loci have no opposition between them, the two composites have no opposition between them in themselves, but accidentally in view of the description which is part of the composite; so if the composite of accident and subject is considered with another composite of accident and subject, there is no opposition between the two composites despite opposition between the two accidents and the two subjects.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that unity and multiplicity are direct opposites. The author quotes an unnamed philosopher who gives two reasons: (1) their subject is not identical in person, and (2) one opposite is not constituted by the other. The refutation of the first reason is that even if the subject is not identical in person, it may be identical in type or genus, and opposition can still be assumed. The refutation of the second reason is that multiplicity is not constituted by unity; rather, the subject of multiplicity is constituted by its own subject. The passage then explains that when opposites are taken together with their subjects, they are not opposites in themselves, and this is illustrated with examples like horse/non-horse. The notes clarify that true opposites in themselves are those whose essences are mutually exclusive, which is not the case for unity and multiplicity.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الكثرة
التقابل بالذات
العلية
المكيالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are direct opposites in their essences.
Correction: They are not opposites in themselves; their connection is through causality and measurability. True opposites are those whose essences are mutually exclusive, which is not the case here.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first reason given for denying direct opposition between unity and multiplicity?
A) Their subject is not identical in person.
B) They cannot be measured together.
C) One is the cause of the other.
D) They are not found in the same subject.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not direct opposites; their relationship is through causality and measurability, and objections to this are refuted by logical analysis of subject identity and constitution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a situation where you assumed that two things were direct opposites without examining their deeper relationship. How did that assumption affect your judgment? Can you recall a time when you wrongly opposed two things that were actually connected by causality or measurement?
?
The refutations in this passage show that even good reasons can be challenged. In your own faith, are there arguments you hold that might be refuted, but the core truth still stands? What does that teach you about humility in theological reasoning?

Objection on Constitution and the True Opposition of Concepts
Objection and answer followed by clarification of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection about the constitution of subjects is answered and why the real opposition between unity and multiplicity concerns their concepts (divisibility and its absence) rather than their numerical instances.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity as numbers are not opposites; their conceptual meanings are.

This lesson begins with an objection: if the subjects of unity and multiplicity are constituted together, could the two attributes coexist? The answer uses a clever example of a sweet white thing where whiteness and non-whiteness coexist in existence without conceptual opposition. The discussion then clarifies that when scholars deny opposition between unity and multiplicity, they refer to the numerical sense, not the concepts of divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part of the passage where someone objects about constitution and the response using the sweet-white analogy.
Notice the use of 'contradiction' in existence vs. conceptual.
2
Understand the clarification
Read the second part where it is clarified that the denial of opposition is about number, not the concepts of divisibility.
The key is the distinction between multiplicity as number and multiplicity as divisibility.
3
Reflect on the sweet-white analogy
Consider how whiteness and non-whiteness (sweetness) can coexist in one object but are still opposites in meaning.
This shows existence does not require conceptual compatibility.

Source Text

ومن ههنا قيل : إن بين مفهوميهما تقابل الإيجاب والسلب . والحق أنهم أرادوا نفي التقابل بين الكثرة التي هي العدد والوحدة التي منها التعدد، وأما مفهومهما المفسران (۳) بالانقسام وعدمه، فالظاهر تقابلهما بالإيجاب والسلب.
من كلام الفلاسفة أن بين الوحدة والكثرة تقابل التضايف بواسطة ما عرض لهما من العلية ،والمعلولية والمكيالية والمكيلية، وذلك أن الكثرة لما كانت مجتمعة من الوحدات كانت الوحدة علة مقومة للكثرة ومكيالاً لها، والكثرة معلولاً متقوماً بالوحدة ومكيلة بها ، وليس بينهما تقابل بالذات لوجهين : أحدهما : أن موضوع المتقابلين يجب أن يكون واحداً بالشخص بما سبق في تفسير التقابل وموضوع الوحدة والكثرة ليس كذلك، لأنه إذا طرأت الكثرة على الشيء بطلت هويته وحصلت هوية واحدة وكان هذا هو مر الإمام بقوله : إذا طرأت الوحدة بطلت الوحدات لأن التي كانت ثابتة فبطل (1) الوحدانية وبالعكس. أي إذا طرأت الوحدة على الأشياء بطلت الهويات المتكثرة موضوع الكثرة، لأن موضوع الكثره مجموع الوحدات، وإلا فمجموع الوحدات نفس الكثرة لا موضوعها .
وثانيهما : أن الوحدة مقومة للكثرة ، ولا شيء من المتقابلين كذلك. أما فيما يكون أحدهما عدم الآخر فظاهر وأما في التضايف، فلأن المقوم للشيء
For if it is said: The constitution between the two subjects necessitates the possibility of conjunction of the two accidents (1) (2). We say: If conceded, it is according to existence, and that does not negate opposition of affirmation and negation, like the white-sweet thing in which there is whiteness and non-whiteness, i.e., sweetness.
And from here it is said: Between their concepts there is opposition of affirmation and negation. But the truth is that they intended to deny opposition between multiplicity which is number and unity from which comes number. As for their concepts interpreted (3) as divisibility and its absence, then apparently they are opposites by affirmation and negation.
From the speech of the philosophers: Between unity and multiplicity there is reciprocal opposition by means of what occurs to them of causality and effectness and measurability and being-measured. That is because multiplicity, since it is composed of units, unity is a constitutient cause and a measure for multiplicity, and multiplicity is an effect constituted by unity and measured by it. And there is no opposition in itself between them for two reasons: First: The subject of the two opposites must be one in person as previously explained in the interpretation of opposition, and the subject of unity and multiplicity is not like that, because when multiplicity occurs to a thing, its identity is annulled and a single identity results, and this is what the Imam meant by his saying: When unity occurs, the units are annulled because those that were established are annulled, and vice versa. That is, when unity occurs to things, the multiple identities, the subject of multiplicity, are annulled, because the subject of multiplicity is the sum of units; otherwise the sum of units is itself multiplicity, not its subject.
Second: Unity is constitutive of multiplicity, and none of the opposites are like that. As for where one is the absence of the other, it is obvious; as for reciprocal opposition, because the constitutive of a thing
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection: if the subjects of unity and multiplicity are constituted (i.e., their subjects are related), does that not allow the two accidents (unity and multiplicity) to coexist? The answer is that even if conceded, it is only in existence, and that does not contradict opposition of affirmation and negation, as in the example of a sweet white thing where whiteness and non-whiteness (sweetness) coexist. Then it is stated that some say the concepts of unity and multiplicity are opposites by affirmation and negation, but the truth is that the denial of opposition was meant for multiplicity as number and unity as the source of number, not for the concepts of divisibility and its absence, which are indeed opposites. Finally, the philosophers' view is presented: unity and multiplicity are related by reciprocal opposition (taqabul al-taddayuf) through causality and measurability. They are not opposites in themselves for two reasons: (1) their subject is not one in person (when multiplicity occurs, identity is annulled; when unity occurs, multiplicity is annulled), and (2) unity is constitutive of multiplicity, but opposites are not constitutive of each other.

التعدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that unity and multiplicity are always in opposition, or that the denial of opposition applies to all aspects.
Correction: The denial applies only to the numerical sense (multiplicity as number and unity as source of number); the concepts (divisibility and its absence) are indeed opposites by affirmation and negation.

B) To show that two accidents can coexist in existence while their concepts are opposites.
C) To prove that unity and multiplicity cannot coexist.
D) To define the concept of number.

Key Takeaway
The opposition of affirmation and negation applies to the concepts of unity and multiplicity as divisibility and its absence, not to their numerical forms.

Philosophers' Argument for Reciprocal Opposition via Causality
Philosophers' argument for reciprocal opposition with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that unity and multiplicity are in reciprocal opposition (تقابل التضايف) through causality and measurability, and why they are not opposites in themselves.

Core Idea
Unity constitutes multiplicity; opposites do not constitute each other.

After clarifying common misconceptions, the passage presents the philosophers' view: unity and multiplicity are in reciprocal opposition (تقابل التضايف) through causality and measurability. They argue this by showing that unity is a constitutive cause and measure for multiplicity, and that they cannot be direct opposites because their subject is not one and because unity is constitutive of multiplicity—a condition opposites cannot satisfy. The passage ends mid-sentence while explaining this second reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the passage starting from 'From the speech of the philosophers' to understand that they posit reciprocal opposition through causality and measurability.
Note the terms: علة مقومة (constitutive cause) and مكيال (measure).
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two reasons why unity and multiplicity are not opposites in themselves: first, the subject of opposites must be one in person, but here when multiplicity occurs identity changes; second, unity is constitutive of multiplicity.
The second reason is incomplete in the text.
3
Examine the first reason in detail
Read the explanation about how when multiplicity occurs to a thing, its identity is annulled, so there is no single subject for the two.
The Imam's quote illustrates this.
4
Note the incompleteness
Understand that the second reason is cut off. The argument would conclude that since unity is constitutive of multiplicity, they cannot be opposites because constitutive elements are not opposites.
Refer to the lesson's review note.

The Objection of Mutual Dependence and Its Answer
Poses and answers an objection about the mutual dependence of attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about the mutual dependence of attributes is answered by distinguishing between existential coexistence and logical opposition.

Core Idea
Attributes can coexist in existence but be logically opposed.

This lesson presents a dialectical objection and its answer from the text. The objection claims that if two attributes depend on each other's subjects, they must be able to coexist. The answer clarifies that this is true existentially, but does not negate their logical opposition. An example is given to illustrate the point, followed by a nuanced clarification of what the scholars actually intended.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: objection, answer, example, then clarification.
The passage is dense but follows a clear dialectical pattern.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is: 'If the two subjects are mutually dependent (تقوم), then it follows that the two attributes can coexist (جواز اجتماع العارضين).' Understand the reasoning: dependence implies possibility of coexistence.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer concedes the possibility existentially but denies that this contradicts logical opposition (تقابل الإيجاب والسلب). Focus on the phrase 'بحسب الوجود' (in terms of existence). The answer uses the example of a sweet white thing: whiteness and sweetness coexist, but sweetness is not-whiteness (non-whiteness).
The example demonstrates how two attributes can be in the same subject while being logically opposite (one is the negation of the other).
4
Understand the final clarification
The text clarifies that the intended opposition is between multiplicity as number and unity as its source, not between the concepts of divisibility and its absence. Those concepts are indeed opposed by affirmation and negation. This refines the understanding of the original claim.
This prevents a misunderstanding about the scope of the logical opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : التقوم بين المعروضين يستلزم جواز اجتماع العارضين. قلنا : لو سلم فبحسب الوجود وهو لا ينافي تقابل الإيجاب والسلب كالأبيض الحلو فيه البياض واللا بياض أعني الحلاوة. ومن ههنا قيل : إن بين مفهوميهما تقابل الإيجاب والسلب . والحق أنهم أرادوا نفي التقابل بين الكثرة التي هي العدد والوحدة التي منها التعدد، وأما مفهومهما المفسران بالانقسام وعدمه، فالظاهر تقابلهما بالإيجاب والسلب.
If it is said: The mutual dependence between the two subjects entails the possibility of the two attributes coming together. We say: Even if granted, it is in terms of existence, and that does not contradict the opposition of affirmation and negation, like the sweet white thing in which there is whiteness and non-whiteness, namely sweetness. And from here it is said that between their two concepts there is the opposition of affirmation and negation. The truth is that they intended to negate the opposition between multiplicity, which is number, and unity, from which plurality comes; but as for their two concepts explained as divisibility and its absence, the apparent is that they are opposed by affirmation and negation.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objection claims that mutual dependence of the two subjects (the bearers of multiplicity and unity) entails the possibility of the two attributes coexisting. The answer refutes this by saying that even if it is granted in terms of existence (i.e., they can coexist in a single subject in existence), it does not contradict the logical opposition of affirmation and negation. The answer gives the example of a white and sweet object: it has whiteness (affirmation) and non-whiteness (sweetness is not whiteness). Then it clarifies that the intended opposition is between multiplicity as number and unity as the source of number, not between the conceptual definitions of divisibility and its absence, which are indeed opposed by affirmation and negation.

Key Terms
تقابل الإيجاب والسلب
التقوم
المعروضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer concedes the objection entirely, meaning there is no logical opposition between unity and multiplicity.
Correction: The answer distinguishes between existential coexistence and logical opposition. It affirms logical opposition (affirmation/negation) even if coexistence is allowed existentially. The example and final clarification show that the opposition is real at the conceptual level.

Checkpoint
What is the example given in the answer to show that two attributes can coexist in existence while being opposed logically?
A) A black and bitter thing
B) A sweet white thing
C) A hot and cold thing
D) A square and circle

Key Takeaway
The answer shows that two attributes can coexist in the same subject existentially without negating their logical opposition, as exemplified by whiteness and non-whiteness in a sweet white thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that two opposite concepts can coexist existentially without contradiction. Where in your life do you hold two seemingly opposing truths—like mercy and justice, hope and fear of God—and struggle to reconcile them? Are you willing to accept that they can coexist in God's wisdom even if your mind sees a logical opposition? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The sweet white thing example shows that whiteness and non-whiteness (sweetness) are logically opposed yet coexist. Is there a quality or action in your own character that you consider 'logically opposite' to being a good Muslim—like anger or weakness? Philosophers' View on Unity and Multiplicity
Philosophers argue unity and multiplicity are in correlation, not essential opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite but correlated through causality and measurability, and the two reasons they give for this.

Core Idea
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites but correlates through cause and effect.

This passage presents the philosophers' argument that the relationship between unity and multiplicity is one of correlation (تقابل التضايف) via causality and measurability, not essential opposition. They provide two proofs to show why they cannot be essentially opposed. Read carefully to follow their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Notice the opening: the philosophers state that between unity and multiplicity there is the opposition of correlation (تقابل التضايف) mediated by causality and measurability.
2
Understand the correlation
Multiplicity is composed of units, so unity is the cause and measure of multiplicity, while multiplicity is the effect and the measured.
3
Examine the first reason
The subject of opposites must be one in person, but when multiplicity comes upon a thing, its identity is nullified and a single identity occurs, so the subject changes.
This is illustrated by the Imam's quote.
4
Examine the second reason
Unity is constitutive of multiplicity, but among opposites (absence, correlation, contrariety) nothing is constitutive of its opposite.
In correlation, the constitutive precedes; in contrariety, they do not coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كلام الفلاسفة أن بين الوحدة والكثرة تقابل التضايف بواسطة ما عرض لهما من العلية والمعلولية والمكيالية والمكيلية، وذلك أن الكثرة لما كانت مجتمعة من الوحدات كانت الوحدة علة مقومة للكثرة ومكيالاً لها، والكثرة معلولاً متقوماً بالوحدة ومكيلة بها ، وليس بينهما تقابل بالذات لوجهين : أحدهما : أن موضوع المتقابلين يجب أن يكون واحداً بالشخص بما سبق في تفسير التقابل وموضوع الوحدة والكثرة ليس كذلك، لأنه إذا طرأت الكثرة على الشيء بطلت هويته وحصلت هوية واحدة وكان هذا هو مراد الإمام بقوله : إذا طرأت الوحدة بطلت الوحدات التي كانت ثابتة فبطل موضوع الكثرة وبالعكس. أي إذا طرأت الوحدة على الأشياء بطلت الهويات المتكثرة موضوع الكثرة، لأن موضوع الكثره مجموع الوحدات، وإلا فمجموع الوحدات نفس الكثرة لا موضوعها . وثانيهما : أن الوحدة مقومة للكثرة ، ولا شيء من المتقابلين كذلك. أما فيما يكون أحدهما عدم الآخر فظاهر وأما في التضايف، فلأن المقوم للشيء يتقدم عليه وجوداً أو تعقلاً . والمتضايفان يكونان معاً في التعقل والوجود، وأما في التضاد، فلأن المقوم للشيء يجامعه ، والضد لا يجامع الضد، بل يدافعه.
From the discourse of the philosophers: between unity and multiplicity there is the opposition of correlation by means of what happens to them of causality and effectness and measurableness and measuredness. That is because multiplicity, since it is composed of units, unity is a constitutive cause of multiplicity and a measure of it, and multiplicity is an effect constituted by unity and measured by it. And there is no essential opposition between them for two reasons: First: The subject of opposites must be one in person according to what preceded in the explanation of opposition, but the subject of unity and multiplicity is not like that, because when multiplicity comes upon a thing, its identity is nullified and a single identity occurs — and this is what the Imam intended by his saying: 'When unity comes upon things, the units that were established are nullified, so the subject of multiplicity is nullified' and vice versa. That is, when unity comes upon things, the multiplied identities (the subject of multiplicity) are nullified, because the subject of multiplicity is the aggregate of units; otherwise, the aggregate of units is multiplicity itself, not its subject. Second: Unity is constitutive for multiplicity, and nothing among opposites is like that. As for that in which one of them is the absence of the other, it is apparent; and as for in correlation, because what is constitutive of a thing precedes it in existence or intellection, but the two correlates are together in intellection and existence. And as for in opposition, because what is constitutive of a thing coexists with it, but a contrary does not coexist with a contrary, rather it repels it.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view that unity and multiplicity are not opposites in essence but have a relation of correlation due to causality and measurability. Multiplicity is composed of units, so unity is the cause and measure of multiplicity, while multiplicity is the effect and the measured. Then the author gives two reasons why they are not essentially opposed. First, the subject of opposites must be one in person, but when multiplicity comes, the previous identity is nullified and a new unity appears, so the subject changes; thus they cannot be opposites. The Imam's quote illustrates this: unity nullifies the multiple identities. Second, unity is constitutive of multiplicity, but among opposites (whether by absence, correlation, or contrariety) nothing is constitutive of its opposite. In correlation, the two are simultaneous, but the constitutive precedes; in contrariety, the constitutive coexists, but contraries repel each other.

Key Terms
تقابل التضايف
العلية والمعلولية
المكيالية والمكيلية
مقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two reasons are themselves the opposition; they actually prove non-essential opposition.
Correction: The two reasons are arguments against essential opposition, not examples of it. They show why unity and multiplicity cannot be essentially opposed.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can unity and multiplicity not be essentially opposed based on the first reason?
A) Because they have the same subject
B) Because their subject changes when one comes
C) Because they are correlated
D) Because unity is not a cause

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that unity and multiplicity are not essentially opposite but are related through causality and measurability, as shown by the changing subject and the constitutive relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage explains that unity is the constitutive cause and measure of multiplicity. In your own life, what is the single unifying principle (e.g., your purpose, your faith, your core value) that gives shape and measure to all your diverse activities and goals? Are you actually letting that unity be the cause, or have you let multiplicity take over?
?
When the philosophers say that when multiplicity comes upon a thing, its identity is nullified and a single identity occurs, think of a time when you tried to juggle too many priorities. Did you lose your sense of self? What would it mean for you to let unity (tawhid, sincerity) nullify the scattered identities you've accumulated?
?
If unity and multiplicity are not opposites but correlates, how should that affect your understanding of Allah's oneness and the diversity of creation? Do you see the multiplicity of the world as separate from Him, or as something caused by and measured by His unity? What practical shift in your worship could come from truly internalizing this correlation?
