Second Objection: Coexistence of Affirmation-Negation Opposites
Answers an objection about coexistence of opposites in a single locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator answers an objection about opposites coexisting in a single locus and why he ultimately critiques the Imam's reasoning.

Core Idea
Opposites can coexist in existence, not truth — but even that answer is weak.

The discussion of unity and multiplicity continues with a second objection. The objection claims that two opposites cannot coexist in the same subject, so the earlier argument is sufficient. The answer points to a later principle that affirmation-negation opposites can coexist in a single entity in terms of existence. However, the commentator finds this reasoning flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. Note that it claims coexistence in the locus negates the receptacle absolutely.
2
Read the answer
The answer denies this and refers to a coming discussion where opposites by affirmation-negation can coexist when a single identity occurs.
The Imam's statement is quoted: when unity comes, units are nullified.
3
Understand the distinction
The answer specifies that this coexistence is in terms of existence, not truth. Reflect on what this distinction means.
4
Note the critique
The commentator states that both faces (the two reasons given by the Imam) are weak. Consider why this undermines the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا كاف في الكل لأن الاجتماع في المحل ينافي القابل مطلقاً . قلنا : ممنوع لما سيجيء من أن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب قد يجتمعان في محل إذا وحصلت هوية واحدة وكان هذا مراد الإمام بقوله : إذا طرأت الوحدة بطلت الوحدات التي كانت ثابتة، فبطل موضوع الكثرة ) كان ذلك بحسب الوجود دون الصدق وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .

We say: Denied, because, as will come, the two opposites by affirmation and negation may coexist in a locus when a single identity occurs — and this was the meaning of the Imam by his saying: 'When unity comes upon things, the units that were established are nullified, so the subject of multiplicity is nullified.' That was in terms of existence, not truth. And both faces are weak.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection claims that coexistence of opposites in a single locus negates the receptacle (the subject) absolutely, so the earlier argument about unity and multiplicity being not opposites is sufficient. The answer denies this by referring to a later point: opposites by affirmation and negation (like unity and multiplicity in a certain sense) can coexist in a locus when a single identity occurs, citing the Imam's statement. This coexistence is in terms of existence, not truth. However, the commentator then critiques by saying that both faces (the two reasons given earlier by the Imam) are weak.

The Subject of Opposites Need Not Be One in Person
Critique of the first face: subjects of opposites need not be one in person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of opposites can vary in unity (person, species, genus, or accidental) and how this weakens the Imam's first argument.

Core Idea
Opposites can share a subject in many ways — not just individually.

This passage critiques the first reason given by the Imam that the subject of opposites must be one in person. The commentator argues that unity can take several forms, such as by species or genus, and provides concrete examples. This shows that the Imam's criterion is not universally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the examples given for each type of unity.
The passage is a direct critique of a previous argument.
2
Identify the types of unity
The commentator lists four types: one in person, by species, by genus, and by a more general accidental attribute. Find each example and see how it fits.
These are found in the middle of the passage.
3
Understand the critique
The commentator argues that these examples show that the subject of opposites is not necessarily one in person, thus refuting the Imam's first face. Also note the mention of the Imam's view on alternation.
The critique does not reject all arguments, only this specific one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول فلأن موضوع المتقابلين لا يلزم أن يكون واحداً بالشخص فكيف يتصور ذلك في مثل الفرسية واللا فرسية، بل صرحوا بأنه قد يكون واحداً بالشخص كالعدل والجور لزيد أو بالنوع كالرجولية والمرئية للإنسان أو بالجنس كالزوجية والفردية للعدد أو بأمر أعم عارض كالخير والشر ذلك فيكفي الفرض والتقدير كإنسان للفرسية واللافرسية في قولنا : للشيء، ومع الإنسان فرس ، والإنسان ليس بفرس. والإمام رحمه الله جعل عدم اتحاد موضوع الوحدة والكثرة دليل عدم التضاد بينهما، فإن من شأن الضدين التعاقب على موضوع واحد، ولو بالإمكان كما إذا كان أحدهما لازماً كسواد الغراب
As for the first [face], it is because the subject of opposites is not necessarily one in person — so how can that be imagined in something like horse-ness and non-horse-ness? Rather, they explicitly stated that it may be one in person, like justice and injustice for Zayd, or by species like masculinity and visibility for man, or by genus like evenness and oddness for number, or by a more general accidental matter like good and evil — that suffices by supposition and estimation, like 'man' for horse-ness and non-horse-ness in our statement: 'for the thing, and there is a horse with man, and man is not a horse.' And the Imam, may God have mercy on him, made the non-unity of the subject of unity and multiplicity a proof of the lack of opposition between them, for it is the nature of two contraries to alternate over a single subject, even if possibly, as when one of them is necessary like the blackness of the raven.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage critiques the first face (the first reason given by the Imam that the subject must be one in person). The commentator argues that the subject of opposites does not necessarily have to be one in person; it can be one in person, species, genus, or even by a more general accidental attribute. Examples are given: justice and injustice for Zayd (one person), masculinity and visibility for man (species), evenness and oddness for number (genus), good and evil (general accidental). Even a hypothetical subject like 'man' suffices for horse-ness and non-horse-ness. The Imam's criterion for contraries — that they alternate over one subject — is then discussed, with the example of blackness of the raven being necessary but still a true contrary. The passage indicates that the Imam's argument is not conclusive.

Correlatives and Contraries in Kalam
Sanusi defines correlatives and contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between correlatives and contraries in Islamic theology and why contraries cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Correlatives coexist in thought and reality; contraries repel one another.

Before diving into complex theological debates, we must grasp basic logical distinctions. The author here defines two key terms—correlatives and contraries—which are foundational for understanding arguments about divine attributes and creation. This lesson clarifies their difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Start by reading the Arabic passage that defines correlatives and contraries. Note how correlatives must exist together both in the mind and in reality.
The passage begins with a remark about precedence, then gives the definition.
2
Understand contrariety
Contraries are explained as opposites that cannot coexist; they repel each other. Contrast this with correlatives which are together.
The author uses the example of a constitutive part vs. a true contrary.
3
Compare and contrast
Summarize the key difference: correlatives are inseparable in existence and conception, while contraries cannot be together in either sense.
Think of examples like father-son (correlatives) vs. black-white (contraries).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتقدم عليه وجوداً أو تعقلاً. والمتضايفان يكونان معاً في التعقل والوجود، وأما في التضاد، فلأن المقوم للشيء يجامعه، والضد لا يجامع الضد، بل يدافعه. فإن قيل: هذا كاف في الكل لأن الاجتماع في المحل ينافي القابل مطلقاً. قلنا: ممنوع لما سيجيء من أن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب قد يجتمعان في محل إذا وحصلت هوية واحدة وكان هذا مراد الإمام بقوله: إذا طرأت الوحدة بطلت الوحدات التي كانت ثابتة، فبطل موضوع الكثرة. كان ذلك بحسب الوجود دون الصدق وكلا الوجهين ضعيف. أما الأول فلأن موضوع المتقابلين لا يلزم أن يكون واحداً بالشخص فكيف يتصور ذلك في مثل الفرسية واللا فرسية، بل صرحوا بأنه قد يكون واحداً بالشخص كالعدل والجور لزيد أو بالنوع كالرجولية والمرئية للإنسان أو بالجنس كالزوجية والفردية للعدد أو بأمر أعم عارض كالخير والشر ذلك فيكفي الفرض والتقدير كإنسان للفرسية واللافرسية في قولنا: للشيء، ومع الإنسان فرس، والإنسان ليس بفرس. والإمام رحمه الله جعل عدم اتحاد موضوع الوحدة والكثرة دليل عدم التضاد بينهما، فإن من شأن الضدين التعاقب على موضوع واحد، ولو بالإمكان كما إذا كان أحدهما لازماً كسواد الغراب.
One of them takes precedence over the other in existence or conception. The two correlatives exist together in conception and existence. As for contrariety, because that which constitutes a thing coexists with it, while the contrary does not coexist with the contrary, but rather repels it. If it is said: This is sufficient in all cases because coexistence in a locus absolutely prevents the receptive. We say: It is forbidden because of what will come that two opposites by affirmation and negation may coexist in a locus when a single identity obtains, and this is what the Imam meant by his saying: 'When unity occurs, the unities that were established are invalidated, so the subject of multiplicity becomes invalid.' That was according to existence, not truth, and both aspects are weak. As for the first, because the subject of two opposites is not necessarily one in person; how can that be conceived in something like horseness and non-horseness? Rather, they have stated that it may be one in person like justice and injustice for Zayd, or in species like manliness and visibility for man, or in genus like evenness and oddness for number, or by a broader concomitant like good and evil. So supposition and estimation suffice, like man for horseness and non-horseness in our saying: 'the thing, and with man is a horse, and man is not a horse.' The Imam, may God have mercy on him, made the non-unity of the subject of unity and multiplicity evidence for the lack of contrariety between them, for it is the nature of two contraries to succeed each other on one subject, even potentially, as if one of them is necessary like the blackness of the raven.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between correlatives (المتضايفان) and contraries (الضدان). Correlatives, like father and son, must coexist in both existence and mental conception; one cannot be conceived without the other. In contrast, a constitutive part of a thing coexists with that thing, but a contrary repels its contrary and cannot coexist with it in the same subject. The author then raises an objection: if two things cannot coexist in a single locus because that would prevent the receptive, then the above distinctions are unnecessary. The response is that this is not always the case: two opposites by affirmation and negation, such as ‘man’ and ‘not man’, can coexist in a single locus when a single identity (هوية واحدة) is considered, as explained by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). The author then critiques two weak interpretations of this coexistence: first, the subject of two opposites need not be one in person; it can be one in species, genus, or by a broader concomitant. Second, the Imam argued that unity and multiplicity are not contraries because their subject is not identical; contraries require successive occurrence on a single subject. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
المتضايفان
التضاد
المتقابلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposites behave the same way; they cannot coexist in any sense.
Correction: Correlatives must exist together, while contraries cannot coexist. They are distinct types of opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between correlatives (المتضايفان)?
A) They exist together in existence but not necessarily in conception.
B) They exist together in both conception and existence.
C) They cannot coexist and repel each other.
D) They succeed each other on a single subject.

Key Takeaway
Correlatives must exist together in both thought and reality, while contraries cannot coexist and instead repel each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship where you are a correlative to someone else (e.g., parent-child, teacher-student). Can you truly exist independently in that role? How does this passage remind you that your very identity is tied to others, and what responsibility does that place on you today?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God that assumed two attributes (like mercy and justice) are contraries that cannot coexist? Confront that assumption now: based on this passage, how might they actually be correlatives that must coexist? What does that mean for how you approach God in prayer today?
?
The passage says that a contrary repels its opposite. Is there a sin or habit in your life that you treat as a 'correlative'—thinking it can coexist with faith? Name it. What would it take to treat it as a true contrary that must be repelled, starting now?

Coexistence of Opposites in a Single Locus
Sanusi refutes an objection about coexistence of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that opposites cannot coexist in a single locus and how scholars argue that affirmation and negation can coexist under certain conditions.

Core Idea
Opposites by affirmation and negation can coexist in one locus when a single identity is considered.

After distinguishing correlatives and contraries, the author addresses a potential objection: if coexistence in a locus is impossible for contraries, then all opposites are prevented. He responds by showing that the opposites 'affirmation and negation' (like 'man' and 'not-man') can be true of the same subject when considered in different respects or as a single identity. This lesson unpacks that nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that if coexistence in a locus is impossible for any opposites, then the previous distinctions are unnecessary. Read the Arabic carefully.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
2
Understand the response
The author responds that opposites by affirmation and negation can coexist when a single identity obtains. He cites Imam al-Razi's statement about unity and multiplicity.
This is a key point: affirmation and negation are not like contraries.
3
Examine the critique
The author then critiques two weak interpretations of this coexistence. The first is about the subject not needing to be one in person; it can be one in species, genus, etc.
Examples: justice/injustice for Zayd (one person), manliness/visibility for man (species), etc.
4
Grasp the Imam's argument
The Imam argued that unity and multiplicity are not contraries because their subject is not identical; contraries must succeed each other on one subject.
This is a subtle logical point about the nature of contrariety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتقدم عليه وجوداً أو تعقلاً. والمتضايفان يكونان معاً في التعقل والوجود، وأما في التضاد، فلأن المقوم للشيء يجامعه، والضد لا يجامع الضد، بل يدافعه. فإن قيل: هذا كاف في الكل لأن الاجتماع في المحل ينافي القابل مطلقاً. قلنا: ممنوع لما سيجيء من أن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب قد يجتمعان في محل إذا وحصلت هوية واحدة وكان هذا مراد الإمام بقوله: إذا طرأت الوحدة بطلت الوحدات التي كانت ثابتة، فبطل موضوع الكثرة. كان ذلك بحسب الوجود دون الصدق وكلا الوجهين ضعيف. أما الأول فلأن موضوع المتقابلين لا يلزم أن يكون واحداً بالشخص فكيف يتصور ذلك في مثل الفرسية واللا فرسية، بل صرحوا بأنه قد يكون واحداً بالشخص كالعدل والجور لزيد أو بالنوع كالرجولية والمرئية للإنسان أو بالجنس كالزوجية والفردية للعدد أو بأمر أعم عارض كالخير والشر ذلك فيكفي الفرض والتقدير كإنسان للفرسية واللافرسية في قولنا: للشيء، ومع الإنسان فرس، والإنسان ليس بفرس. والإمام رحمه الله جعل عدم اتحاد موضوع الوحدة والكثرة دليل عدم التضاد بينهما، فإن من شأن الضدين التعاقب على موضوع واحد، ولو بالإمكان كما إذا كان أحدهما لازماً كسواد الغراب.
One of them takes precedence over the other in existence or conception. The two correlatives exist together in conception and existence. As for contrariety, because that which constitutes a thing coexists with it, while the contrary does not coexist with the contrary, but rather repels it. If it is said: This is sufficient in all cases because coexistence in a locus absolutely prevents the receptive. We say: It is forbidden because of what will come that two opposites by affirmation and negation may coexist in a locus when a single identity obtains, and this is what the Imam meant by his saying: 'When unity occurs, the unities that were established are invalidated, so the subject of multiplicity becomes invalid.' That was according to existence, not truth, and both aspects are weak. As for the first, because the subject of two opposites is not necessarily one in person; how can that be conceived in something like horseness and non-horseness? Rather, they have stated that it may be one in person like justice and injustice for Zayd, or in species like manliness and visibility for man, or in genus like evenness and oddness for number, or by a broader concomitant like good and evil. So supposition and estimation suffice, like man for horseness and non-horseness in our saying: 'the thing, and with man is a horse, and man is not a horse.' The Imam, may God have mercy on him, made the non-unity of the subject of unity and multiplicity evidence for the lack of contrariety between them, for it is the nature of two contraries to succeed each other on one subject, even potentially, as if one of them is necessary like the blackness of the raven.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between correlatives (المتضايفان) and contraries (الضدان). Correlatives, like father and son, must coexist in both existence and mental conception; one cannot be conceived without the other. In contrast, a constitutive part of a thing coexists with that thing, but a contrary repels its contrary and cannot coexist with it in the same subject. The author then raises an objection: if two things cannot coexist in a single locus because that would prevent the receptive, then the above distinctions are unnecessary. The response is that this is not always the case: two opposites by affirmation and negation, such as ‘man’ and ‘not man’, can coexist in a single locus when a single identity (هوية واحدة) is considered, as explained by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). The author then critiques two weak interpretations of this coexistence: first, the subject of two opposites need not be one in person; it can be one in species, genus, or by a broader concomitant. Second, the Imam argued that unity and multiplicity are not contraries because their subject is not identical; contraries require successive occurrence on a single subject. Thus, the objection fails.

Correction: Opposites by affirmation and negation can coexist when a single identity is considered, as with 'man' and 'not-man'.

B) When a single identity (هوية واحدة) is obtained.
C) When the opposites are not truly contraries.
D) When the locus is purely conceptual.

Key Takeaway

Second Objection on Unity and Multiplicity: Constitution of Subject
The author addresses the objection that multiplicity's subject is constituted by unity's subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the essential opposition between unity and multiplicity against the objection that multiplicity is composed of unities, by distinguishing between essence and subject constitution and by invoking the role of predication type.

Core Idea
Composite multiplicity does not contradict essential opposition—predication type determines coexistence.

This passage tackles a second objection: if multiplicity is composed of unities, how can they be truly opposite? The author carefully distinguishes two senses of 'constitution' and uses the distinction between comprehensive and derivative predication to show that opposites can coexist in the same subject without contradiction. The argument draws on logical principles fundamental to kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument settle before analyzing it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where the author splits the objection into two meanings: (a) essence of multiplicity constituted by essence of unity, and (b) subject of multiplicity constituted by subject of unity. Notice the different responses.
The rejection of (a) is straightforward; the acceptance of (b) leads to further discussion.
3
Analyze the objection to (b) and the response
The objector argues that if one subject constitutes another, they must coexist in a single locus, negating opposition. See how the author denies the necessity of a single locus and then qualifies the notion of coexistence.
Look for the shift from 'coexistence in existence' to 'coexistence in a locus'.
4
Understand the predication distinction
The author invokes the logical distinction between comprehensive predication (حمل الموطأة) and derivative predication (حمل الاشتقاق) to show that opposites can coexist under derivative predication, as in the example of 'sweet white'.
This is the key move that preserves essential opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني: فلأنه إن أريد أن ذات الكثرة متقومة بذات الوحدة فممنوع إما بحسب الخارج فلأنهما اعتباران عقليان. وإما بحسب الذهن فلأنا نعقل الكثرة، وهو كون الشيء بحيث ينقسم، بدون تعقل الوحدة وهـو كونه بحيث لا ينقسم، وإن أريد أن معروض الكثرة متقوم بمعروض الوحدة، بمعنى أن الكثير مؤلف يصدق على كل جزء منه، أنه واحد وهذا معنى اجتماع الكثرة من الوحدات فمسلم لكنه لا ينافي التقابل الذاتي بين الوحدة والكثرة، العارضتين بل معروضيهما، ولا نزاع في ذلك، ألا ترى أنهم اتفقوا على أن المتقابلين بالذات إذا أخذا مع الموضوع كالفرس واللا فرس وكالبصير والأعمى، وكالأب والابن وكالأسود والأبيض، لم يكن تقابلهما بالذات، فكيف إذا أخذ نفس الموضوعين. فإن قيل: المراد الثاني وهو ينافي التقابل لأن كون أحد المعروضين مقوماً بالآخر يستلزم اجتماعهما ضرورة اجتماع الكل والجزء وهو يستلزم اجتماع وصفيهما أو إمكانه لا أقل. قلنا ممنوع وإنما يلزم لو كان المعروضان في محل، وهو ليس بلازم وإنما اللازم اجتماعهما في الوجود، ولو سلم، فالاجتماع في المحل إنما ينافي جميع أقسام التقابل إذا كان بحسب الصدق، أعني حمل الموطأة لا بحسب الوجود أعني حمل الاشتقاق لما ذكر في أساس المنطق، من أن امتناع المتقابلين في موضوع واحد يعتبر في تقابل الإيجاب والسلب بحسب الصدق عليه، وفي البواقي بحسب الوجود فيه، كالأبيض الحلو، فإن فيه البياض واللا بياض لأن اللابياض مقول على الحلاوة.
As for the second: If it is intended that the essence of multiplicity is constituted by the essence of unity, it is forbidden, either externally because they are mental considerations, or mentally because we conceive multiplicity—which is that a thing is such that it is divisible—without conceiving unity—which is that it is such that it is not divisible. If it is intended that the subject of multiplicity is constituted by the subject of unity, meaning that the many is composed and it is true of each part of it that it is one, and that is the meaning of multiplicity being composed of unities, then it is granted, but it does not negate the essential opposition between unity and multiplicity as accidents, nor between their subjects, and there is no dispute about that. Do you not see that they have agreed that essential opposites when taken with the subject, like horse and non-horse, and seeing and blind, and father and son, and black and white, they are not essentially opposite; what then if the subjects themselves are taken? If it is said: The second meaning is intended, and it negates opposition because one of the two subjects being constituted by the other necessitates their coexistence necessarily due to the coexistence of whole and part, and that necessitates the coexistence of their attributes or at least its possibility. We say: It is forbidden. It would only be necessitated if the two subjects were in a [single] locus, but that is not necessary; the only necessity is their coexistence in existence. And if it is granted, coexistence in a locus only negates all types of opposition if it is with respect to truth, i.e., comprehensive predication (al-ḥaml al-muwaṭaʾa), not with respect to existence, i.e., derivative predication (al-ḥaml al-ishtiqāq), as mentioned in the Fundamentals of Logic, that the impossibility of two opposites in a single subject is considered in the opposition of affirmation and negation with respect to truth predicated upon it, and in the remaining [types] with respect to existence in it, like sweet white—for in it there is whiteness and non-whiteness because non-whiteness is said of sweetness.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on unity and multiplicity by addressing a second objection. The author explains two possible interpretations of the claim that multiplicity is constituted by unity. First, if it means the essence of multiplicity is constituted by the essence of unity, this is rejected both externally (since they are merely mental considerations) and mentally (since we can conceive multiplicity without conceiving unity). Second, if it means the subject of multiplicity (the thing that is multiple) is constituted by the subject of unity (i.e., the multiple is composed of unities), this is accepted but does not negate the essential opposition between unity and multiplicity as accidents inhering in a subject. The author clarifies that essential opposites, when taken together with their subject, lose their essential opposition; for example, 'horse' and 'non-horse' are not essentially opposite when attached to a specific subject. The objection then argues that if one subject is constituted by the other, they must coexist, which would imply coexistence of their attributes. The answer is that this would only require a single locus for the subjects if they were in a locus, but the only necessity is coexistence in existence, not in the same locus. Even if they did coexist in the same locus, opposition is only negated when predication is by truth (comprehensive predication, like 'Zayd is a man') rather than by existence (derivative predication, like 'Zayd is seeing'). The author cites the example of 'sweet white': the thing is both white (whiteness) and not-white (since non-whiteness is attributed to sweetness), showing that two opposites can exist in the same subject under different types of predication.

Key Terms
الكثرة
الوحدة
التقابل الذاتي
حمل الموطأة
حمل الاشتقاق
أساس المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If multiplicity is composed of unities, then unity and multiplicity cannot be opposites because they coexist in the same subject.
Correction: Coexistence of opposites in a subject does not negate essential opposition if predication is derivative. The same subject can be both white (comprehensive) and non-white (derivative) as in 'sweet white'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the first interpretation (essence constitution)?
A) Because multiplicity cannot be conceived without unity.
B) Because unity and multiplicity are both mental considerations and we can conceive multiplicity without unity.
C) Because the objection is invalid from the start.
D) Because essential opposites cannot be constituted by each other.

Key Takeaway
Essential opposites like unity and multiplicity can coexist in the same subject without contradiction when predication is derivative rather than comprehensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship where you see someone as both 'good' and 'bad'. Do you treat these as essential opposites that cannot coexist? Using the lesson's distinction, can you see how they might be true under derivative predication (different aspects), and might that soften your judgment? What would it take for you to hold both truths without collapsing into contradiction?
?
Is there a part of your own character you consider incompatible with another (e.g., being gentle and assertive)? Have you ever dismissed one as hypocritical? Apply the author's insight: could these be attributes predicted derivatively from different aspects of your self? How would living with that nuance change your self-acceptance or growth?
?
When you encounter a theological paradox (e.g., God's mercy and wrath), do you tend to resolve it by denying one or the other? Could the logical framework here help you hold both without compromising their essential realities? What would it cost you to embrace that tension in your faith?

The logical relationship between unity and multiplicity: no opposition
The author argues that unity and multiplicity are not opposites, using logical principles and addressing an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unity and multiplicity are not considered logical opposites in classical Kalam, and how the argument from composition refutes apparent opposition.

Core Idea
Multiplicity is built from units, so unity and multiplicity cannot be opposites.

This passage tackles a subtle logical point: whether unity (al-wahda) and multiplicity (al-kathra) are opposites. At first glance, they seem contradictory—many vs. one—but the author argues that they are not true opposites because multiplicity is composed of units. This lesson will unpack that argument and address a potential objection involving the transformation of many into one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own wording set the stage.
Notice the dense logical style typical of kalam works.
2
Understand the general logical principle
The passage opens with a principle about impossibility in existence and truth. Grasp this principle as it underpins the whole argument.
3
Apply the principle to unity and multiplicity
See how the principle leads to the conclusion that there is no evidence for negating opposition between unity and multiplicity.
The author points out that their own interpretation actually supports a connection.
4
Grasp why multiplicity being composed of units precludes opposition
Multiplicity (number) is constituted by units, so number and one are not opposite. This is the core insight.
5
Examine the objection and its refutation
The author presents an objection: when many become one (e.g., pouring waters together), it seems unity cancels multiplicity. Then he recounts and critiques the Imam's refutation.
The author finds both the objection's force and the refutation weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودة فيه ، والمقول على الموجود في الموضوع موجود في الموضوع، ثم ما يمتنع اجتماعه بحسب الوجود يمتنع بحسب الصدق من غير عكس، وما يجوز بحسب الصدق يجوز بحسب الوجود من غير عكس، فظهر أنه لا دليل على نفي تقابل الإيجاب والسلب من الوحدة والكثرة، بل تفسيرهم الكثرة بالانقسام مطلقاً ، أو إلى المتشابهات والوحدة بعدمه ظاهر في ثبوت ذلك، وأما اتفاقهم على نفي التقابل بينهما فمعناه أن الكثرة أي العدد لما كانت متقومة بالآحاد ومتحصلة من انضمامها مجتمعة الواحد في المعدود، لم يكن بين العدد والواحد تقابل أصلا، وهذا ظاهر، فيما هو جزء الكثرة، وأما الوحدة التي ترد على الكثرة فتبطلها، كما إذا جعلت مياه الكيزان في كوز واحد فقد يوهم تضادهما، بناء على تواردهما على موضوع واحد هو ذلك الماء ، بطلان أحدهما بالآخر . ونفاه الإمام بأنهما ليسا على غاية الخلاف، وبأن موضوع كل من الوحدات الزائلة التي هي نفس الكثرة جزء موضوع الوحدة الطارئة لا نفسه والكل ضعيف .
What is present in it, and what is predicated on what exists in the subject is present in the subject. Then, what is impossible for it to combine according to existence is impossible according to truth without the reverse, and what is permissible according to truth is permissible according to existence without the reverse. So it appears that there is no evidence for negating the opposition of affirmation and negation from unity and multiplicity. Rather, their interpretation of multiplicity as division absolutely, or into similar parts, and unity as its absence, is evident in establishing that. As for their agreement on negating opposition between them, its meaning is that multiplicity, i.e., number, since it is constituted by units and results from their aggregation, the one aggregated in the counted, there is no opposition between number and one at all. And this is apparent regarding what is part of multiplicity. As for the unity that comes upon multiplicity and nullifies it, such as when you put the waters of jugs into one jug, it might give the impression of their contrariety, based on their both falling upon one subject which is that water, with one of them nullifying the other. The Imam negated this by [arguing] that they are not at the extreme of opposition, and that the subject of each of the transient units which are the multiplicity itself is part of the subject of the supervening unity, not itself. And all of this is weak.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument concerning the relationship between unity (al-wahda) and multiplicity (al-kathra). The author first states a general principle: if something is impossible in existence, it is impossible in truth (statement), and vice versa, but the converse does not hold in the same way. Then he applies this to unity and multiplicity: there is no evidence to deny that they are in opposition of affirmation and negation (i.e., contradictory opposites). However, the author points out that the common interpretation—multiplicity as division and unity as its absence—actually supports the idea that they are logically connected as privation and possession, not true opposites. He then explains the consensus that they are not opposites: multiplicity (number) is constituted by units and results from their aggregation, so number and one are not opposite. This is clear for multiplicity as the sum of units. Then he addresses a possible objection: when a unifying act makes many into one (e.g., pouring waters into one jug), it seems the unity cancels the multiplicity, suggesting opposition. The Imam refutes this by arguing that such unity and multiplicity are not at the extreme of opposition (i.e., true contraries) and that the subject of the transient units (the multiplicity) is part of the subject of the supervening unity, not identical. The author concludes that both of these refutations are weak.

Key Terms
الوحدة
الكثرة
التقابل
الإيجاب والسلب
العدد
الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity and multiplicity are logical opposites like black and white.
Correction: Multiplicity is composed of units, so they are not opposites; they are related as whole to part. The apparent opposition in transformation is not true opposition, and even that case does not establish a logical contradictory relationship.

Checkpoint
What reason does the author give for denying true opposition between unity and multiplicity?
A) Because they are both accidents.
B) Because multiplicity is composed of units, so they are not opposites.
C) Because the Imam said so.
D) Because they cannot coexist.

Key Takeaway
Unity and multiplicity are not opposites because multiplicity is constituted by units; the relationship is one of composition, not contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have many roles in life—father, mother, worker, friend—yet you are a single person. Do you ever feel torn as if these roles are in opposition to each other? How might the truth that multiplicity is composed of unity change the way you approach these aspects of your life, knowing they are not truly opposed but parts of a whole? Commit to seeing one situation today where you felt fragmented and instead recognize the underlying unity of your being.
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you ever feel that focusing on His Oneness (Tawhid) somehow diminishes the importance of the multiple blessings and trials He gives you? The passage shows that the One does not nullify the many but encompasses them. Reflect: Are there specific worldly concerns you have treated as opposed to your faith, when in fact they are part of the same reality? Write down one such concern and remind yourself that it does not threaten the unity of your worship, but is rather a component of it.
?
The objection in the passage deals with a unity that seems to cancel multiplicity. When you dedicate yourself to a single goal in worship (e.g., sincere intention for Allah), do you feel that it negates the multiple actions you perform? How can you see each action as a unit that builds the whole, rather than being erased? Pledge to perform one act of worship today with the awareness that its multiplicity of parts is not in opposition to its unity of intention.

Definition of 'Illa and Its Primary Classification
Al-Taftazani defines 'illa and divides it into internal and external causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defines the term 'cause' ('illa) and classifies it into internal and external causes, along with the subdivisions of each.

Core Idea
The four causes — formal, material, efficient, final — are the key to understanding causation in kalam.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining 'illa as that which a thing needs, but notes that the term often refers specifically to the efficient cause. He then presents a fundamental classification: causes can be either internal or external to the effect. This classification lays the groundwork for understanding how things come into existence and what constitutes their essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'illa
Start by reading the opening lines where al-Taftazani defines 'illa and clarifies its common usage. Note the shift from a general need to a specific meaning.
2
Identify the two main categories
Look for the division into internal and external causes. Internal causes are further divided into formal and material; external into efficient and final.
Remember: internal causes relate to essence, external to existence.
3
Understand the necessity condition
Pay attention to the mention of 'necessity' (wujub) and how it distinguishes actual from potential causes. Also note the objection about non-last parts.
4
Examine the conditions and privative conditions
Read the later part about conditions, instruments, and privative conditions. Understand how a privative condition like removal of an obstacle is considered a cause.
The passage explains that such conditions are in reality revealers of positive conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول تعريف العلة
المادة
:(قال): المحبث الأول : العلة (۱) ما يحتاج إليه الشيء (۲)، وإن كان إطلاقها ينصرف إلى ما يصدر عنه الشيء، ثم إن كانت داخلة في الشيء، فوجوبه معها، إما بالفعل ،فصورية، وإما بالقوة فمادية، ويدخل فيهما الجزء من الصورة (۳) والمادة، وبذكر الوجوب يندفع أن الوجود قد يكون مع بالفعل لا بالقوة، إلا أنه يرد الجزء الغير الأخير من الصورة جمعاً منعاً، إن كانت خارجة، فالشيء إما بها ،ففاعلية أو لها فغائية، ويقال للأولين علة الماهية وللآخرين علة الوجود ومرجع الشروط والآلات إلى الفاعل، ومن الشروط ما هو عدمي كزوال المانع ، ولا يستحيل دخوله في علة الوجود بمعنى أن العقل، إذا لاحظ وجود المعلول، لم يجده حاصلاً بدونه .
الحادث من
وقد يقال : إنه في التحقيق كاشف عن شرط وجودي كزوال الرطوبة لاحتراق الخشبة ينبيء عن اليبوسة التي هي الشرط وليس عدم مباديه إلا بالعرض.
(۱) العلة في اللغة: عرض مؤدي للبنية الجسمية ولما كانت بهذا المعنى سبباً في تأثير الجسم بالأذى وفي اتصافه بذلك الأذى نقلت عرفاً لمطلق ما يكون سبباً في تحقق الشيء فهو عرفاً . (۲) سقط من (أ) و (ب) لفظ (الشيء). (۳) إذ لا يجب حصوله مع ذلك الجزء لصحة حصوله بلا وجود للمعلول وذلك كجزء صورة السرير المقصود حصوله على التربيع مثلاً فإن أحد أضلاعه الأربعة جزء من مجموع ما تحصل به صورته ولا يجب معه السرير لصحة حصوله مع التثليث ولا يجب السرير على ذلك التقدير حتى يحصل مجموع التربيع فيجب بالفعل.
The first chapter: The definition of the cause. The substance: He said: The first chapter: The cause (1) is that which the thing (2) needs, although its unrestricted usage turns to that from which the thing proceeds. Then, if it is internal to the thing, then the necessity of the thing with it is either in actuality, so it is formal, or in potentiality, so it is material; and included in these two is the part from the form (3) and matter. By mentioning necessity, it is repelled that existence may be with it in actuality not in potentiality, except that the non-last part of the form is objected to as a preventing part. If it is external, then the thing is either by it, so it is efficient, or for it, so it is final. And the first two are called cause of the essence, and the last two cause of existence. The conditions and instruments refer back to the efficient. Among the conditions is that which is privative, like the removal of the obstacle, and its entry into the cause of existence is not impossible, meaning that the intellect, when it observes the existence of the effect, does not find it occurring without it. And it may be said that in reality it is a revealer of a positive condition, like the removal of moisture for the burning of wood, indicating the dryness which is the condition, and the absence of its principles is only accidentally. (1) The cause in language: an accident leading to the bodily structure, and since it was in this sense a cause for the body's effect by harm and its being described with that harm, it was transferred conventionally to anything that is a cause for the realization of a thing, so it is conventionally. (2) Dropped from (a) and (b) the word (the thing). (3) Because its occurrence is not necessary with that part for the correctness of its occurrence without existence of the effect, like the part of the form of a bed intended to be obtained as a square; for example, one of its four sides is a part of the sum by which its form is obtained, and the bed is not necessary with it, due to the correctness of its occurrence with a triangle, and the bed is not necessary on that estimation until the entire square is obtained, then it is necessary in actuality.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'illa (cause) and presents the foundational classification into internal and external causes. The author begins by giving the definition: the cause is that which the thing (the effect) needs. However, when the term is used unrestrictedly, it often refers to that from which the thing proceeds, i.e., the efficient cause. The passage then divides causes: if the cause is internal to the effect, then the effect's existence (wujub) is either actual (bi al-fi'l) — making it a formal cause — or potential (bi al-quwwa) — making it a material cause. Both the formal and material causes include the part of the form and the part of matter. The mention of 'necessity' (wujub) is important to avoid the objection that existence may be actual without potentiality. The author then addresses a difficulty: the non-last part of the form is not sufficient for the effect's existence. If the cause is external, then either the effect exists by it (efficient cause) or for it (final cause). The first two (material and formal) are called 'illa al-mahiyya (cause of essence), and the last two (efficient and final) are called 'illa al-wujud (cause of existence). Conditions and instruments are referred back to the efficient cause. Some conditions are privative, like the removal of an obstacle; such can enter into the cause of existence, meaning the intellect does not find the effect without it. An objection is raised: such privative conditions in reality point to a positive condition, e.g., the removal of moisture for burning wood indicates dryness as the real condition, and the absence of the contrary is accidental. The footnote explains the linguistic origin of 'illa: originally an accident affecting the body, then transferred to any cause. Another footnote clarifies a dropped word, and a third gives an example of a part that does not guarantee the whole.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that a privative condition (like removal of an obstacle) is not a cause at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such privative conditions are included as conditions of existence; they are in reality revealers of positive conditions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of causes into formal, material, efficient, and final is essential for understanding both the essence and existence of things in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Taftazani defines cause as 'that which the thing needs'. What do you truly need for your existence to be meaningful? Is it merely the efficient cause (how you came to be) or also the final cause (why you exist)? How will you align your life with that final cause starting today?
?
The passage includes privative conditions (like removal of obstacles) as part of the cause of existence. What specific obstacle is currently preventing you from fulfilling your purpose in worship or learning? What concrete step will you take to remove it by the end of this week?
?

Classification of Causes: Internal and External
Al-Taftazani defines cause and effect and classifies causes into internal (formal, material) and external (efficient, final), distinguishing cause of essence from cause of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's definition of cause and effect, and his fourfold classification of causes into formal, material, efficient, and final, highlighting the distinction between cause of essence and cause of existence.

Core Idea
Causes are either inside the thing or outside; internal causes give essence, external give existence.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining cause and effect, then presents a division of causes based on their relation to the thing. This classification is crucial for understanding causality in Islamic theology and will be the basis for later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of cause and effect
Notice that the author first defines 'cause' as that which the thing needs, and 'effect' as that which needs the thing. He also notes that the term 'cause' when unrestricted often refers to the efficient cause.
This sets the stage for the classification.
2
Identify the internal/external division
The cause is either internal to the thing or external. Internal causes are further divided into formal (actuality) and material (potentiality). External causes are efficient (the thing is by it) and final (for its sake).
Memorize these four: formal, material, efficient, final.
3
Understand cause of essence vs cause of existence
Internal causes (material and formal) are called cause of essence because the thing depends on them in its very essence and existence. External causes are called cause of existence because the thing depends on them only for its existence.
This distinction is important for later arguments.
4
Review intelligibility dependency
The author states that the thing is not intelligible without internal causes, but is intelligible without external causes. This means we can conceive a thing without knowing its efficient or final cause, but not without its form and matter.
Think of a house: you can conceive it without knowing who built it, but you cannot conceive it without its form (structure) and material (bricks).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي قد يراد بالعلة ما يحتاج إليه الشيء. وبالمعلول ما يحتاج إلى الشيء، وإن كانت العلة عند إطلاقها منصرفة إلى الفاعل، وهو ما يصدر عنه الشيء بالاستقلال، أو بانضمام الغير إليه ، ثم علة الشيء أعني ما يحتاج هو (۱) إليه إما أن تكون داخلة فيه أو خارجة عنه فإن كانت داخله فوجوب الشيء معها، إما بالفعل وهي العلة الصورية، وإما بالقوة وهي العلة المادية، وإن كانت خارجة عن الشيء. فإما أن يكون الشيء بها وهي العلة الفاعلية، أو لأجلها العلة الغائية، ويخص الأوليان أعني المادية والصورية باسم علة الماهية ، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في ماهيته (۲) ، كما في وجوده ، ولذا لا يعقل إلا بهما، أو بما ينتزع عنهما كالجنس والفصل(۳)، ويخص الآخر بأنه أعني الفاعلية والغائية باسم علة الوجود، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في الوجود فقط، ولذا يعقل بدونهما، وتمام هذا الكلام ببيان أمور أن لا ذكر في بيان الحصر، وجه ضبط لأنه لا دليل على انحصار الخارج فيما بـه الشيء، وما لأجله الشيء سوى الاستقراء أن المراد بالصورية والمادية وما ينسب إليهما (٤) من الأجزاء لصدق التعريف عليها وكذا في الفاعلية والغائية، وبهذا الاعتبار يندرج الشروط والآلات في الأقسام لكونها راجعة إلى ما به الشيء وما ذهب إليه الإمام من أن الشروط من أجزاء العلة المادية، بناء على أن القابل إنما يكون قابلاً بالفعل معها (٥) ليس بمستقيم، لأنها خارجة عن المعلوم، وقد صرح هو (٦) أيضاً بأن المادية داخلة أن ما ذكرنا من اعتبار الفعل والقوة في الوجوب، وهو الموافق لكلام ابن سينا (۷) أولى من اعتباره في الوجود على ما ذكره الجمهور، لأن المادة إذا لحقها الصورة يكون وجود المعلول معها بالفعل لا
(۱) سقط من (ب) الضمير (هو) .
(۲) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (ماهيته) .
(۳) في (ب) و (ج) وغيره بدلاً من (والفصل) (٤) في (ج) وما ينسب من الأجزاء بدون (إليهما) .
(٥) سقط من (ج) لفظ (معها).
(٦) ليس في (ب) الضمير (هو) .
(۷) راجع ما ذكره ابن سينا في كتابيه الإشارات والنجاة حول هذا الموضوع .
And it (the cause) may be intended as that which the thing needs. And the effect is that which needs the thing, although the cause when unrestricted turns to the efficient, which is that from which the thing proceeds independently or with the addition of another. Then the cause of the thing, meaning that which it (1) needs, is either internal to it or external to it. If it is internal to it, then the necessity of the thing with it is either in actuality, and it is the formal cause, or in potentiality, and it is the material cause. If it is external to the thing, then either the thing is by it, and it is the efficient cause, or for its sake, and it is the final cause. The first two, namely the material and formal, are specifically called cause of the essence, because the thing is dependent on them in its essence (2), as in its existence, and therefore it is not intelligible except by them, or by what is abstracted from them like genus and differentia (3). And the other two, namely the efficient and final, are specifically called cause of existence, because the thing is dependent on them only in existence, and therefore it is intelligible without them. And the completion of this discussion is by explaining matters that are not mentioned in the explanation of the enumeration, a way of verification, because there is no evidence for the exclusiveness of the external to that by which the thing [exists] and that for which the thing [exists] other than induction, that what is meant by the formal and material and what is attributed to them (4) of the parts is for the truth of the definition upon them, and similarly in the efficient and final. And by this consideration, conditions and instruments are included in the divisions because they refer back to that by which the thing [exists]. And what al-Imam held, that conditions are parts of the material cause, based on the idea that the recipient is only actually receptive with them (5), is not correct, because they are external to the effect. And he (6) also stated that the material cause is internal, and that what we mentioned of considering actuality and potentiality in necessity, which agrees with the statement of Ibn Sina (7), is more appropriate than considering it in existence as the majority mentioned, because when the material is joined by the form, the existence of the effect with it is actual, not [potential]. (1) Dropped from (b) the pronoun (he). (2) In (a) with an addition of the word (his essence). (3) In (b) and (c) and others, instead of (and the differentia). (4) In (c): 'and what is attributed of the parts' without (to them). (5) Dropped from (c) the word (with them). (6) Not in (b) the pronoun (he). (7) Refer to what Ibn Sina mentioned in his books al-Isharat and al-Najat on this topic.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed restatement of the definition and classification of causes, and then critiques a particular view of al-Imam (likely al-Ghazali or al-Razi) regarding the inclusion of conditions. The author begins by restating that 'cause' is that which the thing needs, and 'effect' is that which needs the thing. The unrestricted term 'cause' often turns to the efficient cause. The cause is either internal (material or formal) or external (efficient or final). Internal causes are called cause of essence ('illa al-mahiyya) because the thing depends on them in its essence and existence; they are indispensable for intelligibility. External causes are called cause of existence ('illa al-wujud) because the thing depends on them only for existence, and can be conceived without them. The author then notes that the completeness of this discussion requires clarifying matters not mentioned in the initial enumeration. The classification of external causes into efficient and final is based on induction, not demonstration. Conditions and instruments are included under the efficient cause because they refer back to 'that by which the thing [exists]'. The author criticizes al-Imam's view that conditions are parts of the material cause, arguing that conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal. Therefore, it is not correct to consider them as parts of the material cause. The author concludes that the consideration of actuality and potentiality in necessity (wujub) is better than considering them in existence, agreeing with Ibn Sina. When matter receives form, the effect's existence with it is actual, not potential. The footnotes note textual variants and a reference to Ibn Sina.

Key Takeaway
Causes are divided into internal (formal and material) which are cause of essence, and external (efficient and final) which are cause of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe an event, do you instinctively reduce it to an efficient cause (who or what caused it) while neglecting the final cause (why it happened for a purpose)? How might this habit blind you to Allah's wisdom and design in your life? Will you consciously pause today to ask not just 'how' but 'for what purpose'?
?
Your own essence—your soul—has a formal cause (your created nature) and a material cause (your physical body). Are you more focused on the material aspect (body, possessions) than the formal (your purpose as a servant of Allah)? What concrete step will you take today to realign your priorities toward your essence?
?
The ultimate final cause of all existence is Allah. But consider your daily actions: what final cause do you actually live for—pleasure, status, or His pleasure? If you were to die today, would your life's cause be worthy of the One who gave you existence?

Conditions, Instruments, and Critique of al-Imam's View
Al-Taftazani discusses the induction basis of the classification, argues that conditions and instruments belong to the efficient cause, and critiques al-Imam's alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's argument for including conditions and instruments under the efficient cause, and his critique of al-Imam's view that they are part of the material cause.

Core Idea
Conditions are external means that belong to the efficient cause, not the material cause.

After establishing the four causes, al-Taftazani addresses a specific disagreement: where do conditions and instruments fit? He argues they are not part of the material cause as al-Imam claimed, but rather part of the efficient cause. He also clarifies a point about actuality and potentiality in necessity versus existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique context
The author notes that the classification of external causes into efficient and final is based on induction, not demonstration. Then he introduces the issue of conditions and instruments.
Conditions and instruments are things like tools or prerequisites.
2
Understand al-Imam's position
Al-Imam held that conditions are parts of the material cause, because the recipient (matter) becomes actually receptive only when conditions are present.
The material cause is the substrate that receives form.
3
Follow al-Taftazani's counterargument
Al-Taftazani rejects this because conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal. He also notes that al-Imam himself stated that the material cause is internal.
Thus, conditions cannot be part of an internal cause.
4
Understand the final note on actuality and potentiality
Al-Taftazani agrees with Ibn Sina that considering actuality and potentiality in 'necessity' is better than in 'existence', because when matter receives form, the effect's existence is actual, not potential.
This clarifies the terminology used earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي قد يراد بالعلة ما يحتاج إليه الشيء. وبالمعلول ما يحتاج إلى الشيء، وإن كانت العلة عند إطلاقها منصرفة إلى الفاعل، وهو ما يصدر عنه الشيء بالاستقلال، أو بانضمام الغير إليه ، ثم علة الشيء أعني ما يحتاج هو (۱) إليه إما أن تكون داخلة فيه أو خارجة عنه فإن كانت داخله فوجوب الشيء معها، إما بالفعل وهي العلة الصورية، وإما بالقوة وهي العلة المادية، وإن كانت خارجة عن الشيء. فإما أن يكون الشيء بها وهي العلة الفاعلية، أو لأجلها العلة الغائية، ويخص الأوليان أعني المادية والصورية باسم علة الماهية ، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في ماهيته (۲) ، كما في وجوده ، ولذا لا يعقل إلا بهما، أو بما ينتزع عنهما كالجنس والفصل(۳)، ويخص الآخر بأنه أعني الفاعلية والغائية باسم علة الوجود، لأن الشيء يفتقر إليهما في الوجود فقط، ولذا يعقل بدونهما، وتمام هذا الكلام ببيان أمور أن لا ذكر في بيان الحصر، وجه ضبط لأنه لا دليل على انحصار الخارج فيما بـه الشيء، وما لأجله الشيء سوى الاستقراء أن المراد بالصورية والمادية وما ينسب إليهما (٤) من الأجزاء لصدق التعريف عليها وكذا في الفاعلية والغائية، وبهذا الاعتبار يندرج الشروط والآلات في الأقسام لكونها راجعة إلى ما به الشيء وما ذهب إليه الإمام من أن الشروط من أجزاء العلة المادية، بناء على أن القابل إنما يكون قابلاً بالفعل معها (٥) ليس بمستقيم، لأنها خارجة عن المعلوم، وقد صرح هو (٦) أيضاً بأن المادية داخلة أن ما ذكرنا من اعتبار الفعل والقوة في الوجوب، وهو الموافق لكلام ابن سينا (۷) أولى من اعتباره في الوجود على ما ذكره الجمهور، لأن المادة إذا لحقها الصورة يكون وجود المعلول معها بالفعل لا

And it (the cause) may be intended as that which the thing needs. And the effect is that which needs the thing, although the cause when unrestricted turns to the efficient, which is that from which the thing proceeds independently or with the addition of another. Then the cause of the thing, meaning that which it (1) needs, is either internal to it or external to it. If it is internal to it, then the necessity of the thing with it is either in actuality, and it is the formal cause, or in potentiality, and it is the material cause. If it is external to the thing, then either the thing is by it, and it is the efficient cause, or for its sake, and it is the final cause. The first two, namely the material and formal, are specifically called cause of the essence, because the thing is dependent on them in its essence (2), as in its existence, and therefore it is not intelligible except by them, or by what is abstracted from them like genus and differentia (3). And the other two, namely the efficient and final, are specifically called cause of existence, because the thing is dependent on them only in existence, and therefore it is intelligible without them. And the completion of this discussion is by explaining matters that are not mentioned in the explanation of the enumeration, a way of verification, because there is no evidence for the exclusiveness of the external to that by which the thing [exists] and that for which the thing [exists] other than induction, that what is meant by the formal and material and what is attributed to them (4) of the parts is for the truth of the definition upon them, and similarly in the efficient and final. And by this consideration, conditions and instruments are included in the divisions because they refer back to that by which the thing [exists]. And what al-Imam held, that conditions are parts of the material cause, based on the idea that the recipient is only actually receptive with them (5), is not correct, because they are external to the effect. And he (6) also stated that the material cause is internal, and that what we mentioned of considering actuality and potentiality in necessity, which agrees with the statement of Ibn Sina (7), is more appropriate than considering it in existence as the majority mentioned, because when the material is joined by the form, the existence of the effect with it is actual, not [potential]. (1) Dropped from (b) the pronoun (he). (2) In (a) with an addition of the word (his essence). (3) In (b) and (c) and others, instead of (and the differentia). (4) In (c): 'and what is attributed of the parts' without (to them). (5) Dropped from (c) the word (with them). (6) Not in (b) the pronoun (he). (7) Refer to what Ibn Sina mentioned in his books al-Isharat and al-Najat on this topic.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed restatement of the definition and classification of causes, and then critiques a particular view of al-Imam (likely al-Ghazali or al-Razi) regarding the inclusion of conditions. The author begins by restating that 'cause' is that which the thing needs, and 'effect' is that which needs the thing. The unrestricted term 'cause' often turns to the efficient cause. The cause is either internal (material or formal) or external (efficient or final). Internal causes are called cause of essence ('illa al-mahiyya) because the thing depends on them in its essence and existence; they are indispensable for intelligibility. External causes are called cause of existence ('illa al-wujud) because the thing depends on them only for existence, and can be conceived without them. The author then notes that the completeness of this discussion requires clarifying matters not mentioned in the initial enumeration. The classification of external causes into efficient and final is based on induction, not demonstration. Conditions and instruments are included under the efficient cause because they refer back to 'that by which the thing [exists]'. The author criticizes al-Imam's view that conditions are parts of the material cause, arguing that conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal. Therefore, it is not correct to consider them as parts of the material cause. The author concludes that the consideration of actuality and potentiality in necessity (wujub) is better than considering them in existence, agreeing with Ibn Sina. When matter receives form, the effect's existence with it is actual, not potential. The footnotes note textual variants and a reference to Ibn Sina.

Key Terms
الشروط
الآلات
الوجوب
الفعل
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conditions are parts of the material cause because the recipient requires them to be actually receptive.
Correction: Conditions are external to the effect, while the material cause is internal; thus they cannot be parts of the material cause. They belong to the efficient cause.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani reject al-Imam's view that conditions are parts of the material cause?
A) Because conditions are internal to the effect
B) Because conditions are external to the effect
C) Because conditions are efficient causes
D) Because conditions are formal causes

Key Takeaway
Conditions and instruments are part of the efficient cause, not the material cause, because they are external to the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that leads to good, do you unconsciously credit the means (condition/instrument) as if they were part of you, or do you trace them back to the true efficient cause—Allah? How might this lesson's distinction change your daily thankfulness?
?
Al-Taftazani's critique shows that even esteemed scholars can misplace conditions under the material cause. Is there any area in your life where you have 'internalized' a condition—thinking it is essential to your identity—when in reality it is an external means from Allah? What concrete step can you take this week to consciously attribute it to Him?

Refutation of the Second Proof from Contradictories
Author presents and refutes a second proof involving impossibility of contradictories, citing Imam al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second proof from the impossibility of contradictories fails, and how the distinction between modes of predication resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'issuing a' is 'not issuing a', not 'issuing not-a'.

In this passage, the author presents a second proof that from a true one only one effect can proceed—based on the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting. He then reveals the logical error in the proof and refutes even a refined version. By carefully distinguishing between modes of predication, the author shows that multiplicity of issuing acts does not entail logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from proof to refutation.
The passage includes a sarcastic quote from Imam al-Ghazali.
2
Identify the structure
Break down the passage into: presentation of proof, initial refutation, quote from al-Ghazali, refined version, and refutation of refined version.
This helps see how the argument unfolds.
3
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the difference between 'issuing not-a' and 'not issuing a'. The author stresses that these are not equivalent, contrary to what the proof assumes.
This is the core logical error in the original proof.
4
Learn the two modes of predication
The author introduces two modes: ḥaml al-muwāṭaʾa (predication by agreement) and ishtiqāq (predication by derivation). Understand the difference with the example of a sweet white thing.
The refined version fails because it ignores the mode of predication.
5
Apply the distinction
See how the refined proof would only be problematic if the attributes were predicated by agreement, but here they are not. Thus no contradiction arises.
This final step solidifies the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الواحد الحقيقي إذا صدر عنه (أ) فلو صدر عنه (ب) لزم اجتماع النقيضين لأن (ب) ليس (أ) وليس (أ) نقيض (أ) بخلاف ما إذا تعددت الجهة، فإن كلاً من صدور (أ) وليس (أ) يستند إلى جهة، فيكون ما صدر عنه غير ما صدر عنه ليس (أ) فلا يكون تناقضاً، ولما كان فساد هذا الوجه في غاية الظهور، فإن نقيض صدور (أ) عدم صدور (أ) وهو ليس ملازم، وإنما اللازم صدور ما ليس (أ) وهو ليس بنقيض حتى قال الإمام : العجب ممن يفنى عمره في المنطق ليعصمه عن الغلط ، ثم يهمله في مثل هذا المطلب الأعلى فيقع في الغلط الذي يضحك منه الصبيان، قرره بعضهم بأن عدم صدور (أ) صادق على صدور ما ليس (أ) فإذا اجتمع في الواحد صدور (أ) وصدور ما ليس (أ) فقد اجتمع صدور (أ) وعدم صدور (أ) وهما نقيضان، وهذا أيضاً فاسد لأن الممتنع من اجتماع النقيضين هو صدقهما على شيء واحد بطريق حمل المواطأة بأن يصدق على الواحد أنه صدر عنه (أ) ولم يصدر عنه (أ) لا بأن تواجدا فيه، ويحملا عليه بالاشتقاق كالأبيض الحلو الذي يوجد فيه البياض واللابياض الذي هو الحلاوة. وههنا كذلك، لأنه قد وجد في
The second aspect: The true one, if (a) has issued from it, and then (b) were to issue from it, would necessitate the conjunction of the two contradictories (naqīḍayn), because (b) is not (a) and not-(a) is the contradictory of (a). Unlike when the aspect is multiple, for then each of issuing (a) and not issuing (a) is attributed to an aspect, and what issued from it is different from what issued from it of not-(a), so there is no contradiction. Since the corruption of this aspect is extremely apparent, the contradictory of issuing (a) is not issuing (a), and it is not entailed. Rather, what is entailed is issuing that which is not (a), and this is not a contradictory — so much so that the Imām said: 'Wonder at one who spends his life in logic to protect himself from error, then neglects it in such a lofty matter and falls into error that makes children laugh.' Some refined it by saying that not issuing (a) is true of issuing what is not (a); so if there are combined in the one issuing (a) and issuing what is not (a), then issuing (a) and not issuing (a) are combined, and these are two contradictories. This also is flawed, because the impossible in the conjunction of two contradictories is their being true of one thing by the mode of predication of agreement (ḥaml al-muwāṭaʾa), such that it is true of the one that (a) issued from it and (a) did not issue from it — not that they co-exist in it and are predicated of it by derivation (ishtiqāq), like the sweet white thing in which there is whiteness and non-whiteness which is sweetness. And here it is likewise, because there has come to exist in...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is the presentation and refutation of a second proof for the principle that from a true one only one can proceed. The proof is based on the impossibility of contradictories: if a and b are two distinct effects issuing from the same cause, then since b is not a, and not a is the contradictory of a, the cause would be the source of both a and not-a, which is a contradiction. The author immediately points out the flaw: the contradictory of 'issuing (a)' is 'not issuing (a)', not 'issuing not-(a)'. So the proof mistakenly conflates the two. He then quotes Imam al-Ghazālī's sarcastic remark about those who study logic but fail to apply it here. A refined version of the proof is then presented: 'not issuing (a)' is true of 'issuing not-(a)', so if both issuing (a) and issuing not-(a) occur in the same agent, then we have both issuing (a) and not issuing (a), which are contradictories. The author refutes even this version: the rule against contradictories applies only when the two propositions are predicated of the same subject by the mode of agreement (ḥaml al-muwāṭaʾa), i.e., when the same thing is affirmed and denied in the same respect. But here the two attributes (issuing a and issuing not-a) are not in the same respect; they are like the sweet white thing that has whiteness and non-whiteness (sweetness) — these are not contradictories because they are not predicated by agreement but co-exist as different qualifications. The argument is cut off at the end of the page, but the refutation is clear: multiplicity of issuing acts does not entail logical contradiction.

Key Terms
النقيضين
حمل المواطأة
الاشتقاق
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'issuing not-(a)' is logically equivalent to 'not issuing (a)', leading to the false charge of contradiction.
Correction: The author explains that the contradictory of 'issuing a' is 'not issuing a', not 'issuing not-a'. Therefore, the proof incorrectly conflates these.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the refined version of the second proof (combining issuing (a) and issuing not-(a)) still flawed?
A) Because issuing not-(a) is impossible for a true one.
B) Because the conjunction of contradictories is impossible only when predicated by mode of agreement, not when they co-exist as different attributes.
C) Because Imam al-Ghazali rejected it.
D) Because the proof relies on a mistaken notion of causation.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of contradictories applies only when the same predicate is affirmed and denied of the same subject in the same respect, not when different attributes coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been certain that someone's argument was contradictory, only to later realize you had misidentified the opposite of their claim? How will you apply the precision of this lesson to your own thinking when you feel that temptation again?
?
The Imām expressed wonder at those who study logic yet neglect it in lofty matters. As you learn this lesson, what small but powerful logical error in your own reasoning about Allah or causality are you most likely to ignore because it seems too trivial? Will you commit to examining it today?
?
If the true oneness of Allah means that nothing falls outside His knowledge and power, how does your soul react to the possibility that your own logical mistakes might lead you to ascribe contradiction to His actions? What step will you take to guard against that subtle disrespect?
