The Second Face: Contradiction and Copulative Predication
Al-Taftazani analyzes the second face concerning contradiction and predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that proceeding of (A) and (B) from a single principle leads to contradiction fails, and the distinction between copulative and derivative predication.

Core Idea
Coexistence is not contradiction; only copulative predication creates true opposites.

Al-Taftazani continues his analysis of the second face by addressing a common logical error. Critics argue that if a single principle produces both (A) and (B), then opposites must be true. But Taftazani shows that this only works if the predication is copulative, not when attributes coexist by derivation. This lesson unpacks his careful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if from a real one (A) proceeds, then if (B) also proceeds, two opposites would be true because (B) is not (A).
Note the role of the term 'nagid' (opposite).
2
Identify the refutation: multiplicity of aspects
Taftazani counters that when aspects (jiha) are multiple, the contradiction disappears: the proceeding of (A) and not (A) are attributed to different aspects.
This is a key principle in kalam: attribution to different aspects avoids contradiction.
3
Distinguish copulative vs derivative predication
The author explains that the impossible conjunction is only when the two predicates are applied to the same subject in a copulative sense (haml al-muwata'a), not when they coexist as attributes (haml al-ishtiqaq).
Example: white and sweet coexist without contradiction.
4
Apply the distinction to the second face
In the one principle, proceeding of (A) and non-proceeding (which is proceeding of not (A)) coexist, but they do not yield the copulative statement 'proceeded (A) and did not proceed (A)'. So no contradiction.
This refutes the objection.
5
Reflect on the Imam's remark
The Imam's criticism highlights the importance of logical precision even in the highest theological matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الواحد الحقيقي إذا صدر عنه (أ) فلو صدر عنه (ب) لزم اجتماع النقيضين لأن (ب) ليس (أ) وليس (أ) نقيض (أ) بخلاف ما إذا تعددت الجهة، فإن كلاً من صدور (أ) وليس (أ) يستند إلى جهة، فيكون ما صدر عنه غير ما صدر عنه ليس (أ) فلا يكون تناقضاً، ولما كان فساد هذا الوجه في غاية الظهور، فإن نقيض صدور (أ) عدم صدور (أ) وهو ليس ملازم، وإنما اللازم صدور ما ليس (أ) وهو ليس بنقيض حتى قال الإمام : العجب ممن يفنى عمره في المنطق ليعصمه عن الغلط ، ثم يهمله في مثل هذا المطلب الأعلى فيقع في الغلط الذي يضحك منه الصبيان، قرره بعضهم بأن عدم صدور (أ) صادق على صدور ما ليس (أ) فإذا اجتمع في الواحد صدور (أ) وصدور ما ليس (أ) فقد اجتمع صدور (أ) وعدم صدور (أ) وهما نقيضان، وهذا أيضاً فاسد لأن الممتنع من اجتماع النقيضين هو صدقهما على شيء واحد بطريق حمل المواطأة بأن يصدق على الواحد أنه صدر عنه (أ) ولم يصدر عنه (أ) لا بأن تواجدا فيه، ويحملا عليه بالاشتقاق كالأبيض الحلو الذي يوجد فيه البياض واللابياض الذي هو الحلاوة. وههنا كذلك، لأنه قد وجد في الواحد صدور (أ) وعدم صدوره الذي هو صدور ما ليس (أ) ولم يلزم صدق قولنا صدر عنه (أ) ولم يصدر عنه (أ) وكذا تقرير الصحائف، وهو أنه إذا صدر عنه (أ) لم يصدر عنه ليس (أ) لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين فاسد، لأن نقيض قولنا صدر عنه (أ) لم يصدر عنه (أ) لا قولنا صدر عنه ليس (أ) .
The second face: that if from the real one (A) proceeds, then if (B) proceeds from it, the conjunction of two opposites would follow because (B) is not (A), and not (A) is the opposite of (A), unlike when the aspect (jiha) is multiple, for each of the proceeding of (A) and not (A) is attributed to an aspect, so what proceeds from it is other than what proceeds from it not (A), so there is no contradiction. And since the corruption of this face is extremely apparent, for the opposite of the proceeding of (A) is the non-proceeding of (A), and it is not necessary, rather what is necessary is the proceeding of what is not (A), and it is not an opposite. So much so that the Imam said: 'I am amazed at one who spends his life in logic to protect himself from error, then neglects it in such a high matter (al-matalab al-a'la) and falls into error that makes children laugh.' Some of them formulated it as: non-proceeding of (A) is true of the proceeding of what is not (A); so if in the one there is the proceeding of (A) and the proceeding of what is not (A), then there has occurred the proceeding of (A) and the non-proceeding of (A), and these are two opposites. And this is also corrupt, because the impossible in the conjunction of two opposites is their being true of one thing by way of the copulative predication (haml al-muwata'a), such that it is true of the one that (A) proceeded from it and did not proceed from it, not that they co-exist in it and are predicated of it by derivation (ishtiqaq), like the white sweet thing in which whiteness and non-whiteness which is sweetness are found. And here it is similar, because in the one there has occurred the proceeding of (A) and its non-proceeding which is the proceeding of what is not (A), and the truth of our statement 'proceeded from it (A) and did not proceed from it (A)' is not necessitated. And likewise the formulation of the Sahifat: that if (A) proceeds from it, not (A) does not proceed from it due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two opposites, is corrupt, because the opposite of our statement 'proceeded from it (A)' is 'did not proceed from it (A)', not our statement 'proceeded from it not (A)'.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is the second face (al-wajh al-thani) of the argument against the principle. The author presents an objection and its refutation. The objection claims that if from a real one (A) proceeds, then if (B) also proceeds, two opposites would be true: (B) is not (A), and not (A) is opposite of (A). However, the author argues this fails because if the aspects are multiple, the contradiction is avoided: the proceeding of (A) and the proceeding of not (A) are attributed to different aspects, so no real contradiction. The author then criticizes those who misunderstand this, quoting an Imam who ridicules those who neglect logic. He then refutes a specific formulation: that non-proceeding of (A) is true of proceeding of not (A), leading to conjunction of opposites. The author clarifies that the impossibility is in predicating both attributes of the same subject in a copulative sense (haml al-muwata'a), not in the coexistence of the two attributes in the same subject. He gives the example of a white sweet thing where whiteness and non-whiteness (sweetness) coexist without contradiction. Similarly, in the one, the proceeding of (A) and the non-proceeding (which is the proceeding of not (A)) coexist without necessitating the contradictory statement. The formulation in the Sahifat is also rejected because the opposite of 'proceeded (A)' is 'did not proceed (A)', not 'proceeded not (A)'. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
نقيض
جهة
حمل المواطأة
حمل الاشتقاق
صدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That coexistence of two attributes in a subject automatically constitutes contradiction.
Correction: Contradiction requires copulative predication (haml al-muwata'a), not mere coexistence via derivative predication (haml al-ishtiqaq).

Checkpoint
According to the second face, what is required for two statements to be true opposites of each other?
A) Their coexistence in a single subject.
B) Their copulative predication on a single subject.
C) Their derivation from the same attribute.
D) Their attribution to different aspects.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction in predication requires copulative attribution to the same subject; mere coexistence of attributes does not constitute logical opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Allah's attributes—like mercy and justice—do you sometimes assume they are in conflict because they seem opposite? Are you willing to hold them together without contradiction, as the passage teaches about coexistence through different aspects? What might change in your trust in Allah if you truly embraced that?
?
The Imam criticizes those who meticulously guard logic in trivial matters but neglect it in the highest matters. Where in your own life do you apply careful reasoning to worldly affairs but abandon it when thinking about Allah, Qadr, or the afterlife? What would it cost you to bring the same rigor to your faith?
?
If the opposite of 'Allah willed good for you' is only 'Allah did not will good for you', not 'Allah willed evil for you', how does that reshape your understanding of trials? Are you ready to accept that not everything that seems contrary to your desire is actually the opposite of divine goodness? How does that challenge your reactions to hardship?

First Objection: Corporeality Necessitates Multiple Attributes
Al-Taftazani presents the first objection from corporeality against the principle of the one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection raised against the kalam principle 'from the one only one proceeds' based on the nature of corporeality.

Core Idea
Corporeality itself is a single essence that necessitates multiple things, challenging the principle.

This lesson presents the first of several objections against a famous principle in Islamic theology that from the One only one can proceed. The objection focuses on the concept of corporeality: if corporeality is a single attribute, how can it necessitate both spatial occupation and receptivity to accidents? This seems to contradict the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: it introduces the objection to the principle, then states the first objection regarding corporeality.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the challenged principle
Recall the principle that 'from the one only one proceeds'. This means a single simple cause can only produce one effect directly. The objection attempts to find a counterexample.
3
Identify the two necessities
Corporeality is said to necessitate (taqtadi) two things: tahayyuz (occupation of space) and qubul al-a'rad (reception of accidents), or at least the capability for both. Identify these two.
The phrase 'la aqal' means 'at the very least'.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: how does this challenge the principle? If corporeality is a single essence, it should give rise to only one thing, but it gives rise to two or more necessary attributes.
5
Note the incomplete response
This passage only presents the objection; the response from the defenders of the principle is not included in this lesson. Keep that in mind.
The answer will be covered in future lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معارضة القول بأن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد. قال : ثم عورضت بوجوه : الأول : أن الجسمية تقتضي التحيز، وقبول الأعراض أو قابليته لهما لا أقل
Counter-argument to the statement that from the one only one proceeds. He said: Then it was objected in several ways: The first: that corporeality (al-jismiyya) necessitates occupation of space (tahayyuz) and reception of accidents (qubul al-a'rad) or its capability for both, at the very least.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage introduces a counter-argument (mu'arada) against the principle 'from the one only one proceeds', listing several objections. The first objection is about corporeality (al-jismiyya). The author states that corporeality, according to the opponents' school, necessitates two things: occupation of space (tahayyuz) and reception of accidents (qubul al-a'rad), or at least the capability for both. This objection aims to show that from a single essence (corporeality) multiple necessary concomitants arise, thus contradicting the principle. The passage only presents the objection without the response in this window.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that corporeality is a simple attribute that entails only one thing, or that the objection is easily dismissed.
Correction: The objection is serious because it shows that even a basic attribute like corporeality gives rise to multiple necessities, challenging the universality of the principle 'from one only one'.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what does corporeality necessitate?
A) Only occupation of space.
B) Only reception of accidents.
C) Both occupation of space and reception of accidents or at least capability for both.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that even a simple attribute like corporeality necessitates multiple things, thereby challenging the principle that from one only one proceeds.

?

Objection on Circularity in Negations and Relations Resolved
Al-Taftazani rebuts an objection by distinguishing conceptual from existential dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why judgments about negations and relations require the prior existence of the entity negated or related, and how Al-Taftazani resolves an objection about circularity by distinguishing conceptual from existential dependency.

Core Idea
Negations and relations depend on reality, not just concepts.

In this passage, Al-Taftazani addresses a subtle objection. The objector claims that judgments about negations and relations involve a harmless conceptual dependency, not a problematic existential one. But Al-Taftazani insists that to judge something as negated or related in reality, that thing must actually exist. Let's see how he resolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objector's claim: conceptual apprehension of negations/relations depends only on the concept of the other, not on its existence. Thus no circularity.
2
Find the answer
Al-Taftazani says: a valid judgment of negation/relation in reality requires the existence of the other (negated or related entity), because negation requires something negated, and relation requires something related.
3
Distinguish the two dependencies
The objector conflates conceptual dependency (needing to understand the other) with existential dependency (needing the other to exist). The answer clarifies that existential dependency is what matters for real judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن تعقلها إنما يتوقف على تعقل الغير لا على ثبوته ، والمتوقف عليها ثبوت الغير لا تعقله فلا دور والجواب : أن المراد أنه لا يصح الحكم بالسلوب والإضافات في نفس الأمر إلا بعد ثبوت الغير ضرورة اقتضاء السلب مسلوباً، وبالاضافة منسوباً، فلا يصح الحكم باستناد ثبوته إليها للزوم الدور.
And it was objected that the conceptual apprehension of them (negations and relations) depends only on the conceptual apprehension of the other, not on its existence, and that on which the existence of the other depends is its conceptual apprehension, so there is no circularity. The answer: that what is meant is that it is not valid to judge negations and relations in reality except after the existence of the other, necessarily because negation requires something negated, and relation requires something related; so it is not valid to judge that its existence is based on them due to the necessitation of circularity.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the dependency of judgments about negations and relations on the existence of something else. The objector argues that conceptual apprehension (taʿaqqul) depends on concept, not existence, and that the existence of the other depends on concept, not the reverse, so there is no circularity. The author (Al-Taftazani) responds by clarifying that in judgments about reality (fī nafs al-amr), valid judgment of negation or relation requires the prior existence of the negated or related entity; otherwise, the judgment would be meaningless. The alleged circularity arises from conflating conceptual and existential dependency: existence must precede the judgment, not vice versa.

Key Terms

ثبوت (existence)
سلب (negation)
إضافة (relation)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if A depends on B and B depends on A, that is necessarily circular, but the author shows the dependency is of different kinds (conceptual vs. existential).
Correction: The objector's position conflates conceptual and existential dependency. Al-Taftazani shows that for real judgments, existential dependency is required, and that does not create a circle because the existence of the other must precede the judgment.

Checkpoint

A) The difference between conceptual and existential dependency
B) The difference between necessary and contingent existence
C) The difference between negation and relation
D) The difference between agent and recipient

Key Takeaway
Judgments about negations and relations in reality require the actual existence of the negated or related entity, not merely its concept.

Defending Rational Proofs Against the Objection of Specification
Taftazani answers the objection that specifying rational proofs invalidates them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objection that specifying a rational proof to certain cases invalidates it, by distinguishing between imagination and reality and showing that intellectual judgment suffices.

Core Idea
Specification does not invalidate rational proofs; it merely limits them to what is real.

This passage presents a sharp objection: if rational proofs are only valid in some cases, aren't they essentially admitting their own failure? Taftazani responds by clarifying what specification actually means, then offers two perspectives—one from Islamic theology and one from philosophy—before concluding with the truth. Understanding this argument is key to grasping how rational proofs are defended in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
The objector claims that specification admits invalidity. Notice the charge: if the proof's conclusion fails in some cases, the proof is invalid.
2
Understand the Initial Answer
Taftazani clarifies that specification means the proof does not apply in the specific case (صورة النص), not that it is invalid overall.
3
Explore the Two Perspectives
From 'our' view (Islamic), application to what has no reality is imaginary; from 'their' view (philosophers), application requires real order. See how each leads to different conclusions about which things the proof applies to.
4
Grasp the Truth
The proof is intellectual, not external. If the intellect's judgment that correspondence must occur or not is sufficient, the proof applies universally—even to numbers and successive existents.
5
Reflect on Implications
This defense allows rational proofs to be used in theology despite apparent counterexamples, as long as the intellect can suppose the correspondence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : التخصيص في الأدلة العقلية، اعتراف ببطلانها حيث يتخلف المدلول عنها . قلنا : معناه أن الدليل لا يجري في صورة النص، بل يختص بما عداها . أما عندنا فنظراً إلى أن ما لا تحقق له في نفس الأمر لا يمكن التطبيق فيه إلا بمجرد الوهم فينقطع بانقطاعه بخلاف ما في نفس الأمر، فإنه لا بد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء جزء، أو لا يقع، وهو معنى الانقطاع، وأما عندهم فنظراً إلى أن التطبيق بحسب نفس الأمر، إنما يتصور فيما له مع الوجود ترتب ليوجد بإزاء تلك، فلا يجري في الأعداد، ولا في الحركات الفلكية، ولا في النفوس الناطقة والحق أن تحصيل الجملتين من هذه سلسلة كل جزء من تلك، إنما هو بحسب العقل دون الخارج، فإن كفى في تمام الدليل حكم العقل، بأنه لا بد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء جزء أو لا يقع . فالدليل جار في الأعداد وفي الموجودات المتعاقبة، والمجتمعة المترتبة وغير المترتبة، لأن للعقل أن يفرض ذلك في الكل، وإن لم يكن ذلك، بل اشتراط ملاحظة أجزاء الجملتين على التفصيل لم يتم الدليل في الموجودات المترتبة فضلا عما عداها لأنه لا سبيل للعقل إلى ذلك إلا فيما لا يتناهى من الزمان .
If it is said: specification in rational proofs is an admission of their invalidity since the indicated [conclusion] fails. We say: Its meaning is that the proof does not apply in the specific case, but rather is restricted to what is outside it. As for in our view, because what has no actuality in reality cannot be applied except by mere imagination, so it is cut off by its being cut off, unlike what is in reality, for there it must either correspond to every part [with a part] or not correspond, and that is the meaning of termination. And as for in their view, because application according to reality is only conceivable for something that has order with existence so that there exists corresponding to those [parts], so it does not apply to numbers, nor to celestial movements, nor to rational souls. And the truth is that establishing the two series from this chain, each part from that, is only according to the intellect, not externally. So if the intellect’s judgment that there must either correspond to every part [with a part] or not correspond suffices for the completeness of the proof, then the proof applies to numbers, to successive existents, and to simultaneous ordered and unordered [ones], because the intellect can suppose that in all, even if that is not [actual]; rather, stipulating detailed observation of the parts of the two series does not complete the proof in ordered existents, let alone what is outside them, because the intellect has no way to that except in what is infinite in time.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if a rational proof (دليل عقلي) is specified (تخصيص) to only apply in certain cases, that amounts to admitting its invalidity because the conclusion fails in some instances. The author (Taftazani) responds by clarifying that specification means the proof does not apply in the specific case (صورة النص) but is limited to others. He then explains the rationale from two perspectives: From 'our' (Islamic) view, what has no actual reality cannot be applied except by imagination, so the proof's termination is merely imaginary; in true reality, correspondence either occurs or not, which is the real meaning of termination. From 'their' (philosophers') view, application according to reality only works for things that have a real order with existence, so it does not apply to numbers, celestial movements, or rational souls. Finally, the author states the truth: establishing the two series is an intellectual exercise, not external. If the intellect's judgment suffices, the proof applies universally—to numbers, successive existents, and all types of ordered or unordered things—because the intellect can suppose the correspondence. Only if detailed observation of each part were required would the proof fail, but the intellect cannot do that except in an infinite time span.

أدلة عقلية
تطبيق

عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs are invalid if they have any exceptions or specifications.
Correction: Specification does not invalidate a rational proof; it only means the proof does not apply in a particular case, but remains valid in other cases.

Checkpoint

A) Because they exist actually and are ordered.
B) Because the intellect's judgment that correspondence must occur or not is sufficient.
C) Because numbers are infinite and thus comparable.

Key Takeaway
The defense of rational proofs against the specification objection rests on the distinction between intellectual application and external reality.

Matter and Form: Substrate and Agent
Defines matter and form and their causal relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of matter and form and their asymmetrical causal relationship, where matter is a passive substrate and form is an active principle.

Core Idea
Matter is the passive substrate; form is the active agent.

In Islamic philosophical theology, understanding the relationship between matter and form is key to analyzing composite beings. The author defines matter as a locus and form as an agent, explaining why matter cannot exist without form but can persist through changes of form. This lesson unpacks that relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: مادة, صورة, محل, قابل, حامل, فاعل.
2
Identify the definitions
Matter is described as a locus, recipient, and bearer for form. Form is described as an agent or part of an agent for matter. Note that these roles are relative to each other.
Think of a lump of clay (matter) and the shape of a pot (form).
3
Understand the relativity
The passage explains that matter and form are only called such by reference to a composite or to each other. They are not absolute substances.
A thing might be matter relative to a higher composition or form relative to a lower one.
4
Grasp the causal asymmetry
Matter needs form for its existence, but it does not need a particular form—it persists when a particular form ceases. Form, being universal, cannot be a separate cause for a particular matter. Matter's role is pure receptivity, not action.
This is why matter is not considered part of the agent for form.
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this sets the stage for understanding composite substances: matter provides the potential, form actualizes it. This is foundational in Islamic natural philosophy.
This distinction influences later discussions on the soul and the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل، والصورة لها فاعل أو جزء فاعل. لما كانت الجزئية معتبرة في مفهومي المادة والصورة لم يكونا مادة وصورة إلا باعتبار الإضافة إلى المركب منهما وإما باعتبار إضافة كل منهما إلى الأخرى، فالمادة محل وقابل وحامل للصورة والصورة جزء فاعل لها بمعنى أن فيضان وجود المادة عن الفاعل يكون بإعانة من الصورة ضرورة احتياج المادة إليها مع امتناع استقلالها بالعلية لأن المادة إنما تحتاج إلى الصورة من حيث هي صورة، وإلا من حيث هي تلك الصورة المعينة ضرورة بقائها عند انعدام الصورة المعينة والصورة من حيث هي صورة مالا تكون واحدة بالعدد فلا يمكن أن تكون علة مستقلة للمادة الواحدة بالعدد، وإنما لم يجعلوا المادة جزء فاعل للصورة بناء على احتياج الصورة إليها لما تقرر عندهم من أن شأن المادة القبول لا الفعل.
Matter for form is a locus, a recipient, and a bearer; and form for it is an agent or part of an agent. Since particularity is considered in the concepts of matter and form, they are not matter and form except by the addition relative to the composite from them, or by the addition of each of them to the other. So matter is a locus, a recipient, and a bearer for form, and form is a part of the agent for it, meaning that the emanation of the existence of matter from the agent occurs with the assistance of form, due to the necessity of matter's need for it, along with the impossibility of its independent causality. For matter only needs form insofar as it is form, not insofar as it is that particular form, due to the necessity of its persistence upon the annihilation of the particular form. And form insofar as it is form is not numerically one, so it cannot be an independent cause for numerically one matter. And they did not make matter a part of the agent for form due to form's need for it, according to what is established with them that the attribute of matter is receptivity, not action.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between matter (mādda) and form (ṣūra) in the composite. It states that matter serves as a locus (maḥall), a recipient (qābil), and a bearer (ḥāmil) for form, while form is an agent (fāʿil) or part of an agent (juzʾ fāʿil) for matter. The author explains that the concepts of matter and form are relative: they are only called matter and form by virtue of their addition to the composite or to each other. The causal dependency is one-way: matter needs form in its coming-to-be, but not as a particular form, because matter persists even when a particular form ceases. Form, being universal, cannot be a numerically independent cause for a particular matter. The author notes that matter is not considered part of the agent for form because matter's role is passive reception, not action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter and form are independent, absolute substances that can exist separately.
Correction: Matter and form are relative concepts: they are only defined by their relation to each other and to the composite. Matter persists when a particular form ceases, showing it does not depend on that specific form.

Key Takeaway
In a composite, matter is the passive substrate that receives form, while form is the active principle that actualizes matter, and their roles are defined relative to each other.

Matter Cannot Be Constituted by Two Co-Equal Forms
Proves impossibility of two co-equal forms and permits hierarchical forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why matter cannot have two constitutive forms at the same rank and how hierarchical forms are possible.

Core Idea
Forms at the same rank compete; only hierarchical forms can coexist in matter.

This passage from the Kalam tradition establishes a crucial principle about the constitution of material substances. It proves that matter cannot be constituted by two forms that are co-equal, because they would either exclude each other or collapse into a single composite. However, it allows for forms at different levels, like the corporeal and specific forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'تقوم' and 'مقوم'.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage gives two proofs why two forms cannot be co-equal: one based on independence (استقلال) and one based on combination (اجتماع). Find and understand each.
The first argument says each form would make the other unnecessary; the second says the sum becomes a single constitutive.
3
Understand the exception
The passage then explains that forms in different degrees (درجتين) are allowed, like the corporeal form (صورة جسمية) and specific form (صورة نوعية). See how the hierarchy works.
Matter needs the corporeal form, which itself needs the specific form, so the need is indirect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تتقوم المادة بصورتين في درجة، إما بطريق الاستقلال بأن يكون كل منهما مقوماً فظاهر لأن تقومها لكل منهما يستلزم عن الأخرى، وإما بطريق الاجتماع فلأن المقوم حينئذ يكون هو المجموع إلى كل واحد والمجموع أمر واحد ويجوز تقوم المادة بصورتين في درجتين كالصورة الجسمية والنوعية للمادة بمعنى أنها تفتقر في وجودها إلى الصورة الجسمية المفتقرة إلى الصورة النوعية فيقع افتقار المادة إليها في الدرجة الثانية.
And matter is not constituted by two forms in [one] degree, either independently, that each is a constitutive, then it is apparent, because its constitution by each necessitates independence from the other; or by combination, because the constitutive then is the sum with respect to each one, and the sum is one thing. And it is permissible for matter to be constituted by two forms in two degrees, like the corporeal form and the specific form for matter, meaning that it is in need in its existence of the corporeal form which is in need of the specific form, so matter's need for it occurs in the second degree.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the rule that matter cannot be constituted by two forms at the same rank (degree). The author proves this in two ways: first, if the two forms were each constitutive independently, then matter's constitution by one would necessarily exclude the other; second, if the two forms composed matter jointly, then the constitutive would be the sum of both, which is a single thing. Therefore, matter can only have two forms if they are in different ranks, like the corporeal form (jismiyya) and the specific form (nawʿiyya). In that case, matter first receives the corporeal form which itself depends on the specific form, so the dependency of matter on the specific form is indirect, at a second level.

Key Terms
تقوم
مقوم
استقلال
اجتماع
درجة
صورة جسمية
صورة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter can have multiple forms at the same rank.
Correction: Matter can have multiple forms only if they are hierarchically ordered; co-equal forms are impossible because they would either exclude each other or collapse into a single constitutive.

Checkpoint
Why can matter not be constituted by two forms in the same degree according to the passage?
A) Because forms are immaterial.
B) Because if independent, each would exclude the other; if joint, the constitutive would be a single sum.
C) Because matter cannot have any form.
D) Because forms are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Matter cannot have two constitutive forms at the same rank; forms must be hierarchically ordered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson's principle of hierarchical order call you to subordinate the lesser to the greater, and what is your specific form?

How will you reorder them so that one depends on the other, as the corporeal depends on the specific?

Objection and Answer on Circularity Between Matter and Form
Refutes the charge of circularity between matter and form in causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the apparent circularity between matter and form and how the answer resolves it by distinguishing between receptive and agentive causation.

Core Idea
Interdependence is not circular when the modes of dependency differ.

This passage presents a classic objection in Islamic metaphysics: if form needs matter for individuation, and matter needs form for individuation, does that not create a vicious circle? The objection claims that this circularity prevents form from being part of its agent. The answer carefully distinguishes between two perspectives—reception and agency—showing that the interdependence is not circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim: because form requires matter, it cannot be part of matter's agent due to circularity. Note the perceived circularity: form is individuated by matter, and matter is individuated by form.
The objection assumes a single mode of dependence.
2
Examine the answer's distinction
The answer breaks the circle by noting that form's individuation is from matter as a recipient, while matter's individuation is from form as an agent. These are different causal roles.
Key terms: قابل (recipient) vs. فاعل (agent).
3
Synthesize the resolution
Reflect on how the distinction resolves the charge of circularity. The interdependence is not vicious because it is from different perspectives.
This is a standard move in kalam to avoid logical pitfalls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لما احتاجت الصورة إلى المادة امتنع كونها جزءاً من فاعلها للزوم الدور لما زعموا أن تشخص الصورة يكون بالمحل المعين، ومن حيث هو قابل لتشخصها وتشخص المحل يكون بالصورة المطلقة، ومن حيث هي فاعل لتشخصه فلا دور.
For if it is said: Since form is in need of matter, it is impossible for it to be a part of its agent due to the necessity of circularity, because they claim that the individuation of form is by the particular locus, and insofar as it is a recipient for its individuation, and the individuation of the locus is by absolute form, and insofar as it is an agent for its individuation, then there is no circularity.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the relationship between matter and form. The objection argues: if form needs matter, then form cannot be a part of matter's agent, because that would lead to circularity: form's individuation comes from matter, and matter's individuation comes from form. The answer responds that there is no circularity because the two individuations are from different perspectives: form's individuation is from the particular locus (matter) as a recipient, while matter's individuation is from form as an agent. Since the functions differ (reception vs. agency), the interdependence is not viciously circular.

Key Terms
دور
تشخص
محل معين
صورة مطلقة
قابل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any interdependence between matter and form is necessarily a vicious circle.
Correction: Interdependence is not vicious when the modes of dependence differ: one is receptive, the other agentive.

Checkpoint
What is the apparent circularity that the objection raises?
A) That matter needs form but form does not need matter.
B) That form is individuated by matter and matter is individuated by form.
C) That both matter and form are eternal.
D) That form is the agent of matter and matter is the agent of form.

Key Takeaway
The apparent circularity between matter and form is resolved by noting that form individuates matter as an agent, while matter individuates form as a recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim of circular reasoning, do you carefully examine whether the modes of dependency are actually the same, or do you accept the accusation at face value? How can this lesson's precise distinction between 'agent' and 'recipient' help you avoid such errors in your own thinking?
?
Consider a conflict or disagreement you've had where two things seemed mutually dependent. Did you stop to analyze whether one side acts as an agent and the other as a recipient, or did you assume a vicious circle? How might this lesson's approach change the way you handle such situations in the future?

Form as Configuration in Artefacts
Explains that form in artefacts is a configuration inhering in matter, not a new substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in artefacts, form is not a new substance but a configuration (hay'a) inhering in matter, and that the particular individual form necessitates the particular composite, not the type.

Core Idea
Form in artefacts is an accident, not a substance.

This passage from al-Istighnā clarifies a key distinction in kalam philosophy: when an artisan makes a sword, he does not create new substances but imposes a configuration on pre-existing matter. The passage addresses an important objection about the relationship between form and actual functionality, and it refines our understanding of form as a particular accident rather than a general type.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses analogies like whiteness and body to illustrate.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'hay'a' (configuration), 'jawhar' (substance), and 'arad' (accident). Understand how they relate in the passage.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says that a stone with a sword-shape is not actually a sword. The answer distinguishes between the general type of form and the particular individual form. Grasp this distinction.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Ask yourself: why does this matter? It preserves the idea that form in artefacts is an accident, not a new substance, and that particularity links form to the composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستغناء قال: وقد يقال لكل هيئة في قابل وجداني بالذات أو بالاعتبار والمادة لمحلها كالبياض والجسم ويشبه أن يكون مثل السيف، والسرير من هذا القبيل، إذ الصانع لم يحدث فيه جواهر بل هيئة وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الهيئة السيفية، ليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل كما في الحجر، وأما جواب إمام بأنا لا نعني أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب بل إن الصورة الشخصية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادة الشخصية فيشعر بأن الصورة ههنا بالمعنى السابق على أن السيف مثلا اسم للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الجوهر والعارض الذي هو الهيئة.
Al-Istighnā said: And it is said for every configuration in a recipient, perceptible essentially or by consideration, and matter for its locus, like whiteness and body. And it resembles such as the sword and the bed being of this kind, since the artisan did not produce in them substances but a configuration. Then the objection does not apply that the sword-configuration is not that by which the sword is actually entailed, as in stone. But the answer of an imam is that we do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular sword-form, for example, necessitates that sword, unlike its particular matter. So it suggests that form here is in the previous sense, and that 'sword' for example is a name for the composite of the substrate which is the substance and the accident which is the configuration.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the notion of form as a configuration (hay'a) in artefacts. It notes that for every configuration in a recipient, whether perceptible or conceptual, matter is its locus, as whiteness is to body. Artefacts like swords and beds are similar: the artisan does not create new substances but only a configuration. The passage then addresses an objection: the sword-configuration does not necessarily entail an actual sword (e.g., a stone might have a similar shape but not function as a sword). The answer clarifies that it is not the general type of form but the particular individualized form (e.g., this specific sword-form) that necessitates the particular composite (this sword). Thus, form in artefacts is understood in the same sense as before—as an accident (ʿaraḍ) in a substance (jawhar), and 'sword' is the name of the composite of the two.

Misconception: Form in artefacts is a new substance added to the material.
Correction: Form in artefacts is a configuration (accident) inhering in pre-existing matter, not a new substance.

Checkpoint

A) It says form is always substantial.
B) It says the particular individual form necessitates the particular composite, not the type.
C) It says form is independent of matter.
D) It says artefacts have no form.

Key Takeaway
Form in artefacts is a particular configuration (accident) that necessitates the particular composite, not a general type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you treat your possessions as mere configurations, or do you attach false substance to them? Consider an item you treasure—if its form is just an accident, to what extent does your attachment reflect a confusion between substance and accident? Challenge yourself to see the artefact as a composite of matter and accident, not as an independent substance. How does this shift your perspective on ownership and attachment?
?
The passage distinguishes between type and particular form. Think of a specific object you use daily. Is your relationship with it based on its general type or its particular individuality? What does that reveal about your perception of reality? Will you change how you interact with that object?
?
If an artisan does not create new substances but only configurations, then human creativity is about arrangement, not creation ex nihilo. How does this humble your own sense of creative power? Do you recognize that all you do is rearrange what Allah has already created? Let this recognition lead you to gratitude and humility.

Objection and Answer on Form's Need for Matter
Objection and answer on form's need for matter and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity regarding form's need for matter is answered by distinguishing different modes and degrees of constitution.

Core Idea
Form's need for matter does not cause circularity if constitution occurs at different degrees.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection and answer concerning the relationship between form and matter in composition. The objector claims that if form needs matter, then form cannot be part of the agent because of circularity. The author refutes this by carefully analyzing the modes of constitution and showing that circularity is avoided when forms operate at different degrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Identify the objector's claim that form's need for matter leads to circularity.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' introduces the objection.
2
Understand the answer's first part: denial of circularity
The author responds by saying 'there is no circularity'. Why? Because the individuation of form and matter can be explained without reciprocal need.
The key is that form's need for matter is not at the same level.
3
Examine the two modes of constitution
The author discusses two ways matter could be constituted by two forms: independently (each form as a separate constituent) or by combination (the aggregate as constituent). The first is impossible, the second is possible.
This is about the relationship between forms and matter.
4
Learn about the two degrees of dependence
Matter may depend on forms in two degrees: directly on a corporeal form, and indirectly on a specific form through the corporeal form. This avoids circularity because the dependence is not mutual at the same level.
Example: matter → corporeal form → specific form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لما احتاجت الصورة إلى المادة امتنع كونها جزءاً من فاعلها للزوم الدور لما زعموا أن تشخص الصورة يكون بالمحل المعين، ومن حيث هو قابل لتشخصها وتشخص المحل يكون بالصورة المطلقة، ومن حيث هي فاعل لتشخصه فلا دور ولا تتقوم المادة بصورتين في درجة، إما بطريق الاستقلال بأن يكون كل منهما مقوماً فظاهر لأن تقومها لكل منهما يستلزم عن الأخرى، وإما بطريق الاجتماع فلأن المقوم حينئذ يكون هو المجموع إلى كل واحد والمجموع أمر واحد ويجوز تقوم المادة بصورتين في درجتين كالصورة الجسمية والنوعية للمادة بمعنى أنها تفتقر في وجودها إلى الصورة الجسمية المفتقرة إلى الصورة النوعية فيقع افتقار المادة إليها في الدرجة الثانية.
If it is said: Since form needs matter, it is impossible for it to be a part of its agent because of circularity, because they claim that the individuation of form is by the specific locus, and insofar as it is receptive to its individuation, and the individuation of the locus is by the absolute form, and insofar as it is an agent for its individuation, there is no circularity. And matter is not constituted by two forms in one degree; either independently—where each is a constituent, then it is obvious because its being constituted by each entails independence from the other—or by combination, because then the constituent is the aggregate, and the aggregate is one thing, and it is possible for matter to be constituted by two forms in two degrees, like the corporeal and specific forms for matter, meaning that it depends in its existence on the corporeal form, which depends on the specific form, so matter's dependence on them occurs in the second degree.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the composition of form and matter. The objector argues that if form requires matter, then form cannot be a part of its own agent (the composite) because that would lead to circularity: form is individuated by matter, and matter is individuated by form. The answer denies circularity by explaining that matter is not constituted by two forms in the same degree. The author then distinguishes two modes: independent constitution (where each form is a constituent independently, which is impossible) and combined constitution (where the aggregate is the constituent, which is possible). He further allows two degrees: matter depends on a corporeal form, which in turn depends on a specific form, so matter's dependence on the specific form is indirect (second degree). This avoids circularity.

Key Terms
صورة
مادة
دور
تشخص
مقوم
درجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any need of form for matter implies circularity.
Correction: Circularity is avoided when the dependence is at different degrees (e.g., matter depends on form indirectly through an intermediary).

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, in how many ways can matter be constituted by two forms?
A) One way: independently.
B) Two ways: independently and by combination.
C) Three ways: independently, by combination, and by degrees.
D) Only by combination.

Key Takeaway
The need of form for matter does not entail circularity, because form can be a constituent at a different degree from matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your life where you rely on circular justifications—'I do this because I am this way, and I am this way because I do this'—rather than accepting a deeper, non-circular foundation? What would it cost you to break that cycle by acknowledging a higher source?
?
When you examine your relationship with God, do you ever fall into the trap of expecting reciprocation in the same degree, as if your dependence and His mercy are on the same level? How does realizing the 'different degrees' of dependence free you from expecting a transactional relationship with your Creator?
?
Consider a belief you hold about yourself that seems to be caught in a loop: 'I am not good enough because I fail, and I fail because I am not good enough.' Could this be a misplaced certainty about a single degree of constitution? What would change if you allowed yourself to see your flaws as part of a larger, ordered structure of growth rather than a static circular identity?

Form and Matter in Artificial Composites
Defines form and matter in artificial composites with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of form and matter as used in artificial composites, where form is an accident and matter is a substance.

Core Idea
In artifacts, form is a configuration that is an accident; matter is its underlying substance.

Classical theologians distinguished between natural and artificial composites. In artificial objects like a bed or sword, the form is not a substance but an accident added to pre-existing matter. This passage lays out that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the technical terms like 'hay'a' (configuration) and 'arad' (accident).
2
Identify the definitions of form and matter
The passage defines form as the configuration in a substrate, and matter as the locus of that configuration. Compare with the example of whiteness in body.
Form and matter here are used homonymously with the natural sense.
3
Examine the example of the bed
The bed-form produced by the carpenter is an accident subsisting in the planks. This clarifies that in artifacts, form is not a substance.
4
Understand why this avoids the sword objection
The passage briefly mentions that this definition sidesteps the objection that the sword-form does not necessitate the sword. This will be elaborated in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستغناء. قال: وقد يقال لكل هيئة في قابل وجداني بالذات أو بالاعتبار والمادة لمحلها كالبياض والجسم ويشبه أن يكون مثل السيف والسرير من هذا القبيل، إذ الصانع لم يحدث فيه جواهر بل هيئة وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الهيئة السيفية ليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل كما في الحجر. وأما جواب الإمام بأنا لا نعني أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب بل إن الصورة الشخصية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادة الشخصية فيشعر بأن الصورة ههنا بالمعنى السابق على أن السيف مثلا اسم للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الجوهر والعارض الذي هو الهيئة. كل من الصورة والمادة يقال بالاشتراك بمعنى غير ما سبق، فالصورة الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر. والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.
Independence. He said: And it may be said that every configuration (hay'a) in a substrate is found either essentially or by consideration, and matter is its locus, like whiteness and body. And it seems that things like the sword and bed are of this type, since the artisan did not create substances in them but only a configuration. Then the objection does not arise that the sword-configuration is not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, as in stone. As for the answer of the Imam (al-Razi), that we do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter—this indicates that form here is in the previous sense, that the sword, for example, is a name for the composite of the substrate (jawhar) and the accident ('arad) which is the configuration. Both 'form' and 'matter' are used homonymously in a different sense from before. Form is the configuration occurring in something receptive that has unity essentially or by consideration, and matter is the locus of that configuration, like whiteness and body. In this respect, it is correct to attribute each to the other. It appears that the application of form and matter in artificial composites like the sword, bed, and house is in this sense, because the configuration that the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance indwelling them. Similarly for the form of the sword and house. On this basis, the objection of the Imam against the interpretation of the formal cause is repelled—that the sword-configuration is a form for the sword but not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, since it may exist in wood or stone without a sword. The Imam answered: We do not mean by the necessity of the composite with the form that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular wooden? sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter, which does not necessitate it; indeed, the same matter may be matter for something else. And the form occurring in stone is not identical to the form existing in iron but only in type. This indicates that the meaning of form in artificial composites is also the part with which the composite actually necessarily comes. And this is only consistent if we make the sword, for example, a name for the composite of the substrate which is iron and the accident which is the configuration; then each is included in it, and its necessity with the first is potential, and with the second is actual.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of form and matter in artificial composites. It begins by stating that every configuration in a substrate is either essentially or by consideration considered form, and matter is its locus, like whiteness in body. Artifacts like swords and beds have only a configuration (form) added to pre-existing substances; the artisan does not create new substances. This avoids the objection that the sword-form does not actually necessitate the sword (as it can be in wood or stone). The author then presents the Imam's (al-Razi's) answer: the particular form (not the type) necessitates the particular composite. The passage then redefines form and matter homonymously: form is the configuration in a receptive unit, matter is its locus. In artificial composites, the form is an accident (like the bed-form in wood) and the matter is the substrate (e.g., iron for a sword). The Imam's objection about the formal cause is refuted by clarifying that the necessity is for the particular composite with the particular form. Finally, the author concludes that the sword is a composite of matter (iron) and form (configuration), and its existence is potential with the matter alone and actual with the form.

Key Terms

عارض
جوهر
صورة سريرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone may think that form in artifacts is a substance, like the soul in a body.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the carpenter's form (bed-form) is an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the form of a bed?
A) A substance indwelling the wood.
B) An accident subsisting in the wood.
C) The wood itself.
D) A separate entity.

Key Takeaway
In artificial composites, form is an accident and matter is its substance; the artisan only imposes a configuration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You impose configurations on the 'matter' of your time, energy, and relationships every day. Which of these configurations are you mistaking for something substantial and permanent? Will they survive when the matter is gone?
?
The artisan only creates an accident, not a new substance. What in your life are you laboring to produce that is merely a configuration—a shape, a reputation, a routine—while neglecting the substance of your soul? What would it take to shift your focus?
?
Al-Razi teaches that only the particular form necessitates the particular composite. You are not a generic 'type' of Muslim—you are a unique composite of actions and states. Are you living as a particular, irreplaceable servant before Allah, or are you content with a generic shape?

Refuting the Sword Objection: Particular Form vs. Type
Refutes the objection about the sword-form by distinguishing type from particular form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi answers the objection that the sword-form does not necessitate the sword, by distinguishing type from particular form.

Core Idea
The particular form, not the type, necessitates the particular composite.

A common objection against the formal cause in artifacts is that the sword-form does not always produce a sword—it can exist in wood or stone. In this passage, the author presents al-Razi's nuanced answer: it is the particular sword-form that necessitates the particular sword, not the type of form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that the sword-form (hay'a sayfiyya) is not such that the sword actually follows from it, citing examples of wood or stone.
2
Understand al-Razi's answer
Al-Razi clarifies that the necessity is for the particular form (e.g., the specific shape in iron) with the particular composite (that sword), not the type of form.
This resolves the objection because the form in the stone is only similar in type, not identical.
3
Examine the resolution
The passage explains that the sword is a composite of matter (iron) and form (configuration). Its existence is potential with matter alone and actual with the form.
4
Connect to the broader concept
This clarifies the formal cause: the form is the part that actually necessitates the composite's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستغناء. قال: وقد يقال لكل هيئة في قابل وجداني بالذات أو بالاعتبار والمادة لمحلها كالبياض والجسم ويشبه أن يكون مثل السيف والسرير من هذا القبيل، إذ الصانع لم يحدث فيه جواهر بل هيئة وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الهيئة السيفية ليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل كما في الحجر. وأما جواب الإمام بأنا لا نعني أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب بل إن الصورة الشخصية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادة الشخصية فيشعر بأن الصورة ههنا بالمعنى السابق على أن السيف مثلا اسم للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الجوهر والعارض الذي هو الهيئة. كل من الصورة والمادة يقال بالاشتراك بمعنى غير ما سبق، فالصورة الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر. والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.
Independence. He said: And it may be said that every configuration (hay'a) in a substrate is found either essentially or by consideration, and matter is its locus, like whiteness and body. And it seems that things like the sword and bed are of this type, since the artisan did not create substances in them but only a configuration. Then the objection does not arise that the sword-configuration is not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, as in stone. As for the answer of the Imam (al-Razi), that we do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter—this indicates that form here is in the previous sense, that the sword, for example, is a name for the composite of the substrate (jawhar) and the accident ('arad) which is the configuration. Both 'form' and 'matter' are used homonymously in a different sense from before. Form is the configuration occurring in something receptive that has unity essentially or by consideration, and matter is the locus of that configuration, like whiteness and body. In this respect, it is correct to attribute each to the other. It appears that the application of form and matter in artificial composites like the sword, bed, and house is in this sense, because the configuration that the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance indwelling them. Similarly for the form of the sword and house. On this basis, the objection of the Imam against the interpretation of the formal cause is repelled—that the sword-configuration is a form for the sword but not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, since it may exist in wood or stone without a sword. The Imam answered: We do not mean by the necessity of the composite with the form that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular wooden? sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter, which does not necessitate it; indeed, the same matter may be matter for something else. And the form occurring in stone is not identical to the form existing in iron but only in type. This indicates that the meaning of form in artificial composites is also the part with which the composite actually necessarily comes. And this is only consistent if we make the sword, for example, a name for the composite of the substrate which is iron and the accident which is the configuration; then each is included in it, and its necessity with the first is potential, and with the second is actual.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of form and matter in artificial composites. It begins by stating that every configuration in a substrate is either essentially or by consideration considered form, and matter is its locus, like whiteness in body. Artifacts like swords and beds have only a configuration (form) added to pre-existing substances; the artisan does not create new substances. This avoids the objection that the sword-form does not actually necessitate the sword (as it can be in wood or stone). The author then presents the Imam's (al-Razi's) answer: the particular form (not the type) necessitates the particular composite. The passage then redefines form and matter homonymously: form is the configuration in a receptive unit, matter is its locus. In artificial composites, the form is an accident (like the bed-form in wood) and the matter is the substrate (e.g., iron for a sword). The Imam's objection about the formal cause is refuted by clarifying that the necessity is for the particular composite with the particular form. Finally, the author concludes that the sword is a composite of matter (iron) and form (configuration), and its existence is potential with the matter alone and actual with the form.

Key Terms
هيئة سيفية
صورة شخصية
علة صورية
بالقوة
بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any instance of a sword-form (e.g., in wood) should produce a sword.
Correction: Only the particular form in the appropriate matter (iron) necessitates the sword; the form in wood is only similar in type.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer, what necessitates the actual sword?
A) The type of sword-form.
B) The particular sword-form in the matter.
C) The matter alone.
D) The configuration in stone.

Key Takeaway
The formal cause in artifacts is the particular form that actualizes the composite; the same type in different matter does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever assumed that because something looks like a good deed (the type) it is automatically accepted, neglecting your particular intention and circumstances? How does this lesson warn you against that mistake?
?
The same matter (e.g., your time, talents) can be shaped by different forms—some actualizing good and others not. What 'form' are you currently impressing on the matter of your daily actions? Is it actually producing the composite you desire or merely a resemblance?
?
If the particular form necessitates the composite, then your unique situation and choices are decisive. Are you relying on general principles without applying them to your specific 'matter'? What would change if you took this lesson seriously in one concrete area of your life today?

The Definitions of Form and Matter
Al-Taftazani defines form and matter with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental definitions of form (ṣūra) and matter (mādda) as used in Islamic philosophy and kalām.

Core Idea
Form is an accident; matter is its locus.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing the conceptual foundation for understanding form and matter. These terms are not only central to physics and metaphysics but also to the analysis of artificial objects. The passage defines them clearly before applying them to examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the terms 'hay'a' and 'mādda'.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines form (hay'a) as an accident occurring in a subject capable of unity. Matter is the locus of that form.
Example: whiteness (form) and body (matter).
3
Reflect on the 'by consideration' phrase
The phrase 'by essence or by consideration' indicates that unity can be either literal or conceptual. Consider how this applies to artificial composites.
This nuance is important for later lessons.
4
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to ensure you can distinguish form from matter based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر.
والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت. وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها. وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.
The form is the accident occurring in a subject capable of unity either by essence or by consideration, and the matter is the locus of that form, like whiteness and body; in this respect it is valid to attribute each to the other. And it is apparent that the application of form and matter to artificial composites such as the sword, the bed, and the house is in this sense, because the shape which the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is but an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance residing in them; likewise the form of the sword and the house. And on this basis the objection of al-Imam against the interpretation of the formal cause falls away, namely that the sword-shape is a form of the sword yet the sword does not necessarily exist with it actually, for it may be in wood or stone and there is no sword. And al-Imam answered that we do not mean by the necessity of the composite with the form that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the wooden sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its individual matter which does not necessitate it; indeed, it might be the very same matter of something else. And the form occurring in stone is not literally the form present in iron but is of its type. And this indicates that what is meant by form in artificial composites is also the part with which the composite necessarily exists actually, and it is only sound if we make the sword, for example, a name for the composite of the subject which is the iron and the accident which is the shape, so that each of them is included in it, and its existence with the first is potential, and with the second actual.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'form' (ṣūra) and 'matter' (mādda) as used in philosophical and kalām contexts, and then applies them to artificial composites. The author begins by stating that form is the accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs in a subject capable of unity, either essentially (bi-ḥasabi al-dhāt) or by consideration (bi-ḥasabi al-iʿtibār). Matter is the locus (maḥall) of that form, like whiteness in a body. Thus, form and matter can be attributed to each other in this sense. Then the author argues that in artificial composites like a sword, bed, or house, the terms are used similarly: the shape (hayʾa) produced by the carpenter is an accident subsisting in the wood, not a substance. This resolves the objection of al-Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) against the interpretation of the formal cause (al-ʿilla al-ṣūriyya). The objection was: the sword-shape is a form of the sword, yet the sword does not necessarily exist when that shape is present, since the shape could be in wood or stone without constituting a sword. The author reports Imam's answer: they do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that a specific form—e.g., the wooden sword-form—necessitates that particular sword, whereas the individual matter does not necessitate it; the same matter could be used for something else. Moreover, the form in stone is not identical to the form in iron but of its type. This indicates that 'form' in artificial composites means the part with which the composite actually exists, and this works only if we consider the sword as a composite of the subject (iron) and the accident (shape), so that the composite exists potentially with the matter alone and actually with the form.

A) The accident that inheres in a subject
B) The locus or subject of the form

Key Takeaway
Form is an accident; matter is the subject that receives it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you dislike about yourself—a habit or a flaw. According to this lesson, is it an accident (form) or part of your essential substance (matter)? How does this distinction change how you would approach changing it? Be honest: what action can you take today to address the accidental nature of that flaw?
?
Identify a relationship in your life that may be based on 'unity by consideration' rather than essential connection. How does realizing this affect your responsibility and commitment in that relationship? Will you continue to invest in it as if it were essential?

Form and Matter in Artificial Composites
Al-Taftazani applies form and matter to artificial composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms form and matter apply to man-made objects like a sword, bed, and house.

Core Idea
In artifacts, the form is the shape produced by the craftsman, which is an accident.

Al-Taftazani extends the definitions to artificial composites. He argues that the shape a carpenter produces in a bed is an accident, not a substance, and similarly for other artifacts. This clarifies that form in these contexts is not a substantial form but an accidental one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the carpenter
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the examples: sword, bed, house.
Notice the use of 'hay'a' for shape.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the shape produced by the carpenter is an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in the planks, not a substance (jawhar).
This aligns with the previous definition of form as an accident.
3
Connect to the initial definition
Consider how this application matches the earlier definition: form is an accident, matter is its locus.
The bed's matter is the wood, its form is the bed-shape.
4
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to confirm you recognize the form in artifacts as an accident.

Refuting the Objection on Formal Cause
Al-Taftazani reports and refutes an objection regarding formal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Imam's objection regarding the necessity of the composite with the form and al-Taftazani's solution.

Core Idea
The specific form necessitates the specific composite; matter does not.

Al-Taftazani addresses an objection from al-Imam (likely al-Razi). The objection is that if form is what makes the composite actual, then why doesn't a sword shape in wood produce a sword? The answer distinguishes between specific form and type of form, and explains that the composite exists potentially with matter and actually with form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage up to '... ولا سيف'. Understand the problem: the sword-shape alone does not guarantee a sword.
The objection is based on the example of a shape in wood or stone.
2
Analyze al-Imam's answer
Read the answer: they do not mean the type of form necessitates, but the specific form (e.g., wooden sword-form) necessitates that particular composite.
Notice the distinction between type and individual.
3
Understand the matter's role
The matter does not necessitate the composite because it can be used for other things. The form in stone is not identical to that in iron.
This reinforces that form is the actualizing principle.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that form is the part that makes the composite actual, and the composite exists potentially with matter and actually with form.
This requires defining the artifact as a composite of matter (subject) and form (accident).
5
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to confirm you understand the distinction between type and specific form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are you settling for mere 'potential'—good intentions, raw materials—without a decisive form that brings them to actuality? What specific form are you avoiding giving to your character, your worship, or your relationships, and why?
?
The passage shows that the specific form (e.g., wooden sword-form) necessitates a specific composite. What 'forms' are you repeatedly subjecting your heart and mind to—entertainment, thoughts, habits—and what composite are they producing in you? Are you content with that result?
?
Consider the 'matter' of your time and effort: is it being shaped by a clear, purposeful form, or are you letting circumstance determine its actuality? What would it mean to intentionally impose a form that aligns with your ultimate purpose, even if it requires cutting away what seems possible in other materials?

Accidents Specific to Living Beings
The author identifies accidents specific to living beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what accidents are specific to living beings according to the theologians, and why life and its concomitants form a distinct category.

Core Idea
Life, knowledge, power, will, speech, and perception are accidents unique to living beings.

This passage opens the classification of accidents. The author distinguishes between accidents that belong only to living beings and those that can occur in non-living things. By focusing on the first category, we see how the theologians define life and its inseparable attributes. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping later debates about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The Primary Modes (Akwan) and Sensible Qualities
The author enumerates the primary modes (akwan) and introduces sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
