The Mutakallimun's Proof Against Accident Transfer
Taftazani presents the mutakallimun's first proof with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that accidents cannot transfer between substrates due to their non-spatial nature, and the distinction between transfer of substance and transfer of accident.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot move through space because they have no spatial existence.

In this passage, Taftazani restates the agreed-upon position that accidents do not transfer from one substrate to another. He then presents the first proof specific to the mutakallimun, which rests on the non-spatial nature of accidents, along with a key refutation that distinguishes between spatial transfer and subject transfer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thesis and its justification
Read the opening where Taftazani notes agreement between mutakallimun and philosophers, and the reasoning: an accident's existence in itself is its existence in its substrate, so leaving the substrate annihilates it.
Notice that apparent transfers (e.g., heat near fire) are explained as creation, not transfer.
2
Analyze the mutakallimun's proof
Identify the logical steps: every accident is non-spatial (property of substance), and transfer is defined as spatial movement (al-haraka al-ayniyya). Therefore, non-spatial accidents cannot transfer.
The proof relies on the definition of transfer as spatial, not as subject change.
3
Examine the refutation and its implications
The refutation argues that the definition of transfer as spatial movement applies only to substances; for accidents, transfer means obtaining in a new subject. This distinction is crucial for the debate.
The passage ends here, but this sets up further discussion on accident transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثالث، اتفق المتكلمون والحكماء على امتناع العرض من محل إلى آخر لما سبق من أن معنى قيام العرض بالمحل هو أن وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في محله، فيكون زواله عن ذلك المحل زوالاً لوجوده في نفسه فما يوجد فيما يجاور النار من الحرارة أو المسك من الرائحة، أو نحو ذلك ليس بطريق الانتقال إليه، بل الحدوث فيه بإحداث الفاعل المختار عندنا، وبحصول الاستعداد للمحل ، ثم الإفاضة عليه من المبدأ عندهم ، وأقوى ماذكر في كلام القوم من الاحتجاج على هذا المطلوب وجوه : الأول وهو للمتكلمين . إن كل عرض غير متحيز بالذات ضرورة أنه خواص الجوهر، ولا شيء من غير المتحيز بالذات بمنتقل ضرورة أن الانتقال عبارة عن الحركة الأينية ، أي الحصول في حيز بعد الحصول في آخر بمعنى الحدوث، لا بمعنى الثبات فيه لأنه لا سيكون ، ورد بأن كون الانتقال عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في آخر إنما هو انتقال الجوهر، وأمـا انتقال العرض فعبارة عن الحصول في موضوع بعد الحصول في موضوع
He said: The Third Topic: The mutakallimun and the philosophers agreed upon the impossibility of the accident [transferring] from one substrate to another, due to what has preceded: that the meaning of the subsistence of the accident in the substrate is that its existence in itself is its existence in its substrate. So its departure from that substrate is a departure from its existence in itself. So what exists adjacent to fire of heat, or musk of scent, or the like, is not via transfer to it, but rather its occurrence therein by the creation of the free agent (according to us) or by the acquisition of disposition in the substrate then emanation upon it from the First Principle (according to them). And the strongest of what is mentioned in the speech of the group of argumentation for this sought claim are proofs: the first, which is for the mutakallimun. Indeed, every accident is non-spatial by essence, necessarily because it is a property of substance. And nothing non-spatial by essence transfers, necessarily because transfer is the spatial movement, i.e., obtaining in a space after obtaining in another, in the sense of occurrence, not in the sense of permanence, because it will not be. And it was refuted by [saying] that the fact that transfer is defined as obtaining in space after obtaining in another is only the transfer of substance; as for the transfer of the accident, it is defined as obtaining in a subject after obtaining in a subject.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a more detailed restatement of the thesis. It begins by noting agreement between mutakallimun and philosophers. The reason is reiterated: the meaning of an accident's subsistence in a substrate is that its existence in itself is its existence in its substrate. Therefore, leaving the substrate is equivalent to annihilation. Apparent transfers like heat near fire or the scent of musk are not transfers but creations by the agent (for us mutakallimun) or by disposition and emanation from the First Principle (for the philosophers). Then it presents the strongest proof from the mutakallimun: every accident is non-spatial by essence because it is a property of substance. Nothing non-spatial can transfer because transfer is defined as movement in space (al-haraka al-ayniyya), i.e., getting into a space after another, in the sense of occurring, not of remaining, because it will not be (or will not last?). The refutation: that definition of transfer applies only to substance; the transfer of an accident is defined as being in a subject after another subject. The passage ends there, apparently cut off.

Key Terms
متكلمون (mutakallimun)
حكماء (philosophers)
حركة أينية (spatial movement)
انتقال الجوهر (transfer of substance)
إحداث (creation)
إفاضة (emanation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that transfer of accident is the same as transfer of substance, both involving spatial movement.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that accident transfer is defined as obtaining in a new subject, not spatial movement; the two are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's first proof, why is an accident unable to transfer?
A) Because it is not spatial by essence.
B) Because it is too light.
C) Because it is dependent on the substrate.
D) Because it is created by God.

Key Takeaway
Accidents, being non-spatial, cannot undergo spatial transfer; their 'transfer' is actually creation in a new subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you experience a sudden change in your mood or thoughts, do you attribute it to external causes (people, environment) or to Allah’s direct creation? What would it mean for your reliance on Allah if you recognized that no 'accident' ever transfers into you?
?
The mutakallimun argued that accidents are not independent; they exist only in a substrate. How does this affect your understanding of your own existence? Are you living as though you are independent, or as a contingent accident subsisting on Allah every moment?

Impossibility of Accident Transfer: Consensus and First Proof
Taftazani states the consensus and gives the first proof of the mutakallimun with its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus on the impossibility of accident transfer and the first proof of the mutakallimun along with its refutation.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot transfer because their existence is tied to their substrate.

This passage begins with a remarkable consensus between the mutakallimun and philosophers on the impossibility of an accident moving from one substrate to another. Taftazani then presents the strongest proof for this claim from the mutakallimun, followed by a key refutation that redefines what 'transfer' means for accidents. This lesson unpacks that argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus and its rationale
Read the opening statement where Taftazani declares the agreement between the mutakallimun and philosophers. Note the reasoning: an accident's existence is identical to its existence in its substrate.
The examples of heat and scent are not transfers but new creations.
2
Examine the first proof from the mutakallimun
Focus on the argument: accidents are non-spatial, transfer is spatial motion (locomotion), so accidents cannot transfer. Identify the key terms: 'ghayr mutaḥayyiz', 'al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya'.
This proof relies on defining transfer strictly as spatial movement.
3
Analyze the refutation
Read how the proof is refuted: the definition of transfer for accidents is different—it is change of subject, not spatial motion. The refutation denies that accidents being non-spatial prevents such transfer.
The mutakallimun still affirm that accidents appear in new subjects by divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث، اتفق المتكلمون والحكماء على امتناع العرض من محل إلى آخر لما سبق من أن معنى قيام العرض بالمحل هو أن وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في محله، فيكون زواله عن ذلك المحل زوالاً لوجوده في نفسه فما يوجد فيما يجاور النار من الحرارة أو المسك من الرائحة، أو نحو ذلك ليس بطريق الانتقال إليه، بل الحدوث فيه بإحداث الفاعل المختار عندنا، وبحصول الاستعداد للمحل، ثم الإفاضة عليه من المبدأ عندهم، وأقوى ما ذكر في كلام القوم من الاحتجاج على هذا المطلوب وجوه: الأول وهو للمتكلمين. إن كل عرض غير متحيز بالذات ضرورة أنه خواص الجوهر، ولا شيء من غير المتحيز بالذات بمنتقل ضرورة أن الانتقال عبارة عن الحركة الأينية، أي الحصول في حيز بعد الحصول في آخر بمعنى الحدوث، لا بمعنى الثبات فيه لأنه لا يكون، ورد بأن كون الانتقال عبارة عن الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في آخر إنما هو انتقال الجوهر، وأما انتقال العرض فعبارة عن الحصول في موضوع بعد الحصول في موضوع آخر، ولا نسلم أنه من خواص المتحيز.
The third section: The mutakallimun and the philosophers agreed upon the impossibility of an accident (al-'arad) moving from one substrate (mahall) to another, because of what preceded: that the meaning of an accident subsisting in a substrate is that its existence in itself is its existence in its substrate, so its departure from that substrate is a departure of its existence in itself. Thus what exists near fire of heat, or musk of scent, or the like, is not by way of transfer to it, but rather its coming-to-be in it by the act of the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) according to us, and by the acquisition of preparedness in the substrate, then emanation upon it from the Principle according to them. The strongest of what is mentioned in the discourse of the people as a proof for this claim are aspects: The first, which is for the mutakallimun: Every accident is non-spatial (ghayr mutaḥayyiz) in essence necessarily because it is a property of substance, and nothing non-spatial in essence is transferable necessarily because transfer is locomotion (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), i.e., obtaining in a location after obtaining in another in the sense of coming-to-be, not in the sense of permanence because it would not exist. It was refuted that the transfer being obtaining in a location after obtaining in another is only the transfer of a substance; as for the transfer of an accident, it is obtaining in a subject (mawḍūʿ) after obtaining in another subject, and we do not grant that it is a property of what is spatial.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the consensus among the mutakallimun and philosophers (hukamāʾ) on the impossibility of an accident transferring from one substrate to another. The reason given is that the very existence of an accident is identical to its existence in its substrate; thus, leaving that substrate would annihilate it. Apparent examples like heat near fire or scent near musk are not cases of transfer but of new creation by God (according to us) or by emanation after preparedness (according to the philosophers). Taftazani then introduces the strongest proofs for this claim. The first proof, from the mutakallimun, argues that accidents are not spatially extended (mutaḥayyiz) in essence because they are properties of substance. Since transfer (intiqāl) is defined as locomotion (al-ḥaraka al-ayniyya), i.e., obtaining in one location after another, and this concept only applies to what is spatial, accidents cannot transfer. The refutation denies that the definition of transfer for accidents is the same as for substances; for accidents, transfer means obtaining in one subject after another, not spatial movement. Therefore, the argument that accidents are not spatial does not preclude their transfer by change of subject.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
متحيز
انتقال
الحركة الأينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny any change of accidents; they affirm creation of similar accidents in new substrates by divine power.
Correction: The mutakallimun do not deny that accidents can appear in new substrates; they attribute this to divine creation (ikhtirāʿ) rather than physical transfer of the same accident.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof of the mutakallimun, why can an accident not transfer?
A) Because transfer requires spatial motion, and accidents are not spatial.
B) Because accidents are created directly by God.
C) Because accidents have no existence in themselves.
D) Because transfer only applies to substances.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of accident transfer is argued via the nature of accident existence, but the definition of transfer differs for accidents and substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The refutation shows that even respected scholars can make a category mistake: misdefining transfer for accidents. Where in your own thinking might you be using a definition that doesn't fit the issue? Are you willing to have your assumptions refuted?
?
If every accident is directly sustained by God, then nothing in your life—health, wealth, relationships—is merely a chain of causes. What would it mean for you to live today as if every good thing is a direct gift from the Creator, not a result of natural processes?

Third Proof: Accident's Need for a Specific Substrate and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents and refutes the third proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for why an accident cannot transfer as an individual and its refutation based on generic determination.

Core Idea
Accidents don't transfer as individuals, but as likenesses through recreation.

Taftazani presents the third proof that an accident requires a determinate substrate, concluding transfer is impossible. However, he immediately provides the classic refutation: the substrate is determinate only in a generic sense, allowing God to recreate the accident elsewhere. This passage exemplifies the dialectical method of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a proof followed by a refutation.
The passage is short but dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof argues that the needed substrate must be either determinate or non-determinate. Non-determinate is impossible because every existent is determinate. If determinate, the accident cannot leave it. This is the desired conclusion that transfer is impossible.
The key is the dichotomy and the impossibility of the first option.
3
Understand the refutation's key point
The refutation says the accident is determinate only by a generic determination (like a body needs some space, not a specific one). Therefore, it can transfer by having a similar accident created in a new substrate.
This is the principle: 'the accident does not transfer as the same individual but as its like.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن العرض يحتاج إلى ضرورة فمحله المحتاج إليه إما أن يكون غير معين وهو ليس بموجود ضرورة أن كل موجود معين فيلزم أن يكون غير الموجود محلاً للموجود، وهو محال، وإما أن يكون معيناً فيمتنع مفارقته عنه، وهو المطلوب. ورد بأنه المعين بتعين ما سواء كان هذا أو ذاك، كالجسم يحتاج إلى حيز ما، كذلك ولا يمتنع انتقاله عنه وهذا هو المعنى بقولهم إن العرض لا ينتقل بعينه بل بمثله.
The third: The accident necessarily needs [a substrate], so its needed substrate is either non-determinate, but it does not exist necessarily because every existent is determinate, so a non-existent would be a substrate for an existent, which is impossible; or it is determinate, in which case it is impossible for it to separate from it, and that is the desired [conclusion]. It was refuted that it is determinate by a determination of some sort, whether this or that, just as a body needs some space; likewise, its transfer from it is not impossible, and this is the meaning of their saying: the accident does not transfer as the same individual but as its like.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof and its refutation. The proof argues that an accident necessarily requires a substrate. That substrate must be either non-determinate (which is impossible because every existent is determinate) or determinate. If determinate, the accident cannot leave it, thus transfer is impossible. The refutation responds that the accident is determinate only by a generic determination (e.g., it needs some substrate, not a specific one), just as a body needs some space but can move from one space to another. This is the basis of the principle that accidents do not transfer as individuals but as their likenesses (i.e., a new similar accident is created in the new substrate).

Key Terms
معين
غير معين
حيز
المطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that an accident needs a substrate altogether.
Correction: The refutation confirms the need for a substrate but argues it need not be a unique specific substrate; it can be any substrate of a given type, allowing God to create a similar accident elsewhere.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third proof, how can an accident 'transfer'?
A) By moving its substrate along with it.
B) By the creation of a similar accident in a new substrate.
C) By changing its quiddity.
D) By becoming a substance.

Key Takeaway
The third proof is refuted by showing that the accident's substrate is only generically determinate, allowing transfer through recreation of a similar accident, not the identical individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical argument that seems airtight, do you trust your intellect more than God's power? The proof here seemed valid until the refutation. Is there any belief you hold that you have not allowed God's omnipotence to challenge?
?
The refutation relies on the principle that God creates similar accidents anew. If God could recreate your faith each moment, would you rely more on His sustaining power than on a past spiritual state? Does your current devotion assume that past merit transfers automatically?
?
This lesson shows that accidents do not transfer as individuals but as likenesses. Is there an 'accident' of character or habit in your life that you have despaired of changing, thinking it is fixed? How does God's ability to recreate give you hope for genuine transformation?

The Second Proof: Accident's Individuation from Its Substratum
Taftazani presents the second proof that an accident's individuation is from its substratum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers concluded that an accident's individuation must come from its substratum, and how they eliminated other possibilities.

Core Idea
An accident's individuality cannot come from itself, its parts, or external factors—only from its substratum.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid presents the second proof from the philosophers regarding the cause of an accident's individuation. The author systematically eliminates four possible causes, leaving only the substratum. This argument is foundational for understanding how accidents are identified and how they relate to their bearers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: negation of possibilities then affirmation of the only remaining cause.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the four rejected causes
The passage rejects individuation coming from the essence, something inhering in the accident, something separate, or the accident's identity. For each, note the specific reason given.
Each rejection uses a distinct logical argument: necessity of confinement, circularity, arbitrary preference, and priority.
3
Understand the objections and responses
After concluding that the substratum is the cause, the passage considers two objections: one about something inhering in the substratum, and another about special relation of a separate cause. See how Taftazani replies.
The replies invoke circularity, infinite regress, and the obviousness of special relation for voluntary agents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this proof supports the Ash'ari position on accidents and their dependence on substrata. Ask yourself why this argument matters for kalam.
This connects to broader discussions of causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علته الثاني : وهو للحكماء. إن تشخص العرض لا يجوز أن يكون لماهيته وإلا لزم انحصار الماهية في شخص ضرورة امتناع تخلف المعلول عن الموجبة، ولا لما هو حال في العرض، وإلا لزم الدور لأن الحال في الشيء محتاج إليه متأخر عنه في الوجود، فلو كان علة لتشخصه لكان متقدماً عليه، ولا لأمر منفصل عنه لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء فإفادته هذا التشخص دون ذلك ترجح بلا مرجح ولا لهويته على ما أورده صاحب المواقف سنداً لمنع الحصر، لأن الهوية تطلق على التشخص وعلى الوجود الخارجي، وعلى الماهية من حيث كونها مشخصة وشيء من هذه المعاني ليس بمتقدم على التشخص ليكون علة له فيتعين أن يكون تشخص العرض لمحله .
فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون لأمر حال في محله قلنا ينقل الكلام إلى علة تشخص ذلك الأمر، ويرجع آخر الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وإذا كان تشخصه لمحله امتنع بقاؤه بالشخص عند انتقاله عن ذلك المحل ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن نسبة المنفصل إلى الكل على السواء لجواز أن يكون له نسبة خاصة إلى هذا التعين سيما إذا كان مختاراً وهو ظاهر.
Its second cause [i.e., proof]: and it is for the philosophers. Indeed, the individuation (tashakhkhus) of the accident cannot be from its essence (mahiyyah), otherwise the confinement of the essence to a single individual would follow necessarily, since the effect cannot be separated from the necessitating cause; nor from what inheres in the accident, otherwise there would be circularity because that which inheres in a thing depends on it and is posterior to it in existence, so if it were a cause for its individuation it would be prior to it; nor from something separate from it because its relation to all [individuals] is equal, so its conferring this individuation rather than that would be an arbitrary preference without a determinant; nor from its identity (huwiyyah) as al-Sahib al-Mawaqif adduced to preclude the enumeration, for identity is applied to individuation, to external existence, and to the essence insofar as it individuates, and none of these meanings is prior to individuation so as to be a cause for it. Thus it is determined that the individuation of the accident must be from its substratum (mahall). If it is said: it is possible that it be from something inhering in its substratum, we reply: the discussion is then transferred to the cause of the individuation of that thing, and ultimately the matter returns to the substratum, avoiding circularity and infinite regress. And if its individuation is by its substratum, its persistence as the same individual when it moves from that substratum is impossible. It is objected: we do not accept that the relation of the separate thing to all is equal, because it may have a special relation to this particular determination, especially if it is a voluntary agent, and that is evident.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from the philosophers regarding the cause of an accident's individuation (tashakhkhus). The author systematically eliminates four possible causes for the accident's individuation: (1) its own essence, (2) something inhering in the accident, (3) something separate from it, and (4) its identity as a factor. Each is refuted: if from essence, no two accidents of the same kind could exist; if from something inhering in it, circularity arises because that inhering thing would be both prior and posterior; if from something separate, it would require arbitrary preference without a determinant; and if from identity, identity itself is posterior to individuation. Thus the only remaining cause is the substratum (mahall). The passage then addresses an objection: why not something inhering in the substratum? The answer is that this would shift the problem to the cause of that thing's individuation, ultimately returning to the substratum. A further objection is that a separate cause could have special relation to a determination, but the author dismisses this as evident. The argument ends with the conclusion that the accident's individuation is from its substratum.

Key Terms
تشخص (tashakhkhus)
عرض ('ard)
ماهية (mahiyyah)
محل (mahall)
علة ('illa)
هوية (huwiyyah)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that individuation could come from something inhering in the accident, such as a quality.
Correction: The circularity objection shows that anything inhering in the accident depends on it and thus cannot be prior as a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why cannot the individuation of an accident be from its essence?
A) Because the essence is unknowable.
B) Because then the essence would be confined to a single individual, preventing multiple instances of the same kind.
C) Because the essence is separate from the accident.
D) Because the accident is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The individuation of an accident must come from its substratum, as all other potential causes lead to logical contradictions like circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your emotions, thoughts, and desires are accidents—they inhere in your soul but are not your essence. When you feel anger, do you identify with it or recognize it as a passing state that depends on a deeper substratum? Today, when a fleeting emotion arises, will you pause and ask: 'Is this my essence or an accident?' and detach accordingly?
?
The proof shows that an accident cannot persist without its substratum. Your deeds (accidents) vanish with time, but their record remains. Are you investing in deeds that will survive the shift to the next substratum—the afterlife? What fleeting action can you transform into a lasting investment for your akhirah today?
?
The philosophers argued that individuation comes from the substratum, not from the thing itself. Your identity in this world—your name, status, body—is tied to this earthly substratum. When you die, that identity ceases. What part of your identity is rooted in what never changes: your soul's relationship with Allah? Will you today strengthen that unchanging identity over the temporary one?

The Third Proof: Accident's Substratum and the 'Determinate but Not Specifically' Objection
Taftazani presents the third proof, objection, and clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that an accident cannot leave its substratum, the objection that the substratum is determinate but not specifically one, and how this objection refutes the proof.

Core Idea
An accident needs a place, but not a specific place—so it can move.

This passage presents a key debate in kalām: whether accidents can transfer between substrata. Taftazani offers a proof that they cannot, then considers an objection that uses the subtle distinction between 'determinate' and 'determinate but not specifically one'. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping later discussions on atomism and motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument structure.
Notice the dilemma: either non-determinate or determinate substratum.
2
Understand the proof as a dilemma
The proof argues that the substratum must be determinate, else it would be non-existent. If determinate, the accident cannot leave it.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the opposite.
3
Grasp the objection and its key phrase
The objection says the substratum is 'determinate with a determination' (mu'ayyan bi-ta'ayyun ma), meaning it must be some place but not a specific one.
The phrase 'mu'ayyan la bi-'aynihi' is crucial.
4
See how the objection resolves the dilemma
The clarification shows that 'determinate but not specifically' does not mean vague or non-existent; it is a general necessity, allowing transfer.
This refutes the proof's conclusion that separation is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن العرض يحتاج إلى ضرورة فمحله المحتاج إليه إما أن يكون غير معين وهو ليس بموجود ضرورة أن كل موجود معين فيلزم أن يكون غير الموجود محلاً للموجود، وهو محال، وإما أن يكون معيناً فيمتنع مفارقته عنه، وهو المطلوب، ورد بأنه المعين بتعين ما سواء كان هذا أو ذاك، كالجسم يحتاج إلى حيز ما، كذلك ولا يمتنع انتقاله عنه وهذا هو المعنى بقولهم إن المحتاج إليه محل معين لا بعينه ولا يرد عليه أن ما يكون لا بعينه كان مبهماً غير موجود والمرجح لقولهم أنه محل لغير معين بمعنى أنه لا يشترط التعين، وهو أعم من الذي يشترط اللاتعين فلا يلزم عدمه .
The third: that the accident necessarily needs [a substratum]; so its needed substratum is either non-determinate (ghayr mu'ayyan), but it is not existent, since necessarily every existent is determinate, so it would follow that the non-existent is a substratum for the existent, which is absurd; or it is determinate (mu'ayyan), and then its separation from it is impossible, which is the desired [conclusion]. It is objected: it is determinate with a determination (ta'ayyun ma) whether this or that, like the body needs a space (hayyiz ma), and similarly its transfer from it is not impossible. And this is the meaning of their saying: the needed thing is a determinate place but not specifically (mu'ayyan la bi-'aynihi). And it is not objected against it that what is 'not specifically' would be vague and non-existent. Rather, the preferred [view] is that it is a place for a non-specific [thing] in the sense that determination is not required, which is more general than that which requires non-determination (al-ta'ayyun), so its non-existence does not follow.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the claim that an accident cannot leave its substratum. The proof is a dilemma: an accident's needed substratum must be either non-determinate or determinate. If non-determinate, it cannot exist because every existing thing is determinate; thus the non-existent would be a substratum for the existent, which is absurd. If determinate, then the accident cannot leave it, which is the desired conclusion. The objection then states that the substratum is determinate with a determination (ta'ayyun ma) that is not specifically one place—like a body needing a space but not a particular space. The accident can therefore transfer from one determinate place to another. The author clarifies that the phrase 'determinate but not specifically' means that determination in general is required, but not a specific determination. This does not imply vagueness or non-existence, because it is a general necessity, not a specific one. Thus the third proof is refuted by showing that the accident's substratum can be a 'specified non-specific' place, allowing transfer.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
معين
لا بعينه
تعين
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'mu'ayyan la bi-'aynihi' means the substratum is completely indeterminate or non-existent.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means determination in general is required, but not a specific one; it is a general necessity, not implying vagueness or non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the key claim of the objection against the third proof?
A) Accidents need no substratum.
B) The substratum is determinate but not specifically one particular place.
C) The substratum must be non-determinate.
D) Separation is impossible because the substratum is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The third proof is refuted by showing that an accident's substratum is determinate in general not in particular, so the accident can move between substrata.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that every accident needs a determinate substratum. What 'accidents'—habits, desires, or thoughts—have you allowed to attach to your soul without examining whether they have a rightful place? Will you let this lesson push you to audit what you have allowed to settle in your heart?
?
The objection teaches that determination can be general without being specific. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes demand specific outcomes (a certain job, a particular spouse) rather than trusting His general decree? How can you practice saying 'mu'ayyan la bi-'aynihi' in your dua—knowing He will give you what is best, even if not exactly what you imagine?
?
The clarification insists that 'determinate but not specifically' does not mean non-existent. Are there areas of your faith where you have confused 'not yet specific' with 'non-existent'—like a promise from Allah that seems vague? What step can you take today to act on that promise with certainty?

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility of Accident Transfer
Taftazani presents the fourth proof, two objections, and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof of the impossibility of accident transfer, the objections using body motion and a third space, and the conclusion about indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
An accident cannot move between substrates without paradox or infinite regress.

The fourth proof tackles a subtle point: can an accident shift from one substratum to another? Taftazani presents a logical dilemma and then addresses objections, using the analogy of body motion and the concept of indivisible atoms. This passage sharpens our understanding of the nature of accidents and substrates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the argument's flow come through.
Notice the compact style: the proof is stated concisely.
2
Identify the main dilemma
The proof argues that during transfer, the accident is either in the old substratum (stability, not transfer) or the new (arrival, not transfer). Both are impossible. Also, a third substratum leads to infinite regress.
This is the core of the fourth proof.
3
Understand the first objection
The first objection (naqd) uses body transfer as a counterexample. If bodies can move through space, why can't accidents? The author's response: the same dilemma applies to bodies, so the answer is the same.
This objection tests the proof's universality.
4
Grasp the second objection
The second objection proposes that the accident is in a third space partly overlapping both old and new substrata. The author accepts this but shows it leads to infinite regress or ends at an indivisible atom.
This connects to atomism: only indivisible atoms block transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز انتقال العرض فهو حالة الانتقال، إما أن يكون في المحل المنتقل عنه، أو المنتقل إليه، وهو باطل لأن هذا استقرار وثبات قبل الانتقال أو بعده لا انتقال أو في محل آخر ضرورة امتناع كون العرض لا محل فينقل الكلام إلى انتقاله إلى هذا المحل ويعود المحذور، ورد أولا بالنقض بانتقال الجسم من حيز إلى حيز، فإنه حالة الانتقال إما أن يكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه، أو في المنتقل إليه، أو غيرهما، والكل باطل لما ذكرتم . فما هو جوابكم ؟ فهو جوابنا .
وثانياً : بأنا نختار أنه في حيز ثالث هو بعض من المنتقل عنه وبعض من المنتقل إليه، وهكذا حالة الانتقال إلى هذا المحل وإلى ما بينهما إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو ينتهي إلى جزء لا يتجزأ . غاية الأمر أنه يمتنع انتقال العرض الذي يكون في الجوهر الفرد .
The fourth: that if the transfer of the accident were possible, then during the state of transfer, it would either be in the substratum it left, or in the one it moves to, but that is invalid because this is stability and fixity before or after transfer, not transfer; or in another substratum, since it is impossible for an accident to have no substratum, so the discussion is then transferred to its transfer to this [other] substratum, and the same difficulty returns. It is objected, first, by parity with the transfer of a body from space to space: during transfer, it is either in the space it left, or in the one it moves to, or another, and all are invalid according to what you mentioned. So what is your answer? It is our answer. And second: we choose that it is in a third space which is part of the space left and part of the space moved to, and likewise the state of transfer to this substratum and to what is between them ad infinitum, or ending in an indivisible atom. The only exception is that transfer is impossible for the accident that is in the indivisible atom.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against the possibility of an accident transferring from one substratum to another. The argument is: if transfer occurs, during the moment of transition the accident must be either in the substratum it is leaving, or in the one it is entering. Both are impossible because being in the old substratum means it has not left, and being in the new means it has already arrived; there is no real transfer. The third option—being in another substratum—leads to an infinite regress of transfers. The objection (رد) first uses parity with the transfer of a body: the same dilemma applies to a body moving through space, yet bodies do transfer. The author responds that the same answer applies to both. The second objection is that the accident could be in a third space that is partly the old and partly the new, and this third space itself moves incrementally, either ad infinitum or ending in an indivisible atom. The passage concludes that transfer is impossible only for an accident in an indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard).

Key Terms
انتقال
عرض
محل
استقرار
ثبات
حيز
جوهر فرد
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth proof disproves all accident transfer.
Correction: The proof only concludes that transfer is impossible for accidents in indivisible atoms; accidents in continuous bodies may still transfer according to the second objection.

Checkpoint
What is the second response to the fourth proof's objection regarding where an accident is during transition?
A) The accident ceases to exist during transition.
B) The accident is in a third space that is part of both the old and new substrata.
C) The accident is in the mind only.
D) The accident transfers instantaneously without being in any space.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that accident transfer leads to logical paradoxes, but the discussion reveals that only accidents in indivisible atoms are truly incapable of transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to think about what cannot logically happen. Is there a belief you hold that, if examined with this kind of rigor, would collapse? Are you willing to submit your theology to such logical scrutiny, even if it means abandoning a cherished idea?
?
Your daily experience is full of changes—accidents moving in your body, emotions shifting. Does this proof challenge your assumption that such changes happen by themselves? How does the fact that accidents cannot transfer on their own point you to Allah's constant maintenance of creation?
?
The proof ends with an exception for indivisible atoms. This shows that even absolute statements have limits. Do you treat your own beliefs as open to such refinement, or do you cling to them rigidly? What would it cost you to admit you were mistaken on a point of theology?

Refuting Accident- Accident Subsistence
Refutation of arguments for accident-accident subsistence using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's systematic refutation of the claim that accidents can subsist in accidents, including their responses to specific examples and the clarification of unity and existence.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot subsist in accidents; alleged cases are non-existent, substances, relational, or mental.

This passage presents the theologians' response to a key objection: if a point can subsist in a line, and a line in a surface, then accidents can subsist in accidents. The mutakallimun systematically refute these examples and clarify that unity and existence are not accidents in the strict sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Note that this is a refutation of a false claim.
The passage is dense with multiple sub-arguments.
2
Identify the first two examples and their refutations
The objectors mention point/line/surface and smoothness/straightness. See how the theologians respond: either the terms refer to non-existent entities or they are substances, not accidents.
3
Understand the speed and slowness argument
The passage argues that speed and slowness are not additional accidents to motion. They are either intrinsic to the motion (due to interspersed rests) or relational considerations relative to another motion.
This is a key clarification in kalam physics.
4
Grasp the unity and existence discussion
The final part addresses unity and existence. The theologians explain that unity is a mental consideration (or even non-existent), and existence is identical to the essence in external reality, or mental, or intermediate. Thus, they are not accidents.
This connects to earlier discussions in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعرض وزعموا أن النقطة عرض قائم بالخط، والخط بالسطح، بمعنى أن ذا النقطة هو الخط، وذا الخط هو السطح، لا الجسم، ومن القائلين بجواز قيام العرض بالعرض من بالغ في ذلك وتمادى في الباطل، حتى زعم أن كلا من الوحدة والوجود عرض قائم بمحله. فتسمى فوحده العرض ووجوده يكون من قيام العرض بالعرض. وأجاب المتكلمون بأن مثل النقطة والخط عدمي، ولو سلم فمن الجواهر لا الأعراض على ما سيجيء، ومثل الملاسة والاستقامة على تقدير كونه وجودياً إنما يقوم بالجسم، وبأن السرعة والبطء ليس عرضاً زائداً على الحركة قائماً بها، بل الحركة أمر ممتد يتخلله سكنات أقل، أو أكثر باعتبارها سريعة أو بطيئة، ولو سلم أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فطبقات الحركات أنواع مختلفة والسرعة والبطء عائد إلى الذاتيات دون العرضيات أو هما من الاعتبارات اللاحقة للحركة بحسب الإضافة إلى حركة أخرى بقطع المسافة المعينة في زمان أقل أو أكثر، ولهذا يختلف باختلاف الإضافة فتكون السريعة بطيئة بالنسبة إلى الأسرع وبالجملة فليس هناك عرضي هو الحركة، وآخر هو السرعة أو البطء، وأما الوحدة والوجود فقد سبق أن الوحدة اعتبار عقلي بل عدمي وأن الوجود في الخارج نفس الماهية، أو هو من الاعتبارات العقلية أو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وفي الجملة فجعله من قبيل الأعراض خطأ فاحش لا ينبغي أن يقول به المحصل فإن من شأن العرض أن يفتقر في التقوم إلى المحل ويستغني عنه المحل.
By accident, they claimed that the point is an accident subsisting in the line, and the line in the surface, meaning that the possessor of the point is the line, and the possessor of the line is the surface, not the body. And among those who assert the permissibility of the subsistence of an accident in an accident are those who exaggerated in that and persisted in falsehood, until they claimed that each of unity and existence is an accident subsisting in its substrate. So they named it: the accident's unity and existence arise from the subsistence of an accident in an accident. The mutakallimun replied that such as the point and line are non-existent; and even if granted, they are from substances not accidents, as will come. And such as smoothness and straightness, if they are considered existent, they only subsist in the body. And that speed and slowness are not an accident additional to motion subsisting in it; rather, motion is an extended matter interspersed with fewer or more rests, being fast or slow by virtue of them. Even if one grants that slowness is not due to interspersed rests, the layers of motions are different types, and speed and slowness revert to the intrinsic rather than the accidental, or they are among the considerations attached to motion according to its relation to another motion by traversing the specified distance in less or more time. Hence, they differ by variation of relation, so the fast becomes slow relative to the faster. In sum, there is no accidental that is motion and another that is speed or slowness. As for unity and existence, it has preceded that unity is a mental consideration, nay non-existent, and that existence externally is the essence itself, or it is among mental considerations or an intermediary between existent and non-existent. In sum, making it a category of accidents is a gross error which the thorough scholar should not utter, for the nature of an accident is to depend on a substrate for its subsistence while the substrate is independent of it.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that a point is an accident subsisting in a line, a line in a surface, etc., as evidence that accidents can subsist in accidents. They exaggerate by asserting that unity and existence are accidents subsisting in their substrates. The mutakallimun answer: first, point and line are non-existent (عدميان); and even if granted, they are substances, not accidents. Second, smoothness and straightness, if existent, subsist directly in the body, not in another accident. Third, speed and slowness are not additional accidents to motion; motion is extended with interspersed rests making it fast or slow. Even if one denies rests, the various types of motion differ essentially, and speed/slowness are either intrinsic attributes or relational considerations to another motion. Fourth, unity is a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي) or non-existent, and existence is identical to essence externally or mental or an intermediary. Thus classifying them as accidents is a gross error; an accident by nature depends on a substrate while the substrate does not depend on it.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر
عدم
حركة
سرعة
بطء
وحدة
وجود
اعتبار عقلي
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The examples (point in line, speed in motion) prove that accidents can subsist in accidents.
Correction: The theologians show that these examples are either non-existent entities, substances, or relational attributes, not accidents inhering in accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun in this passage, how do they explain the relationship between speed and motion?
A) Speed is a separate accident subsisting in motion.
B) Speed and slowness are additional accidental attributes.
C) Speed and slowness are relations or intrinsic to motion, not separate accidents.
D) Speed is the motion itself without any distinction.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that all claimed instances of accidents subsisting in accidents are either non-existent, substances, relational, or mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the theologians considered many supposed attributes (like speed as a separate entity) to be mental constructs or relations. How much of what you think is 'real' about your own character or your relationship with Allah is actually a mental construct that needs to be re-examined? Will you commit to testing your assumptions against the Qur'an and Sunnah today?
?
The source warns that making existence an accident is a 'gross error.' Reflect on your understanding of Allah's attributes: are you ever tempted to think of them as separate 'accidents' added to His essence? How can you purify your belief to truly affirm that His attributes are neither identical to nor separate from His essence, as the Ahl al-Sunnah teach?
?
The mutakallimun took great care to refute each false claim about accidents. What is one comfortable belief or habit in your life that you have never critically examined? Will you take the time to study it with the same rigor, even if it challenges you?

Accidents Do Not Last: Two Classical Proofs
Two arguments against accident endurance with rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical arguments against the endurance of accidents: the definitional reasoning of the externalists and the two logical proofs of the verifiers, along with their rebuttals.

Core Idea
Accidents are momentary by nature; their endurance would lead to logical paradoxes.

This passage tackles a foundational doctrine in kalam: that accidents cannot persist for two consecutive moments. It presents two distinct lines of reasoning — one based on the very definition of what an accident is, and another that explores the logical consequences if accidents were to endure. As you read, pay attention to how the arguments are structured and how objections are anticipated.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation once without pausing. Get a sense of the overall flow: the classification of proponents and their arguments.
Notice the two groups: externalists (الظاهريون) and verifiers (المحققون).
2
Analyze the externalists’ argument
Identify why the externalists think accidents cannot endure by definition. Consider their two sub-arguments and why they are considered weak.
They argue that endurance is incompatible with the concept of an accident.
3
Examine the verifiers’ first proof
Understand the claim that an enduring accident would require an accident to subsist in another accident, which is deemed impossible. Note the rebuttal.
The rebuttal is that both premises can be denied.
4
Examine the verifiers’ second proof
This is a more complex argument: if an accident endured, it could never cease. Follow the five ways an enduring thing might cease and why each fails.
The five ways: by itself, by removal of condition, by a contrary, by an agent, or by annihilation of substrate.
5
Consider the objection and response
The analogy with bodies raises a challenge. See how the response distinguishes between accidents and bodies.
Bodies endure but they are removed by divine action, not by inherent change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس الأعراض لا تبقى زمانين قال: ذهب كثير من المتكلمين إلى امتناع بقاء العرض، فالظاهريون لأن استحالة البقاء معتبرة في مفهوم هذا الاسم كالعارض ونحوه ولأنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيدوم بدوامه ويتصف بسائر صفاته وإما ببقاء آخر فيمكن بقاؤه مع فناء المحل وضعفهما ظاهر والمحققون لوجهين: الأول: أنه لو كان باقياً يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، وهو محال ورد بمنع المقدمتين. الثاني: لو بقي لامتنع زواله إذ لو أمكن فإما بنفسه فيمتنع وجوده، أو بزوال شرط فيتسلسل أو بطريان ضد فيدور لأن اتصاف المحل بأحد الضدين مشروط بانتفاء الآخر على أن زوال الباقي بالطارىء ليس أولى، بل بالعكس لأن الدفع أهون من الرفع أو بفاعل فيقتضي أثراً والنفي المحض لا يصلح، ورد أولاً بالنقض بالجسم، وقد يدفع بأنه يزول بأن يخلق الله تعالى.
The fifth investigation: Accidents do not last for two moments. He said: Many of the mutakallimun have gone to the impossibility of the endurance of an accident. The externalists (al-zahiriyun) because the impossibility of endurance is considered in the concept of this name, like 'al-‘arid' and the like, and because if it lasted, then either by the endurance of its substrate, so it would continue by its continuation and be qualified by all its attributes, or by another endurance, so it could possibly continue with the annihilation of the substrate—and the weakness of both is apparent. The verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) state two proofs: First: that if it were enduring, the subsistence of an accident in an accident would necessarily follow, which is impossible, and it is rebutted by denying the two premises. Second: if it lasted, its removal would be impossible; for if removal were possible, then either by itself—so its existence would be impossible—or by removal of a condition—so that would lead to infinite regress—or by the occurrence of a contrary (did)—so that would lead to circularity, because the qualification of the substrate by one of the two contraries is conditional on the absence of the other, while the removal of the enduring by the occurring contrary is not more appropriate; rather, the opposite, because prevention is easier than removal—or by an agent, and that would require an effect, and pure negation is not suitable. It is first rebutted by the objection of bodies, and it may be repelled by saying that it is removed by God creating.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. It begins the fifth investigation (المبحث الخامس) on the impossibility of accidents lasting for two moments. It classifies the proponents: many mutakallimun hold this view. The externalists (الظاهريون) argue from the definition: 'accident' (عارض) implies non-endurance, and if it lasted, it would either last with its substrate (so it would be eternal as the substrate is eternal? Actually it says if it lasts by its substrate's endurance, then it would continue as long as the substrate continues, and be qualified by its attributes; or if by another endurance, it could outlast the substrate—both are weak. The verifiers (المحققون) give two proofs: First, if an accident endured, it would entail an accident subsisting in an accident (قيام العرض بالعرض), which is impossible. This proof is met by denying both premises (منع المقدمتين). Second, if it endured, its removal would be impossible. The argument then enumerates the possible ways an enduring accident could cease: by itself (impossible, as it would have to not exist while enduring), by removal of a condition (leads to infinite regress), by a contrary (circularity because contraries are conditioned on absence of each other, and removal by the occurring contrary is not more justified than the opposite), or by an agent (requires an effect, but pure negation is not a suitable effect). An objection is raised by analogy to bodies (which endure but are removed); the response is that removal of bodies occurs by God creating something causing their removal.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
زوال
قيام العرض بالعرض
ضد
فاعل
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of removal argument is a simple logical point that dismisses the endurance of accidents without nuance.
Correction: The argument is actually a detailed analysis of possible modes of removal (by itself, by condition, by contrary, by agent) and shows the problems inherent in each, but it also acknowledges objections and responses.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof of the verifiers (المحققون) against the endurance of accidents?
A) If accidents lasted, they would be eternal.
B) If accidents lasted, they would require an accident to subsist in an accident.
C) If accidents lasted, they could not be created.
D) If accidents lasted, they would be substances.

Key Takeaway
The kalam denial of endurance for accidents is defended by both definitional and logical arguments, but each argument invites nuanced objections and responses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your life—your health, wealth, and emotions—can end in a moment, and only God can cause its removal or continuation, how does that change the way you cling to these things? What specific attachment do you need to release today?
?
The argument shows that prevention is easier than removal (al-daf' ahwan min al-raf'). Where in your own spiritual life are you waiting for removal of a bad habit when you could have prevented it? What would prevention look like starting now?
?
The verifiers' arguments force you to confront the logical impossibility of anything enduring without God's constant creation. If you truly believed that every atom of your being is being recreated by God every moment, how would you differ in your prayer, gratitude, and fear of Him? Commit to one change tonight.

Accidents Do Not Persist: Position and Refutation
Taftazani states the position that accidents do not persist and refutes two arguments from ahl al-zahir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological position that accidents do not persist, the two arguments of ahl al-ẓāhir against accident persistence, and the refutation of these arguments based on linguistic and logical analysis.

Core Idea
Accidents are continually renewed by God; their linguistic meaning does not prove non-persistence.

This passage presents the Ash'ari position that accidents do not endure for two moments but are continuously recreated by God. It then introduces two arguments from those who take the outward meaning of language (ahl al-ẓāhir) to argue that accidents cannot persist. Finally, it refutes these arguments by distinguishing linguistic meaning from technical terminology and by clarifying the conditional nature of accident persistence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the position
Read the opening statement that accidents do not persist and what is meant by their 'permanence'—that it is the renewal of similar accidents by God's will.
Note the connection between accident persistence and the need for a sustaining cause.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two arguments presented by ahl al-ẓāhir: the linguistic derivation of 'accident' implying non-persistence, and the logical dilemma of persistence requiring either the persistence of the locus or another persistence.
3
Examine the refutation
See how the author counters each argument: first by distinguishing linguistic meaning from technical meaning, second by showing that persistence by another does not entail independence from the locus.
Focus on the conditional nature of accident existence and persistence.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader kalām framework of causality and divine action—accidents are entirely dependent on God's continuous creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: ذهب كثير من المتكلمين إلى أن شيئاً من الأعراض لا يبقى زمانين بل كلها على التقضي والتجدد كالحركة والزمان عند الفلاسفة، وبقاؤها عبارة عن تجدد الأمثال بإرادة الله تعالى . وبقاء الجوهر مشروط بالعرض، فمن هاهنا يحتاجان في بقائهما إلى المؤثر، مع أن علة الاحتياج هي الحدوث لا الإمكان. احتج أهل الظاهر منهم بوجهين . أن العرض اسم لما يمتنع بقاؤه، بدلالة مأخذ الاشتقاق). يقال عرض لفلان أمر أي معنى لا قرار له وهذا أمر عارض، وهذه الحالة ليست بأصيلة، بل عارضة، ولهذا يسمى السحاب عارضاً، وليس اسماً لما لا يقوم بذاته، بل يفتقر إلى محل يقوم به، إذ ليس في معناه اللغوي ما ينبيء عن هذا المعنى . الثاني، أنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيلزم أن يدوم بدوامه لأن الدوام هو البقاء، وأن يتصف بسائر صفاته من التحيز والتقوم بالذات، وغير ذلك لكونها من توابع البقاء. وإما ببقاء آخر فيلزم أن يمكن فناء المحل ضرورة أنه لا تعلق لبقائه ببقائه، وكلا الوجهين في غاية الضعف، لأن العروض في اللغة إنما ينبيء عن عدم الدوام لا عن عدم البقاء في زمانين وأكثر ، ولو سلم فلا يلزم في المعنى المصطلح عليه اعتبار هذا المعنى بالكلية، ولأن بقاءه ببقاء آخر لا يستلزم إمكان بقائه مع فناء المحل الجواز أن يكون بقاؤه مشروطاً ببقاء المحل كوجوده بوجوده.
The fifth investigation: Many theologians have held that no accident persists for two moments; rather, all are subject to passing away and renewal, like motion and time according to the philosophers, and their permanence consists in the renewal of their likes by the will of God Almighty. The permanence of a substance is conditional upon the accident, so from this they both need the effecting agent in their permanence, even though the cause of need is originatedness (al-ḥudūth), not possibility (al-imkān). The people of outward meaning (ahl al-ẓāhir) among them argued with two proofs. [First:] That 'accident' is a name for what is impossible to persist, by the indication of the derivation (al-ishtiqāq). It is said: 'an accident (ʿaraḍ) occurred to so-and-so,' meaning a meaning that has no stability, and this is a contingent matter, and this state is not original but contingent, and for this reason a cloud is called 'accidental' (ʿāriḍ), [but accident] is not a name for what does not subsist by itself, but rather is in need of a locus in which it subsists, since in its linguistic meaning there is nothing indicating this meaning. Second: If it were to persist, then either by the persistence of its locus, in which case it would be necessary that it endure with its endurance because endurance is persistence, and that it be qualified by its other attributes, such as occupying space and subsisting by itself, and others, because they are among the concomitants of persistence; or by another persistence, in which case it would be necessary that the annihilation of the locus be possible, because its persistence has no connection with its persistence. Both aspects are extremely weak, because 'accidentalness' (al-ʿurūḍ) in language indicates only lack of duration, not lack of persistence for two or more moments; and even if granted, this meaning is not necessarily considered in the technical sense at all; and because its persistence by another persistence does not entail the possibility of its persistence with the annihilation of the locus, for it is possible that its persistence be conditional upon the persistence of the locus, just as its existence is conditional upon its existence.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an investigative section (mabḥath) in which the author presents the theological position that accidents do not persist for two moments; rather, they are continually renewed, akin to how philosophers conceive of motion and time. Permanence of an accident is just the creation of similar accidents by God. This view implies that the persistence of a substance depends on accidents, so both need a sustaining cause, and the reason for need is originatedness (ḥudūth), not mere possibility (imkān). Then the author reports two arguments from ahl al-ẓāhir (those who take the outward meaning of terms) to prove that accidents cannot persist. 

First argument: The very name 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) in Arabic derivation implies something that does not last, as in the phrase 'an accident occurred' meaning a non-stable matter. They argue that an accident is inherently contingent and non-enduring. But the author refutes this by pointing out that the linguistic meaning of 'accidentalness' indicates lack of duration, not lack of persistence across multiple moments; and even if it did, the technical theological definition does not need to follow that linguistic sense entirely. 

Second argument: If an accident were to persist, it would require either the persistence of its locus (leading to it inheriting all of the locus's enduring attributes) or another persistence (which would make the annihilation of the locus possible). The author dismisses both as weak: the first does not follow necessarily, and the second ignores the possibility that the accident's persistence is conditional upon the persistence of its locus, just as its existence was conditional. Thus both arguments fail to prove the impossibility of persistence.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
بقاء (persistence)
حدوث (originatedness)
إمكان (possibility)
أهل الظاهر (ahl al-zahir)
اشتقاق (derivation)
عارض (contingent/accidental)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic meaning of 'accident' (عرض) directly determines its technical theological meaning, so accidents cannot persist because the word implies instability.
Correction: The linguistic meaning only indicates lack of duration, not lack of persistence across multiple moments. The technical definition is broader and not necessarily tied to the linguistic implication of non-persistence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what mistake do the two arguments of ahl al-ẓāhir make regarding the concept of 'accident'?
A) They confuse linguistic meaning with technical meaning and assume that persistence by another entails possibility of survival without the locus.
B) They correctly prove that accidents cannot persist for two moments.
C) They rely on the philosophers' view of time and motion.
D) They assume that the existence of accidents requires a sustaining cause.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are not self-persisting but are continuously renewed by God, and linguistic etymology does not override the technical theological definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every moment of your existence is a new gift from God, sustained by His will, or do you take your continued existence for granted? What would change if you genuinely believed that your very being is renewed each instant?

The arguments of ahl al-zahir seem reasonable but are refuted. Are there beliefs you hold that seem reasonable but contradict the deeper truths of tawhid? How can you guard against such errors?
?
If accidents do not persist, then nothing in creation is self-sustaining. How does this impact your trust in God versus trust in worldly means? Will you begin each day with a conscious reliance on Him alone?

The Qalb (Reversal) Refutation
The author uses reversal (qalb) to turn the opponent's argument back on itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the qalb (reversal) argument turns the opponent's own reasoning against him using the same disjunction of causes for annihilation.

Core Idea
Qalb defeats the opponent by using his own disjunction against him.

Sanusi now presents a powerful refutation technique called qalb (reversal). Instead of introducing new arguments, he takes the opponent's own disjunction of possible causes for an accident's annihilation and shows that, when applied consistently, it undermines the opponent's own position. This passage demonstrates the elegance and economy of the qalb method.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without parsing it deeply, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the rhythm and the structure of the reversal.
Notice that the passage begins with 'وأما القلب' (And as for the reversal).
2
Identify the disjunction used by the opponent
The passage lists four possible causes for annihilation: by itself, removal of condition, occurrence of a contrary, or existence of an efficient cause. Identify each one.
These are the same options the opponent had originally proposed.
3
Understand how the reversal works
Sanusi says that each of these options is invalid 'in the very same way mentioned' – meaning the opponent's own criticism applies to his own argument. Reflect on why this is a clever refutation.
The reversal does not require new premises; it turns the opponent's tools against him.
4
Apply the logic diagram
Study the flow diagram that maps the opponent's disjunction and the reversal. This will help visualize the logical structure.
Follow the arrows to see how the reversal leads back to the opponent's dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القلب فلأن العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل بعين ما ذكر.
And as for the reversal (qalb): Because if the accident does not endure, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by another [cause] from the removal of a condition, or the occurrence of a contrary, or the existence of an efficient cause. And all of that is invalid in the very same way mentioned.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation by reversal (qalb). The opponent had argued that the non-endurance of accidents leads to a particular disjunction of causes for their annihilation. The author turns this around: if the accident does not endure, then the same disjunction applies, and each option is invalid for the same reasons the opponent had used. Thus the opponent's own argument is reversed against him.

Key Terms
قلب (qalb)
عرض ('arad)
فناء (fana')
عدم ('adam)
زوال شرط (zawal shart)
طريان ضد (turyan didd)
وجود مؤثر (wujud mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qalb is just denying one of the premises of the opponent's argument.
Correction: The qalb actually uses the opponent's own disjunction and shows that his own reasoning leads to a contradiction when applied consistently, thus reversing the argument without introducing new premises.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the qalb (reversal) argument in this passage?
A) To prove that accidents do not endure.
B) To show that the opponent's disjunction leads to an infinite regress.
C) To turn the opponent's own disjunctive reasoning back against him.
D) To introduce a new set of causes for annihilation.

Key Takeaway
The qalb is a powerful dialectical tool that defeats an opponent by turning his own argument against him, demonstrating the internal consistency of one's own position without new evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you turned someone's argument back on them? Were you motivated by a desire for truth or by the need to win? How can you ensure that your use of logical tools like qalb serves justice and understanding rather than mere victory?
?
The qalb requires deep understanding of the opponent's position. Think of a person whose views you strongly disagree with. Have you ever taken the time to truly understand their reasoning, or do you dismiss it? What would change if you did?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold that, if you followed their logical implications consistently, would undermine themselves? Can you courageously examine your own position with the same scrutiny you apply to others?
