Refuting the Argument that Accidents Perish by Themselves
First refutation: accident perishing by itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the persistence of accidents and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
An accident's self-annihilation is conditional and does not preclude existence.

The author begins by setting up the possible ways an accident could cease to exist. The first possibility is that it perishes by itself. The opponent argues that this would make its existence impossible. The author responds with a nuanced distinction, using the example of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory framework
Read the first part where the author lists four ways an accident might cease. Understand that these are the options the opponent holds, and the author rejects them all.
2
Examine the first argument and refutation
The opponent claims that if an accident perishes by itself, it would be impossible for it to exist. The author refutes by distinguishing between absolute and conditional self-annihilation.
Notice the example of motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, but only under certain conditions.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author's key point: self-annihilation does not imply existence is impossible, because the accident's essence may require non-existence only after a period of persistence, not absolutely.
This prevents the opponent's objection from leading to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل. وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه.

الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.

والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.

والثالث: لا نسلم أنه لو زال بطريان الضد لزم الدور المحال أو الترجح بلا مرجح.

أما الأول: فلأنه إن أريد بتوقف طريان الضد على زوال الآخر واشتراطه أن تحققه يحتاج إلى تحقق الزوال، والزوال مقدم عليه ولو بالذات ليكون تقدم الطريان عليه بالعلية دورانا لازمو ممنوع، وإن أريد أنه لا يفارقه أن يتحقق بدونه فالاستحالة ممنوعة، وذلك كدخول كل جزء من أجزاء في حيز الآخر وخروج الآخر عنه فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير استحالة. نعم يكون للطريان سبق علته، وهو لا ينافي المعية الزمانية على أنه يجوز أن تكون العلة طريان الضد على المجاور، ويكون طريانه على المحل وزوال الباقي عنه معاً بحسب الذات، لا تقدم أحدهما على الآخر أصلاً.

وأما الثاني: فلجواز أن يكون الطارئ أقوى بحسب السبب فيرفع الباقي، ولا يندفع به، وإن تساويا في التضاد.

الرابع: لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أثراً للفاعل، كيف وهو حادث يفتقر إلى محدث، والفاعل مقدم يلزم أن يكون أثره العدم، ولو سلم فنختار أنه ليس بفاعل، بمعنى أنه لا يفعل العرض أي يترك فعله لا بمعنى أن يفعل عدمه.

قال: والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب. والحق يريد أن امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير فرق.
If the accident does not persist, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by something else: by removal of a condition, or by the advent of an opposite, or by an efficient cause. But all are false. As for the solution, it prevents some premises of the demonstration of the invalidity of the disjunctive parts, and that is from several aspects.

First: We do not grant that if its passing away were by itself, then its existence would be impossible. That would only follow if its essence necessitated non-existence absolutely, but if it necessitated it in some states, like the state after persistence, then not. Such is motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, except that the preponderance of a certain time for passing away requires a condition so that the effect does not lag behind the complete cause.

Second: We do not grant that if its passing away were by the removal of a condition, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, because it is possible that the existence of the accident is conditioned by the existence of accidents that are renewed in their places on a basis of exchange, such that a later accident becomes a substitute for an earlier one in conditionality until their alternation ends at an accident for which the agent does not create a substitute. Then the accident conditioned by this condition ceases due to the removal of the condition.

Third: We do not grant that if it ceased by the advent of an opposite, then impossible circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow.

As for the first [aspect]: If by the dependence of the advent of the opposite on the passing away of the other and its being conditioned that its actualization requires the actualization of the passing away, and the passing away is prior to it even in essence, so that the advent's priority over it by causality is a circularity whose necessary consequence is impossible, it is not granted. And if it is meant that it is inseparable that it occurs without it, then the impossibility is not granted. Such is the entering of each part into the space of the other and the exiting of the other from it, for neither occurs without the other, without impossibility. Indeed, the advent has the priority of its cause, which does not contradict temporal simultaneity, on the basis that it is possible that the cause is the advent of the opposite upon the adjacent [thing], and its advent upon the subject and the passing away of the remaining [accident] from it are simultaneous in essence, with no priority of one over the other at all.

As for the second [aspect]: Because it is possible that the adventitious [opposite] is stronger in terms of the cause, so it removes the remaining [accident] and it is not repelled by it, even if they are equal in opposition.

Fourth: We do not grant that non-existence is not suitable as an effect of the agent. How so? For it is an event and requires an originator, and the agent is a cause; it would necessarily follow that its effect is non-existence. And if it is granted, then we choose that it is not an agent, meaning that it does not produce the accident, i.e., it leaves off producing it, not that it produces its non-existence.

He said: The truth is that persistence of the accident in general is like persistence of the body, especially the accidents that subsist in the soul. And reliance is not on mere observation, for successive examples may be observed as a continuous thing like water poured from a spout. The truth intends that the impossibility of persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a school of the Ash'aris and many of their claims are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul like sciences, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies with no difference.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of four arguments that accidents cannot persist (baqa') and an affirmation of the truth that some accidents do persist. The author first presents the possible ways an accident might cease to exist (by itself, by removal of a condition, by an opposite, or by an efficient cause), then denies all of them.

First Objection: If the accident perished by itself, its existence would be impossible. The author replies: Not necessarily; it is like motion, which necessitates non-existence after existence but requires a condition for the preponderance of the time of cessation.

Second Objection: If it perished by removal of a condition, circularity or infinite regress would follow. The author replies: No, because the condition could be a series of alternating accidents that ends at one for which the agent creates no substitute.

Third Objection: If it perished by an opposite, circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow. The author gives two answers: (1) The dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior passing away is not a vicious circle—it is like two things that enter each other's space simultaneously. (2) The adventitious opposite might be stronger and thus remove the other, without equality requiring a preponderant.

Fourth Objection: Non-existence cannot be the effect of an agent. The author replies: Non-existence is an event and needs an originator; if the agent causes it, that is fine. If not, the agent simply ceases to produce the accident.

Conclusion (from 'He said'): The truth is that persistence of accidents in general is like persistence of bodies, especially those subsisting in the soul (mental states). One can know the persistence of some accidents (colors, shapes, knowledge, perceptions) just as one knows the persistence of bodies. The Ash'ari position that all accidents are non-persistent is not absolute truth; some are known to persist.

Key Terms
بقاء العرض
فناء العرض
زوال بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an accident perishes by itself, it cannot exist at all.
Correction: The author shows that self-annihilation can be conditional and does not preclude existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the author use as an example to illustrate conditional self-annihilation?
A) Fire
B) Motion
C) Sound
D) Light

Key Takeaway
An accident can perish by itself without making its existence impossible, as long as the self-annihilation is conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold that you treat as absolutely necessary, when in reality they may only be conditionally true? How would your perspective shift if you examined the conditions under which those assumptions hold?
?
Consider a time you dismissed a possibility as impossible without considering conditional scenarios. How might the distinction between absolute and conditional necessity help you approach such situations with more intellectual humility?
?
The author uses the example of motion to illustrate that something can necessitate non-existence after existence yet still exist. In your own spiritual journey, are there states or practices that you avoid because you fear they will 'annihilate' something good, when in reality they might only lead to transformation under certain conditions? What would it cost you to reexamine that fear?

5 min
Refuting the Argument of Removal of a Condition
Second refutation: accident perishing by removal of condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the persistence of accidents (removal of a condition) and how the author refutes it by allowing alternating conditions.

Core Idea
Removal of a condition can cause cessation without circularity if conditions alternate.

The opponent argues that if an accident ceases due to the removal of a condition, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. The author responds by proposing a scenario where conditions are renewed in an alternating series, ending with an accident for which no substitute is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
The opponent claims that cessation by removal of a condition leads to circularity or infinite regress. Read the argument carefully.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author posits that the existence of the accident may be conditioned by a series of alternating accidents. Each successive accident replaces the previous one as a condition, until the series ends with a condition that has no replacement.
This avoids circularity because the chain is linear and finite.
3
Understand the implication
When the final condition lacks a substitute, the original accident ceases because its condition is removed. This is a valid scenario that defeats the opponent's objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل. وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه.

الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.

والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.

والثالث: لا نسلم أنه لو زال بطريان الضد لزم الدور المحال أو الترجح بلا مرجح.

Refuting the Argument of the Advent of an Opposite (1): Circularity and Preponderance
Third refutation: accident perishing by advent of opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument (advent of an opposite) and the author's two-fold refutation regarding circularity and preponderance.

Core Idea
The advent of an opposite does not necessitate circularity or preponderance without a preponderant.

The opponent argues that if an accident ceases due to the advent of an opposite, it leads to either a vicious circle or preponderance without a preponderant. The author provides two detailed responses, drawing on examples like spatial interpenetration and relative strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument and first sub-refutation
The first part addresses the claim of circularity. The author argues that the dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior cessation can be simultaneous in essence, not involving a vicious circle.
The example of two objects entering each other's space is given.
2
Read the second sub-refutation
The second part addresses the claim of preponderance without a preponderant. The author suggests that the adventitious opposite may be stronger and thus overcome the existing accident, which is not preponderance without cause.
3
Synthesize the responses
Combine both responses to see how the author defends the possibility of cessation by an opposite without logical flaws.

Source Text

But all are false. As for the solution, it prevents some premises of the demonstration of the invalidity of the disjunctive parts, and that is from several aspects.

Third: We do not grant that if it ceased by the advent of an opposite, then impossible circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow.

As for the first [aspect]: If by the dependence of the advent of the opposite on the passing away of the other and its being conditioned that its actualization requires the actualization of the passing away, and the passing away is prior to it even in essence, so that the advent's priority over it by causality is a circularity whose necessary consequence is impossible, it is not granted. And if it is meant that it is inseparable that it occurs without it, then the impossibility is not granted. Such is the entering of each part into the space of the other and the exiting of the other from it, for neither occurs without the other, without impossibility. Indeed, the advent has the priority of its cause, which does not contradict temporal simultaneity, on the basis that it is possible that the cause is the advent of the opposite upon the adjacent [thing], and its advent upon the subject and the passing away of the remaining [accident] from it are simultaneous in essence, with no priority of one over the other at all.

As for the second [aspect]: Because it is possible that the adventitious [opposite] is stronger in terms of the cause, so it removes the remaining [accident] and it is not repelled by it, even if they are equal in opposition.

Fourth: We do not grant that non-existence is not suitable as an effect of the agent. How so? For it is an event and requires an originator, and the agent is a cause; it would necessarily follow that its effect is non-existence. And if it is granted, then we choose that it is not an agent, meaning that it does not produce the accident, i.e., it leaves off producing it, not that it produces its non-existence.

He said: The truth is that persistence of the accident in general is like persistence of the body, especially the accidents that subsist in the soul. And reliance is not on mere observation, for successive examples may be observed as a continuous thing like water poured from a spout. The truth intends that the impossibility of persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a school of the Ash'aris and many of their claims are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul like sciences, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies with no difference.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of four arguments that accidents cannot persist (baqa') and an affirmation of the truth that some accidents do persist. The author first presents the possible ways an accident might cease to exist (by itself, by removal of a condition, by an opposite, or by an efficient cause), then denies all of them.

First Objection: If the accident perished by itself, its existence would be impossible. The author replies: Not necessarily; it is like motion, which necessitates non-existence after existence but requires a condition for the preponderance of the time of cessation.

Second Objection: If it perished by removal of a condition, circularity or infinite regress would follow. The author replies: No, because the condition could be a series of alternating accidents that ends at one for which the agent creates no substitute.

Third Objection: If it perished by an opposite, circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow. The author gives two answers: (1) The dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior passing away is not a vicious circle—it is like two things that enter each other's space simultaneously. (2) The adventitious opposite might be stronger and thus remove the other, without equality requiring a preponderant.

Fourth Objection: Non-existence cannot be the effect of an agent. The author replies: Non-existence is an event and needs an originator; if the agent causes it, that is fine. If not, the agent simply ceases to produce the accident.

Conclusion (from 'He said'): The truth is that persistence of accidents in general is like persistence of bodies, especially those subsisting in the soul (mental states). One can know the persistence of some accidents (colors, shapes, knowledge, perceptions) just as one knows the persistence of bodies. The Ash'ari position that all accidents are non-persistent is not absolute truth; some are known to persist.

Key Terms
طريان ضد
دور محال
ترجح بلا مرجح
قوة السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two opposites are equal, neither can overcome the other without a preponderant, so cessation cannot occur.
Correction: The author states that the adventitious opposite may be stronger in cause, not necessarily in the accident itself, so it can overcome.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what allows the adventitious opposite to remove the remaining accident without a preponderant?
A) Equality of opposites
B) The adventitious opposite is stronger in cause
C) Temporal priority
D) Circular reasoning

Key Takeaway
The advent of an opposite can cause an accident to cease without circularity or requiring an external preponderant, because the advent and cessation can be simultaneous and the opposite may be stronger in cause.
